HEADLINE: Needed: A Recycling Compromise

 BODY:
The Giuliani administration says it wants to recycle more of New York City's garbage, but cannot afford the cost. The City Council leader and litigants in a lawsuit say sorry, the city pledged to recycle more energetically than it does now and is legally bound to keep its promise. The courts agree and have ordered City Hall to afford what it says it cannot.
That sounds like an impasse, but there is a sensible way out. The City Council approved the recycling plan and it has the legal authority to change the deadlines and rate of compliance. Slower compliance, less cost. The administration will have to make a good case to the Council for amending the law; if its arguments are credible, and they appear to be, the Council will have a clear obligation to revise its law. 
 Unfortunately, Speaker Peter Vallone says he will demand that the city comply with the court order, and is sounding unusually rigid. New Yorkers will have to hope that Mr. Vallone is flexing his political muscle in anticipation of spring budget negotiations with the Mayor's office, and will ultimately show the responsible leadership he's demonstrated many times before.
Absent a compromise extending the deadlines, the city will have to sacrifice other services to underwrite expanded recycling. Recycling is important, but so are programs already suffering from the budgetary knife.
The litigants in the lawsuit, including the National Resources Defense Council, are frustrated because the city has failed to comply with its original 1989 commitment to recycle about 4,250 tons of materials a day by this month -- about 25 percent of its residential garbage. The city now recycles 2,526 tons a day, about 15 percent, at a cost of $111 million a year -- one-sixth of the Sanitation Department's budget.
Today's tonnage is three times what it was in 1992; after a weak start, the city has made important strides. The question is how much further it can go, and at what pace.
Judge Irma Santaella of State Supreme Court has given the city until July 1996 to comply with the law. In a more perfect world it would, and it is clear New York can improve on the 15 percent figure and make recycling a stronger element in the city's garbage disposal system. That system has to include recycling, incineration and reduction in the amount and kinds of refuse that New Yorkers throw out in the first place.
It is equally clear that the city cannot meet the judge's 25 percent target in so short a period without causing great budgetary pain. The Sanitation Department estimates that would cost as much as $139 million more a year -- more than double the present annual cost of recycling. Ambitious plans do not always survive when the economy fails to cooperate. It is the job of wise city officials to be flexible enough to change in response to reality, however unwelcome that reality is.

HEADLINE: New Tenants Around Times Square;
Vacancies Drop as Businesses Find Space to Fit Their Needs

BYLINE: By SHAWN G. KENNEDY 

 BODY:
When Proskauer Rose Goetz & Mendelsohn packed up its Park Avenue headquarters for a move to Times Square nearly four years ago, some of the law firm's employees faced the move with trepidation befitting a covered wagon trip across the Great Plains.
"We thought of Times Square as the wild, wild west, all right," said Robert J. Kafin, chief operating partner in the firm. "We were something of pioneers back then, and there was some resistance at the time." 
 But while still colorful -- and intimidating -- with the ever-present street hustlers, sex shops and pulsating neon signs, Times Square is becoming much less of a frontier as other blue-chip companies and firms move into the neighborhood.
In recent years companies like Bantam Doubleday Dell Publishing Group, RCA Records, the accounting firm Ernst & Young, the law firm Cravath, Swain Moore, and Viacom International have set up shop in the neighborhood.
In a year or so Morgan Stanley, the financial services company, will move into a building it bought on the west side of Broadway between 47th and 48th Streets. Some have bought or leased space in bankrupt office towers that were completed just as the city's economy plunged into recession.
The magnet for most new companies has been bargains on space: rents are at least $10 a square foot less expensive than the $40 per square foot for buildings on the East Side. Many companies have also found that by moving to the Times Square area, they can house all their employees in one location. And most compelling of all, the office buildings built near Times Square over the last decade offer telecommunications capabilities they could only dream of in their old locations; many companies have relocated from buildings built in the 1950's or 1960's where the possibilities for installing advanced computer and telephone systems were limited.
"The financial services industry today has technological requirements that demand certain kinds of space," said William M. Lewis Jr., managing director of Morgan Stanley, whose company will be moving from two locations on the Avenue of the Americas. "One requirement is for large, column-free floors. If you need that, there just are not that many buildings that satisfy the requirement."
 
10,000 New Office Workers
 The company expects to occupy about 900,000 square feet in the building, and it is close to a deal to buy another nearly empty Times Square building, at 750 Seventh Avenue between 49th and 50th Streets.
With Morgan Stanley's move, the neighborhood will gain nearly 10,000 new office workers.
Although retailing in the area is still dominated by souvenir shops, electronics equipment stores and other businesses geared to tourists, in recent years dozens of new table-cloth restaurants and stylish coffee bars have opened in the neighborhood, smoothing some of the district's honky-tonk edges.
Some newcomers say they have found the neighborhood cleaner and safer than they expected. Because the Times Square area straddles two precincts, the Police Department does not keep crime statistics for the neighborhood as a whole. But the Times Square Business Improvement District, a coalition of companies and merchants that provides security and street cleaning services, says its analysis of statistics provided by the Police Department shows a 19 percent decline in street crime. Purse-snatching and pickpocketing dropped by 38 percent, the Business Improvement District's analysis showed.
 
Reviving Times Square
 A district rich with the razzle-dazzle of its theatrical heritage, it is hard to imagine a more unlikely neighborhood for these sleek new office towers than Times Square. But their development was no accident. As part of an effort to revive the area, a joint city and state redevelopment plan for 42d Street was announced in 1981. That plan -- which involves the properties at the intersection of 42d Street and Broadway and Seventh Avenue -- is still on the drawing board.
A companion planning initiative, however, did produce results. In the mid-1980's, in an effort to shift commercial development in midtown from the congested East Side to the West Side, the city offered a temporary incentive that allowed developers to build bigger buildings in the Times Square area.
Many of the developers who rushed to exploit the incentive built as real-estate values and development costs peaked. Some paid top dollar for their sites, construction costs and architectural designs. By the time some new buildings were finished, the faltering economy had substantially weakened the demand for office space. Over all, rents for prime space on the west side of midtown fell from $37 a square foot in 1990 to $31 last year.
 
Bankruptcy Prices
 Three of the most prominent projects in the Times Square area, the office buildings at 1540 and 1585 Broadway and 750 Seventh Avenue, stood empty or nearly so for months while bankers and builders sorted out their finances. The uncertain status of these buildings further hampered efforts to sign leases on the space.
Some companies that were looking for new office space in Manhattan took advantage of bankruptcy prices for these buildings. Bertelsmann A.G., the German media and entertainment company that owns RCA and Arista Records and the Bantam Doubleday Dell publishing companies, for example, paid $119 million for the building at 1540 Broadway between 45th and 46th Streets -- a property on which the builders, industry observers say, lost about $200 million.
Once Bertelsmann bought the 46-story tower, which has about one million square feet of space, it made no sense to continue paying rent at 666 Fifth Avenue, where its Bantam Doubleday Dell publishing divisions were situated. Bertelsmann is still looking for subtenants for part of the space it vacated in the Fifth Avenue building.
 
A Gradual Move by Viacom
 Rather than take space in one gulp as Bertelsmann and Morgan Stanley have done, Viacom has expanded its presence in Times Square in small bites. It signed its first lease in 1990 in the building at 1515 Broadway, a 12-year-old office tower refurbished a few years ago to compete with new buildings. Today Viacom, whose cable television channels include Nickelodeon and MTV, has 25 floors in the building at 45th Street.
"Some of the companies that have gone to Times Square have been able to take advantage of disasters," said Jamie V. Covello, a vice president at Cushman & Wakefield, a leading real-estate brokerage. "Today's market is much stronger. Now tenants going into that area are dealing with higher rents."
Even for those who thrive on the high-voltage atmosphere of Times Square, the move to the district has been an adjustment.
When the steel drum bands in Duffy Square tune up for the Wednesday matinee crowds in line for discounted theater tickets, Joe Galante, president of RCA Records, feels as if his desk is on the square rather than in an office 36 floors above it.
 
'Always Something Going On'
 "I love it here," said Mr. Galante, speaking from his office with the color-splashed neon supersigns just outside his window. "But there is always something going on in Times Square; parades, demonstrations, promotions. It's great, but sometimes I wish that I could turn it off."
Marsha Pront, director of library services for Proskauer Rose, said many of the people in the firm who were at first reluctant to leave Park Avenue have discovered unexpected advantages in Times Square's nearly nonstop bustle.
"I know some of us miss certain things about being on Park Avenue," Mrs. Pront said. "People who take trains from Grand Central have a longer commute. I miss being close enough to my beauty shop to get my hair cut on my lunch hour. But nobody misses our old buildings."
Mrs. Pront said Park Avenue was quiet, but its wide sidewalks were nearly deserted after business hours.
"People here work late," Mrs. Pront said, referring to her fellow employees. "There are people on the street until late at night, and you can almost always get a cab as soon as you leave the building."
Despite the changes, the area still has its troubled corridors; most noticeable is the western section of 42d Street near Eighth Avenue. According to the Business Improvement District, a steady decline in the number of pornography shops there was reversed with the opening of four new sex-oriented businesses in the last 18 months.
But Rebecca Robertson, president of the 42d Street Development Project, said that improvements should be apparent soon.
"We have the money from the city to finish the condemnation process," she said. "Our next targets are the theaters and other properties on the western half of 42d Street."
 
THE PLAYERS
Filling in Times Square
 
Buildings in the Times Square district where office companies and law firms have recentll taken place.
 
1675 Broadway -- built by Rudin Management -- completed in 1989; major tenants Lord Day & Lord Barrett Smith, a law firm, D'Arch Masius Benton & Bowles, an advertising agency and Time Warner for Entertainment Weekly magazine.
 
1585 Broadway - built by Solomon Equities; completed in 1990; bought by Morgan Stanley in 1993 for own use. Only tenant Proskauer, Rose, Goetz & Mendelsohn, a law firm in the building since 1990
 
750 Seventh Avenue - built by Solomon Partners -- completed in 1990; Morgan Stanley in negotiations to buy for own use through bankruptcy. Current tenants: Mendes & Mount, a law firm, and Ernst & Young, an accounting firm.
 
1540 Broadway - built by Ian Bruce Eichner, completed in 1990. Bought by Bertelsmann A.G. in 1992 for own companies, including Bantam Doubleday Dell Publishing Group and RCA Records.
 
1515 Broadway built by the Minskoff Corporation; completed in 1972. Major tenants are Viacom Inc., whose companies include M-TV, Showtime and Nickelodeon cable channels and Paramount, and Frederick Atkins Inc., a marketing company.
 
One Worldwide Plaza - on block bounded by Eighth and Ninth Avenues and 49th and 50th Streets. Built by the Zeckendorf Organization, completed in 1988. Major tenants, Cravath Swaine & Moore, a law firm and Ogilvy & Mather, an advertising agency.

HEADLINE: PRO BASKETBALL;
With Regular Season Done, Knicks Think Championship

BYLINE: By CLIFTON BROWN, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: CHICAGO, April 24

 BODY:
In their final tune-up before the playoffs, the Knicks had an impressive road test.
 New York closed the regular season by handling the Chicago Bulls, 92-76, this afternoon at Chicago Stadium. The Knicks finished the regular season with a 57-25 record, but the most important season comes later this week, when the National Basketball Association playoffs begin. And the Knicks' first-round opponent will be the Nets. 
Anything less than a championship will be considered a disappointment by the Knicks. So while today's game had no bearing on the playoffs, the Knicks came ready to play. Patrick Ewing, the Knicks' co-captain and emotional leader, made a pre-game address to the team in the locker room. As far as Ewing was concerned, the Knicks' playoff preparation began today. And they played like it, beating the team that has won the last three N.B.A. titles and looms as a possible second-round opponent for New York.
 The Knicks and Nets will square off in a three-of-five-game series that will begin Friday at 7 P.M. at Madison Square Garden. It will be a major test for the Knicks, who were beaten four out of five times by the Nets during the regular season. But the Knicks feel they can beat anyone. And soon, they will get a chance to prove it.
 "I don't care who we play," said Ewing, who led the Knicks with 25 points. "It's a new season. We'll do all our talking on the court.
 "It's time to get in a playoff state of mind, starting today, and take it for two months. It's money time now. The regular season is all a warmup, a warmup for this. I'm very confident. I think this is definitely our chance. We had a chance last year and let it slip away. But I think we have a very good chance this year."
 The Knicks have some major decisions to make, because all playoff rosters must be submitted to the league on Monday.
 The first decision involves Anthony Mason, New York's versatile sixth-man forward, who has been serving an indefinite suspension for "conduct detrimental to the team" since Tuesday. An announcement regarding Mason's status will be made Monday, and while he is expected to be reinstated, neither Pat Riley nor Dave Checketts, the Knicks' president, have guaranteed that Mason will be back for the playoff opener.
 
Knicks Need Mason
 However, Mason has at least two things in his favor. The Knicks need him. And his teammates want him.
 "We're going to need everybody -- Mase, everybody," said Ewing.
 "We need all the people we can get," said Derek Harper, the Knick point guard. "Mase does a great job against small forwards. He can play the game."
 The Knicks will have John Starks back for the playoffs. Starks will return to practice Monday, but how effective he will be remains to be seen. Starks underwent arthroscopic knee surgery to repair torn cartilage on March 14, forcing Starks to miss the final 23 games of the season.
 
Starks Is Rushing Back
 Starks admitted that he was rushing himself back.
 "I won't know until I get down to Charleston and see how it feels," Starks said. "You always want to rest an injury like that, but the playoffs aren't next week. They're this week. So, I just have to go for it."
 Riley said he was not counting on Starks, an All-Star, to perform like himself during the first round.
 "We want to see him against live competition this week," Riley said of Starks. "His conditioning and fatigue right now are really bad. I think this week will be a good measuring stick as to how much he can help us early, if he can at all."
 A decision must also be made Monday whether or not to activate Doc Rivers for the playoffs. The point guard has been out since December after undergoing reconstructive knee surgery. Even if Rivers is activated, he would be be ready to play until late May at the earliest. Since only 12 players can be placed on the playoff roster, the Knicks may not be able to afford the luxury of activating him.
 
'Major Decisions'
 "We've got to make three or four major decisions tomorrow," Riley said. "We've got some guys that are hurt. Doc, I don't think, could be ready until the finals."
 The Knicks should also be concerned about the prospect of facing the Nets, a dangerous team. With no dominant team in the league this season, the possibility of first-round upsets exists. And Riley does not want the Knicks to be one of the early playoff casualties.
 "The first round of the playoffs is a tough series for anybody," Riley said. "You can play great and lose a tough game and your back's against the wall. We have to be right our first two home games, regardless of who it is.
 "I think we're going in on a high note. I think it's going to be one of the great playoff series ever. I don't think there's a team in the Eastern Conference that doesn't think they can't beat somebody else."
 The Knicks said their 1-4 record against the Nets this season would be irrelevant during the playoffs.
 
Playoff Series Is 0-0
 "They can say anything they want," said guard Greg Anthony. "Talk doesn't win anything for you. As far as we're concerned, it's 0-0. We'll see when the series is over who's talking"
 Said Starks, "There's no doubt in my mind that we're going to play real strong and move on to the second round."
 But there is no doubt that more pressure will be on the Knicks than the Nets. But the Knicks believe they can handle it.
 "What's pressure?" Ewing said. "Pressure is being unemployed and trying to feed five kids. This is ain't pressure. This is fun."
 
REBOUNDS
The Knicks and Nets have met once before in the playoffs, in 1983. The Knicks won a first-round, two-out-of-three series in two games. . . . CHARLES GRANTHAM, director of the Players Association, said that he would speak to DAVE CHEKETTS, the Knicks' president, about ANTHONY MASON's suspension. "How can you suspend someone indefinitely?" Grantham said.

HEADLINE: After a Ruling, Hawaii Weighs Gay Marriages

BYLINE: By JANE GROSS, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: HONOLULU, April 23

 BODY:
This island state, where multiculturalism is a way of life and not just a slogan, could become the first to legalize marriage between people of the same sex -- or at least offer marital benefits for gay couples who register as domestic partners.
Whether by sanctioning gay marriage, which no other nation has done, or by passing America's first statewide domestic partnership act, Hawaii would lead the way in this fundamental redefinition of family, which some see as a sweeping expansion of civil rights and others see as an erosion of traditional values. 
 To stand on the far shore of change is a fitting role for this state, which is known for a progressive public policy, a liberal state constitution, a tolerance for diversity, an acceptance of intermarriage and a culture of flourishing same-sex relationships.
"Because the culture here is so intermixed, we are used to living together and letting people be whatever they want to be," said Hoku Akiu, a security guard, who hopes to marry his partner, Dwight Ovitt, and has already celebrated their union in a church full of sympathetic relatives and friends.
The stage was set for this far-reaching social change when the State Supreme Court ruled last May that refusing to license the marriage of three gay couples, thus depriving them of financial and legal benefits, violated the due process clause of the Hawaii Constitution. The State Constitution is more elaborate than its Federal counterpart and explicitly prohibits sex discrimination.
The Supreme Court sent the case back to the trial court to determine whether preserving the marriage ban was "a compelling state interest," the highest level of judicial scrutiny and one that legal experts say is rarely met unless public safety is at stake. The trial court is to hear the case next year.
 
Lawmakers Get Busy
 Stunned by the court's landmark ruling and pressured by conservative constituents, lawmakers here aimed in their current session to clarify the state's marriage statute, either as a way of persuading the courts to defer to the Legislature or as a way of giving extra ammunition to the Attorney General, who must defend the ban.
Hawaii's legislators considered, on the one hand, a constitutional amendment that would limit marriage to heterosexuals and, on the other, a broad domestic partnership act. Instead, loath to offend anyone in a year when they are up for re-election, the lawmakers have agreed on a bill reiterating that marriage is meant for "one man and one woman" and creating a commission to propose remedies for the inequities faced by same-sex couples, who are ineligible for tax breaks, Social Security benefits, rights of inheritance and cheaper hunting licenses, to name but a few of the privileges that fall to married couples.
The Attorney General, Robert A. Marks, had been clutching at straws as he sought ways to prove compelling state interest. But the clarifying language from the Legislature might persuade the court that limiting marriage to heterosexual couples is merely a benign form of discrimination. And the creation of the commission, the Attorney General said, might persuade the court that the state is truly committed to wider rights for gay couples.
 
'How Much, How Soon'
 A result of this judicial and legislative jockeying, virtually everyone agrees, will be either legalization of gay marriage by the court or a broad domestic partnership act after the commission completes its work, and perhaps both. "That was something people thought was unattainable before May 5," said Daniel R. Foley, the lawyer for the three gay couples, citing the date of the Supreme Court's 3-to-2 decision. "And now the only question is how much, how soon."
Joseph Mellio, a 46-year-old chef, and Patrick Lagon, a 36-year-old graphic artist, hope to be the first to the altar if they win the case that they began more than three years ago, along with two lesbian couples here.
Both men were raised in Roman Catholic families, Mr. Mellio in Summit, N.J., and Mr. Lagon in a Filipino enclave here, and they speak proudly of their "simple" life together: a yard full of dogs, a tropical flower garden, quiet nights in front of the TV, weekend drives and family gatherings.
"Why does anybody want to get married?" Mr. Mellio asked. "You answer for me. Our reasons aren't different. Our agendas aren't different. You meet someone. You fall in love. You get married and live happily ever after. That's all we want."
 
Raising Concerns
 Legal experts say that the court here is likely to lift the ban on same-sex marriage, given the bold rationale of its preliminary ruling and the changes in its composition since then.
And that likelihood has raised concern among local officials about the impact on tourism, among fundamentalists about the radical redefinition of family and among gay rights advocates about whether marriage is an unalienable right or an inequitable institution. Constitutional scholars are unsure whether other states will recognize gay marriages in Hawaii.
The reciprocity issue, they say, is sure to land in the United States Supreme Court, which heard an analogous case in 1967 in Loving v. Virginia and upheld the marriage of an interracial couple wed in the District of Columbia and jailed upon their return to a state that still had a miscegenation law.
In general, states recognize one another's legal acts, including marriage, under the full faith and credit provision of the United States Constitution. The only exception is when one state claims a violation of local public policy, which is expected to be the explanation for not recognizing gay marriage in the 23 states where sodomy remains a criminal act.
 
Tourism and Hand-Wringing
 The tourism question has led to much hand-wringing. Tourism, is the state's No. 1 industry, generating $9 billion a year and accounting for one-third of Hawaii's gross state product and 4 of 10 of its civilian jobs. Amid recession in California and Japan, where most of the visitors originate, tourism has declined in recent years, fueling concern that a flood of gay couples might scare off family-minded tourists and traditional honeymooners.
A few economists predict that an influx of gay tourists would more than make up for any lost business, but they concede that their calculations will be affected by the success of conservative groups that plan boycotts if gay marriage goes forward.
"This is part of the pan-sexual movement's attempt to deconstruct traditional morality in the culture and we take it very seriously," said Robert H. Knight, director of cultural studies at the Family Research Council in Washington.
While legalizing same-sex marriage is seen by groups like the Family Research Council and the Roman Catholic Church as a radical move, gay rights advocates consider it the most conservative item on their agenda and disagree about whether it is a worthy goal. On one side are the assimilationists, who consider marriage a way of gaining benefits and respect. On the other side are the separatists, who say state-sanctioned monogamous unions are an affront to their movement and a means of taming an alternative life style.
That debate was given full voice for the first time in 1989, the year that Denmark recognized gay partnerships, conferring on them all the economic rights of marriage but not the right to adopt children. That was also the year San Francisco and West Hollywood (and later about 150 municipalities, New York among them) passed far more limited laws giving medical and other benefits to the gay partners of city employees.
 
Kentucky and Minnesota
 The debate, framed by two gay rights lawyers, Thomas B. Stoddard and Paula L. Ettelbrick, did not envision the legalization of gay marriage anytime soon. In fact, the last significant challenges to the ban on same-sex marriage came 15 years before, when courts in Kentucky, Minnesota and Washington State upheld the status quo by asserting that marriage was by definition the collaboration of a man and a woman, which Hawaii last year labeled as "circular and unpersuasive" reasoning.
As Mr. Stoddard sees it, just months after his own ceremony with Walter Rieman, the issue remains "equal access to an institution of great practical and emotional importance to most Americans."
For Ms. Ettelbrick, by contrast, "the true access question is whether marriage is the appropriate vehicle" for determining economic rights and privileges. "I'd rather spend our resources developing a broader view of family and not replicating heterosexist institutions," she said.
 
Staking Out Differences
 But on these fragrant islands, so far from the mainland in both distance and attitude, the crossfire between Mr. Stoddard and Mr. Ettelbrick seems an unnecessary argument, a way of staking out differences. And that is not the way things are done here, where the local style is live and let live, interracial marriage is ho-hum and homosexuality is barely remarked upon.
"The host culture has no problem with mahu's or moe-aikane, because almost every family has them," said Annelle C. Amarel, a state representative, using the Hawaiian words for men who dress as women and men who have sex with other men.
That was certainly Mr. Akiu's experience growing up gay in a family that has been here for generations, mingling its Hawaiian, Chinese, Filipino and Portuguese ancestry and practicing myriad Eastern and Western religions. His "wedding" party included his mother, uncle, cousins, brothers and his partner's children from a former heterosexual marriage. But nobody stood around congratulating themselves for their diversity.
"It's a Western view that if you're different you have to talk about it," Mr. Akiu said. "People here just quietly learn and adapt and adjust."

HEADLINE: AS PREMIER TAKES OFFICE IN JAPAN, COALITION CRACKS

BYLINE: By DAVID E. SANGER, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: TOKYO, Tuesday, April 26

 BODY:
Tsutomu Hata took office on Monday as Japan's 51st Prime Minister, but his fractious governing coalition disintegrated this morning as the largest party in the group bolted and left Mr. Hata heading Japan's first minority Government in four decades.
The decision by the Socialist Party to back out of the coalition stripped Mr. Hata of his narrow majority in Parliament and cast into grave doubt his ability to enact a series of steps sought by the United States. 
 These measures include an economic stimulus program, deregulation of the economy, steps to open Japanese markets to imports and a solid stand in the effort to force North Korea to accept nuclear inspections.
The Socialist Party's move came after its members discovered what they called a plot by the rest of the coalition to freeze them out of the new Government.
Only last Friday, the Socialists joined with other members of the coalition, which has governed since last summer, to designate Mr. Hata as the successor to Prime Minister Morihiro Hosokawa, who resigned over a financial scandal.
Their action plunges Mr. Hata's Government into a crisis before he has had time to choose his Cabinet or even move into the Prime Minister's residence. Without the Socialists, he commands only 182 votes in the lower house of Parliament, well short of the 256 he needs to pass legislation.
For his Government to survive, he essentially faces three choices. He can try to set off another political earthquake that would allow him to pull in 70 or more defectors from the defeated Liberal Democratic Party, which has 206 members. He can call new elections. Or he can try to lure the Socialists back.
The first option seems the most likely. The Liberal Democrats ran the country for 38 years until they were ousted last summer in a revolt Mr. Hata helped lead, and have been disintegrating ever since.
The eruption on Monday night, just hours after Parliament officially declared the 58-year-old Mr. Hata the Prime Minister, was prompted by an effort among five ideologically like-minded parties to form a parliamentary bloc of 180 or so members that would give the coalition a greater semblance of unity.
The Socialists, who have been battling for months with the conservative architect of Japan's coalition strategy, Ichiro Ozawa, were excluded from the bloc. They saw the move -- correctly, most analysts say -- as a major step toward ousting them as Mr. Ozawa and Mr. Hata try to form a second conservative party to rival the Liberal Democrats.
 
'We Are Pulling Out'
 "It is a betrayal," Tomiichi Mura yama, the chief of the Socialist Party, declared at a news conference at 1:30 this morning. "There is no room for further talk. We are pulling out of all forums of the ruling coalition."
Mr. Hata, appearing in front of television cameras in a hallway of the Prime Minister's office a little after 3 A.M., said, "I did not know that the parliamentary bloc would be formed yesterday." This was seen as a remarkable statement, because the party he heads with Mr. Ozawa, the Japan Renewal Party, is at the core of the new group.
He said the Socialists' decision "was quite regrettable" and had resulted from "a misunderstanding." He said he hoped to talk to the Socialists later today.
But the damage was clearly done. In the last several months, the Socialists have been at odds with the rest of the coalition on matters as petty as old grudges and as grand as major policy differences -- chiefly on the question of raising sales taxes and taking a hard line on North Korea.
Historically, the Socialists have opposed any increase in taxes aimed at ordinary Japanese shoppers, and they have long had ties with the North Korean Government.
Several times since a coalition Government was formed last summer under former Prime Minister Hosokawa, who formally stepped down on Monday, the Socialists have threatened to walk out and bring the coalition to the brink of collapse. But at the last minute a deal was always worked to keep them in.
 
Socialists Feel Betrayed
 This time, though, the Socialists felt double-crossed. They had just spent two weeks drawing up a policy accord with the other members of the coalition, and then agreed to back Mr. Hata in an effort to end the infighting that crippled Mr. Hosokawa's Government.
To many members of the Socialist Party, a split like this was inevitable.
The political revolution under way in Japan has been tearing down the cold war structure that ran the country since 1955. Under this arrangement, the conservative Liberal Democratic Party ruled and the Socialists formed a perpetual opposition.
Now that structure is being smashed apart. The Liberal Democrats have already lost more than a third of their members as prominent members -- including Mr. Hata and Mr. Ozawa -- defected to form the coalition.
More Liberal Democrats have fallen away since, and Mr. Ozawa's clear aim is to smash apart the remainder, drawing in younger members and leaving the party's elders at sea, with little choice but retirement.
He is likely to step up that effort now, in an attempt to secure a new majority for Mr. Hata.

HEADLINE: THE SOUTH AFRICAN VOTE: ZULUS;
A Greatly Feared Zulu Watches Over the Political Machine

BYLINE: By KENNETH B. NOBLE, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: LINDELANI, South Africa, April 23

 BODY:
A week ago, Thomas Mandla Shabalala proudly counted himself among the two or three most feared men in South Africa. He may still be, but he has also become something else: the Inkatha Freedom Party's most formidable vote getter.
The self-proclaimed mayor of this sprawling squatter township on the hilly outskirts of Durban, Mr. Shabalala is perhaps the most visible of Inkatha's urban bosses. He is regularly reviled (misrepresented, he says) by the South African press for controlling his fief with what critics say is a mix of terror and patronage. 
 He is also one of party's most magnetic leaders, capable of mobilizing thousands of club-wielding, spear-carrying Amabutho, or Zulu warriors, into street battle with political rivals, mainly young African National Congress loyalists. And if Inkatha had gone to war, as many South Africans speculated it was preparing to do, Mr. Shabalala would almost certainly have taken a prime spot on the front lines.
All that changed after Inkatha abruptly ended its boycott of the elections, leaving the party scant time to embrace the razzmatazz of an American-style campaign. For Mr. Shabalala, Inkatha's change of mind transformed him virtually overnight from warlord to ward boss.
 
'One of Our Best Assets'
 Not only here in Lindelani, but also in much of the Natal midlands, Mr. Shabalala's success in pursuing votes for Inkatha is highly regarded, especially among the poor, often unemployed and mostly illiterate Zulus who form the bedrock of the party's support. Chief Mangosuthu G. Buthelezi is, of course, the better known politician, but at the grass roots, by some accounts, Mr. Shabalala has a greater following.
"Thomas Shabalala has been demonized, but most of what is said about him is simply not true," said Ziba Jiyane, the organization's chief spokesman. "He is absolutely one of our best assets."
In other words, said a political analyst, "while Buthelezi takes all the limelight, Shabalala is the guy who will make sure that Inkatha's supporters actually get to the polls.'
"And you can be sure," he said, "that he'll know who does and doesn't vote, and those that don't will have to face the consequences."
For his part, Mr. Shabalala appears bemused by his reputation as a roughneck. "Because I'm Inkatha, I'm called a warlord," he said. "But if I were A.N.C., I'd be called a hero."
In the coming days, Mr. Shabalala's various talents will be sorely tested. Opinion polls and conversations with politicians here suggest that Inkatha should consider itself fortunate if it wins a majority of the votes even in nationalistic Zulu strongholds like Lindelani. The party's prospects will almost certainly be even worse nationwide, where by nearly all accounts, they will be routed by the African National Congress and the National Party.
 
'A Lot of Work To Do'
 "Many of our people don't even know where to put the 'X' on their ballot sheets," Mr. Shabalala said outside his modest compound. "They don't know anything about voting. We've got a lot of work to do, and not nearly enough time."
He himself was, among other things, an amateur heavyweight boxing champion and a bus driver, two occupations that served him well in later life. Boxing helped cement his already fearsome reputation as a street fighter, while driving a bus, and later as the owner of a fleet of taxis, gave him an intimate knowledge of the territory as well as a firm grounding in the peculiar economics of running a business under the apartheid system.
It was his fleet ownership, in particular, that propelled his future career. "Shabalala was simply the most ruthless man in town," said a lawyer who has long experience in working in the townships. "He thought nothing of knocking a chap in the head if he felt like it, and he often felt like it."
Those days are far behind, Mr. Shabalala insists. And in a display of his new role as campaigner for peace and fraternity, Mr. Shabalala was dressed in a brand-new T-shirt with two doves, one white the other blue, which have become a symbol of what most South Africans hope will be a relatively tranquil transition, despite recent pre-election violence.
So far, amid much vague oratory, Inkatha's single unmistakable thesis is that the African National Congress is run by Communists who are bent on turning South Africa into a centralized, totalitarian state.
The rest of the party line appears to depend on the audience: it can include anti-ethnic innuendo against the Xhosa tribe, especially Nelson Mandela. The Xhosas were defeated by Zulu regiments in 19th-century battles and they are still scorned by some Zulu traditionalists. To wealthy white businessmen who have bankrolled Inkatha in the past, party officials spout the gospel of the free-enterprise system.
Mr. Shabalala's politics are a bit more complicated. For one thing, despite his reputation as a wrangler, he strongly defends what he calls traditional Zulu values, like discipline and obedience to one's elders. One reason he says he cannot abide the A.N.C. is that he "can't support a party run by children."
Until now, Mr. Shabalala was best known in Natal for his reputation for violence. He has been formally charged with two murders and implicated in numerous other assaults, but never convicted.
But Mr. Shabalala's stature depends on more than just being a street fighter. He is also in many ways the quintessential ward boss. In Lindelani, he controls the allocation of nearly all community services -- from setting up health clinics and schools, to distributing pensions, to police and fire protection. He also owns and operates the biggest taxi service here, giving him a crucial role in transporting voters to and from polling booths on election day.
 
'I Have No Fears'
 Even if, as nearly everyone assumes, the African National Congress wins the election, it will hardly be the end of Mr. Shabalala's political career. For one thing, it is unclear what will happen to his political machine, particularly his exclusive channels of patronage.
In theory, the A.N.C. will be able to replace Mr. Shabalala in Lindelani with an official of its own choosing, or perhaps call elections to allow the people of KwaZulu, the Zula homeland, to decide freely for the first time who their leaders will be.
But political analysts say it is far from certain whether the congress will have the political clout to remove Mr. Shabalala, and, for his part, he is certain that he will not be leaving anytime soon.
"I have no fears," he said. "I believe that the KwaZulu government will be here forever."

GRAPHIC: Photo: Thomas Mandla Shabalala has become the Inkatha Freedom Party's most formidable vote getter in an area where it needs him most. (Ozier Muhammad/The New York Times)

HEADLINE: U.S. Accepts Jordan Plea On Iraq Trade

BYLINE: By STEVEN GREENHOUSE, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: LONDON, April 25

 BODY:
The United States removed a major stumbling block today to Jordan's full participation in Middle East peace talks by agreeing to adopt new procedures for enforcing the trade embargo against Iraq that are far less onerous to Jordan.
After meeting with King Hussein in a London suburb, Secretary of State Warren Christopher announced that the United States had accepted Jordan's request for land-based inspections of cargo, rather than sea-based inspections, which Jordan said were hurting its economy. 
 In recent weeks, King Hussein has vaguely threatened not to rejoin the talks with Israel unless the inspection procedures were changed. He asserted that under the inspections, about 460 vessels had been turned back before reaching Jordan's major port of Aqaba, mainly because the ships' paperwork did not detail their cargo's ultimate destination.
Jordanian officials, whose country was once a major trading partner with Iraq, complained that those inspections have done $300 million in damage to their economy.
 
King Is Satisfied
 "The enforcement of the sanctions against Iraq is highly important," Mr. Christopher said at a news conference with King Hussein. "But this was having a very adverse effect on Jordan's economy."
King Hussein said he was satisfied with the new inspection procedures, under which professional trade experts from Lloyd's Register will look for prohibited cargo in warehouses at Aqaba. The embargo bars all trade with Iraq except food and medicine.
The King sounded eager to return to the peace talks, but said he was awaiting the end of Mr. Christopher's one-week swing through the Middle East to see exactly when Syria, Lebanon and his country would resume talks with Israel in Washington.
Mr. Christopher said the new inspection procedures would not undermine the embargo's effectiveness.
"We believe in some respects land-based inspection will be an improvement in our ability to enforce sanctions against Iraq," Mr. Christopher said, after lunch at King Hussein's heavily guarded 73-acre country estate west of London. The King is in London for medical treatment.
A senior American official traveling with Mr. Christopher said inspecting cargo in warehouses would be easier and more comprehensive than stopping ships and rummaging around their holds on the high seas.
Administration officials said the land-based inspections would cost about $2 million to $3 million a year and would be paid by Jordan.

HEADLINE: Giuliani and Whitman Stress Mutual Understanding in Broadcast

BYLINE: By IVER PETERSON 

 BODY:
There were promises of mutual support and understanding during a television appearance last night shared by Mayor Rudolph W. Giuliani and Gov. Christine Todd Whitman as the two Republican newcomers to office set out to ease the tensions over crime and business competition between New York City and New Jersey.
Conscious of the often fractious past relations between the cross-Hudson neighbors, Mrs. Whitman and Mr. Giuliani took pains to support each other's views. 
 They agreed on a need to improve coordination of cross-border crime fighting, particularly in identifying and arresting drug buyers who shuttle between the states. They said they would focus their business-building efforts on attracting investment to the region from other states and countries, not from each other's tax rolls. The Governor and the Mayor also took a hard line against the Nation of Islam and its minister, Louis Farrakhan, while calling for more programs to educate children in the ways of tolerance.
 
A Regional Economy
 "I think we both understand that this is a region, and that this is a regional economy that we're trying to establish here," Mayor Giuliani said. "If New York City continues to lose jobs, it will hurt New Jersey, and if New Jersey were to lose jobs, it would hurt New York. We're in this together."
Mayor Giuliani and Governor Whitman made their appearance at a town meeting televised live on WNET Channel 13. A studio audience posed questions on crime, the regional economy, taxes and race relations.
Both officials supported the belief that lower taxes will promote a stronger economy and favored laws against bias crime and called for nationwide handgun registration.
But on race relations the two drew sharp lines against any meetings with Mr. Farrakhan. When a member of the Nation of Islam asked the two elected officials if they favored giving school children a grounding in black history, and suggested that they meet Mr. Farrakhan, the Mayor recalled the still unsolved beatings of eight New York City police officers at a Harlem mosque during his first weeks in office.
"I would certainly be willing to make certain that every child in New York City got a balanced view, but I wouldn't sit down with someone representing Louis Farrakhan, and certainly not with someone who ran a mosque that has yet to turn in the people who beat up eight police officers."
Mrs. Whitman said, "I would be very interested in sitting down with responsible representatives of the Muslim faith, but I would have no interest in sitting down with him."

HEADLINE: A Russian Reformer Is Down but Not Out

BYLINE: By BARNABY J. FEDER, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: CHICAGO, April 25

 BODY:
Boris G. Fyodorov, who warned when he resigned as Russia's Finance Minister in January that anti-reform politicians and industrial leaders were plotting an "economic coup," may be biding his time, but he's not biting his tongue.
If anything, Mr. Fyodorov has become more vociferous since retiring -- temporarily, as he sees it -- to a position in the Soviet Parliament. Appearing here on Friday at a conference sponsored by the University of Chicago on the role of central banks in Eastern Europe and the former Soviet Union, Mr. Fyodorov assailed the International Monetary Fund for its recent approval of a $1.5 billion loan to the Government of President Boris N. Yeltsin and Prime Minister Viktor S. Chernomyrdin. 
 Mr. Fyodorov said the loan sent a destructive message -- that the West approved of the Government's abandonment of the push for changes that he and other economic reformers had championed. He accused Russia's central bank of acting like "a licensed industrial and agricultural lobbyist," concerned primarily with propping up enterprises that were remnants of the centralized economy. And he criticized a recent proposal to link the monetary systems of Russia and Belarus as a "dangerous experiment" rather than a useful step toward economic integration.
 "If we don't change our policies, external help won't make any difference," Mr. Fyodorov said in an interview after his presentation.
He said that the I.M.F. loan reflected misguided political pressure on the I.M.F. from the United States and other members of the Group of Seven major industrial nations. Supporters of the loan say that it will help Russia avoid social unrest that could sink any reforms. But Mr. Fyodorov said the loan was too small to have a real impact and it would never have been approved had the I.M.F. followed the views of its own technical staff as to whether Russia was living up to its promises of economic change.
 
Economic Experience
 Such strong assertions would not surprise anyone who has followed Mr. Fyodorov career. A former Communist whose early assignments included posts at the central bank of the old Soviet Union and in the banking supervision department of the Communist Party Central Committee, Mr. Fyodorov, who is now 36, emerged as an outspoken monetarist and one of the most radical of the economic reformers under Mikhail S. Gorbachev. He has never been known for self-doubt, understatement or a charitable view of opponents.
 "I was arrogant and rude," he said unflinchingly about his tenure as Finance Minister. "I took power from other people because I wasn't afraid to lose my job," he added, citing instances of refusing to spend money the Government had pledged to various programs because he felt that the promises violated the integrity of the budget and contributed to inflation.
Eventually, though, Mr. Fyodorov felt stymied by the power of Deputy Prime Minister Aleksandr Zaveryukha, an old-line collective-farm boss, and Viktor Gerashchenko, head of Russia's central bank. He turned down an offer to continue as Finance Minister in the new Cabinet formed after the December election when Mr. Chernomyrdin refused his demand to dismiss the hard-liners.
"We must have the healthiest economy in the world because we only had eight bankruptcies in the whole country last year," he said, sarcastically describing his inability to force the Government to pull the plug on failed former Soviet enterprises.
Mr. Fyodorov's main platform these days is as chairman of the Russian Parliament's subcommittee on monetary and central bank affairs. One of his biggest mistakes as Finance Minister, he said, was not working harder on tax reform. Russia has an enormous range of taxes without apparent regard to what behavior they are supposed to encourage, he said, citing such anti-development examples as a sales tax on loans. He now believes that the monetary reforms he advocates cannot achieve as much as he once hoped without more coherent industrial and agricultural policies.
Mr. Fyodorov said he did not expect vigorous reformers to stay on the sidelines. He dismissed Vladimir Zhirinovsky, the extreme nationalist who gained international attention after his party's strong performance in December's elections, as "a marginal figure for Western consumption who is already losing support." The much better organized Communists, he said, remained the true threat to progress.
"We will return to the Government," he said confidently. "If not me, then someone with my views."

HEADLINE: CURRENCY MARKETS;
Dollar Falls Against Mark As Rate-Cut Pressure Ebbs

BYLINE: By Bloomberg Business News 

 BODY:
The dollar fell to a three-week low against the mark yesterday after forecasts that Germany's economy was recovering, easing pressure on the Bundesbank to cut interest rates.
"The market's focus this week is going to be German monetary policy," said Lisa Finstrom, currency analyst at Smith Barney Shearson. "People think the Bundesbank isn't going to cut rates very quickly." 
 The dollar was quoted late yesterday at 1.6799 marks, down from 1.6892 marks in New York on Friday. It fell to 1.6782 marks earlier in the day, its lowest level since April 1.
Traders bought marks after Hans Tietmeyer, the president of the Bundesbank, and other central bank officers said a faster pace of rate cuts was not warranted. Lower German interest rates would make investments denominated in marks less attractive.
The Bundesbank holds a meeting on monetary policy on Thursday. The central bank cut rates at its last meeting, lowering the discount rate a quarter-point, to 5 percent, and the Lombard rate to 6.5 from 6.75 percent. The discount rate is the lowest rate banks pay to borrow from the Bundesbank, while the Lombard is an emergency borrowing rate.
The dollar was little changed against the yen, meanwhile, after the Socialist Party of Japan quit the Government's fragile coalition.
Unlike other currencies, the yen typically is not hurt by a political crisis. Indeed, turmoil often strengthens the yen as it increases speculation that progress toward trade negotiations between the United States and Japan will be delayed as a result.
The dollar was quoted at 103.09 yen late yesterday, down from 103.30 yen on Friday.
Many currency traders expect the United States to endorse a stronger yen to press Japan on trade. A strong yen makes Japanese exports more expensive. The yen has risen 20 percent since February 1993.
"The pressure is still on the yen" after the latest round of political wrangling, said Steve Flanagan, a trader at Paine Webber.
In other trading in New York, the British pound rose to $1.4920, from $1.4886 on Friday. The dollar fell to 5.7650 French francs from 5.7941 francs. It was steady at 1.4345 Swiss francs. The dollar fell to 1,609 Italian lire from 1,619 lire. The Canadian dollar rose to 72.57 United States cents, from 72.56 cents on Friday.

HEADLINE: Transit Revolution Coming. Bring a Fare Card.;
Electronic Turnstiles Promise Vast Changes in Pricing, Planners Say

BYLINE: By MATTHEW L. WALD 

 BODY:
For 40 years, the most complicated decision about transit fares has been how much to charge for a token. This week, as Herald Square becomes the last of New York's 69 busiest subway stations to receive electronic turnstiles, the transit system is on the threshold of a brave new world of pricing possibilities that some say could transform the role of mass transit in the city's economy.
But nobody is sure just how, and the Metrocard electronic-fare system is for now a shiny $700 million tool that no one has decided how to use. 
 This summer the board of the Metropolitan Transportation Authority plans to begin choosing among a variety of options, from monthly passes to off-peak discounts, that it hopes will increase mass-transit ridership by tens of millions of people a year.
"This is not merely an electronic token," said Tito Davila, an M.T.A. spokesman. "This is a product that will help us to offer the discounts that will attract additional riders and make the system more convenient."
But transit planners say discounts will not produce enough new riders to make up for the revenue lost in reduced token sales, an amount that could reach $250 million a year. They say a multidimensional political fight in Albany is likely over the subsidies needed to make up for that revenue.
Suburban and rural interests will almost certainly oppose higher taxes to subsidize the discounts. Occasional users will oppose paying more for tokens to subsidize the monthly passes of daily commuters. If the M.T.A. offers free or almost-free transfers between the buses and the subways, people who buy monthly tickets on the Long Island Rail Road and Metro North will want the same. The fight could make past battles over a 10-cent increase in the price of a token seem like a high-school debate.
At least there will be no battle over charging by the mile; the new system controls only entry, not exit, so it cannot price the ride by distance, as Washington and other cities do.
But with the computerized fare card system, the Transit Authority could, for instance, offer monthly passes, at a price equal to 90 trips, or 50, or maybe just 38. Transfers from bus to subway could be discounted 25 percent, or even be free. Fares could be adjusted for the time of day.
To justify the sacrifices that taxpayers or less-frequent riders would have to make, planners are venturing into the uncharted territory of trying to determine the how the city would benefit financially from increased use of mass transit.
Rudimentary research has been done on the benefits of getting drivers out of their cars and into public transportation, reducing congestion and air pollution. But there is hardly any research on another development that could be more important: getting people who might otherwise have stayed home to make trips around the city, trips on which they are sure to spend money that will stimulate the economy.
Proving that may be essential to convincing the public and lawmakers that increased subsidies are justified.
"They're going to give away revenue, which is going to have to be made up somehow," said Bernard Cohen, the chief financial officer of the M.T.A., the parent body of the New York City Transit Authority and of the suburban railroads.
Experts agree that the options could have a big impact on traffic in the streets and on the economic life of the city. William M. Wheeler, director of planning at the M.T.A., is now trying to figure out how. But he is finding no studies of other cities to guide him. Other cities, he said, do not need such analyses because they are usually making only minor changes, having instituted pass systems years ago.
The system is entering a new age at a time when New York is under severe pressure to reduce auto use. Under the Clean Air Act, the State Department of Transportation imposed rules on April 6 under which big employers must take steps to lure their workers out of single-passenger automobiles.
And even without air-pollution problems, dense traffic causes congestion that raises the cost of doing business in the city and, though it is hard to quantify, lowers the quality of life.
Counting those costs and others, like accidents, law enforcement and land use for highways, the Tri-State Transportation Campaign, a coalition of environmental groups and the Regional Plan Association, recently calculated that vehicle use costs the public 25 cents a mile.
But there are other ways of looking at the economic benefits. If, say, free transfers and monthly passes lured 10,000 cars off the Gowanus Expressway every day, that would be good not only for the drivers of those cars, but also for the drivers who still use the Gowanus. And, by reducing road congestion and thus speeding up the delivery of goods and services, such a move would benefit the economy as a whole.
 
A Battle Over Subsidies
 If a monthly pass or free transfers created a benefit by getting people out of their cars, it would not flow to the M.T.A., but to the public as a whole, leaving the M.T.A. to argue in Albany that tax revenues should finance the investment.
That makes the lack of a way to estimate benefits a problem, said Gene Russianoff of the Straphangers Campaign, a transit riders' advocacy group, "because they're going to need extra subsidies to do what they want, and they're going to have to make a case, and they're going to need to say something."
Mr. Russianoff is hoping that the idea of providing free transfers for people who must take both a subway and a bus between home and work will prove so politically attractive that it will sweep Albany. One such part of the city, he noted, is the Lower East Side of Manhattan, which is represented by the Speaker of the Assembly, Sheldon Silver.
On the other side will be all the forces that usually oppose bigger subsidies for mass transit, people who don't want higher taxes, especially taxes that would be paid by suburbanites and others who make little use of the transit system.
Norman J. Levy, the chairman of the State Senate's transportation committee, said that the system had "exciting potential," but he added that his top priority was to avoid increasing the fare for anyone. That limits the resources available to offer new options like monthly passes.
Those options could eventually make a big difference for individuals, especially after conversion of the fare boxes in the buses, which starts next year. If it is followed by free transfers from subway to bus and back, using the Metrocard could cut in half the cost of traveling between Midtown or Wall Street and neighborhoods where the subway is beyond walking distance, like College Point in Queens or Riverdale in the Bronx.
Monthly passes would make many trips free, with customers buying a monthly pass to get to and from work, and then realizing that an extra trip to a store, a restaurant or a museum would cost nothing extra.
For years, experts say, transit use in places like New York has suffered because the economics of buying tokens is different from the economics of driving a car. The car has a high up-front cost -- car payments, insurance and registration. The cost of subsequent trips is cheap because gasoline, oil, tires and maintenance are relatively inexpensive.
 
Affecting Suburbanites
 But a rider on the subways or buses pays the same each time. With a monthly pass, then mass transit, too, would have a high up-front cost, but the price difference between a round-trip to work each day for a month versus a round-trip to work plus two or three other trips a day would be nil.
New pricing options could even affect suburban dwellers and the way they use their cars. One option under consideration is a combination monthly pass on suburban rail lines and the subways, which could make a weekend shopping trip in the city a cheaper choice for suburban commuters than a drive to a suburban mall.
At the New York Chamber of Commerce and Industry, James P. Gifford, an executive vice president, who is a member of an advisory committee to the M.T.A. on fares, said that a monthly pass would stimulate many extra trips, on which people would surely spend money that would stimulate the economy.
"There's a little bit of larceny in most of our hearts," he said, referring to a desire to get something for nothing. "I think of my own behavior. I can well imagine there will be those extra stops you wouldn't have made."
Some options could be in effect by the end of this year, although next year is more likely, officials say.
The agency has already done extensive advance work, surveying 24,000 people by telephone and asking them what price they would be willing to pay for different fare packages. The answers were used to calculate the ridership growth -- and the revenue loss -- with various combinations of fare options.
The Authority has run about 1,000 combinations through its computer, Mr. Cohen said. He provided two hypothetical fare plans to show the kind of combinations the agency has analyzed.
If, for example, the M.T.A. sold a monthly pass for $62.50 -- the price of 50 trips -- and made transfers between subways and buses free, ridership could be expected to grow by 71.3 million, from a base of about 1.2 billion, meaning growth of about 6 percent. Counting revenue from new riders but also revenue given up from people who would formerly have paid the transfers or bought more than 50 tokens in a month, the revenue loss would be $236.8 million, or about 15 percent of anticipated revenues.
If the monthly pass cost $68.75, the price of 55 trips, and a passenger transferring from subway to bus, or vice versa, was given a 75 percent discount on the second fare, and riders could get a 10 percent discount for buying $25 fare cards, then ridership would grow by 69 million, and the revenue loss would be $232 million, the model predicts.
The M.T.A. has been thinking about discounts and potential revenue losses for years, but on a much smaller scale. In the budget for this year it set aside $17 million for discounts, and for 1995 it plans to set aside $70 million. Since it is unlikely this year's allocation will be used, the agency could roll the two together. But it would not go far toward covering the cost of a monthly pass.

HEADLINE: Constantine Simonides, 59, an Official at M.I.T.

BYLINE: By WILLIAM H. HONAN 

 BODY:
Constantine Simonides, who was both vice president of the Massachusetts Institute of Technology and secretary of the M.I.T. board, died on Sunday while playing tennis at Wellesley College. He was 59 and lived in Wellesley, Mass.
The apparent cause was heart failure, said his son, Philip. 
 Mr. Simonides was the chief strategist for M.I.T. during more than two years of legal battles in Federal courts in which the Department of Justice contended that the institute and the eight Ivy League colleges and universities had violated Federal price-fixing laws by trading information about how much financial aid they were offering to students accepted at more than one institution.
A settlement of the case, which many educators considered a victory for M.I.T. and the Ivy League schools, was announced last December.
As vice president for the last 24 years, Mr. Simonides served four M.I.T. presidents. His responsibilities included personnel, the medical department, public relations, admissions, athletics, career services and the M.I.T. Press.
As secretary of the board for the last decade, Mr. Simonides was one of four chief administrators of the institute serving on the 73-member board of trustees.
Born in Athens, Mr. Simonides came to the United States as an exchange student at St. Andrew's School in Middletown, Del. He attended M.I.T., later received a degree in economics from Boston University and earned an M.B.A. at Harvard in 1960.
That year he joined M.I.T., rising from assistant to the director of the summer session to assistant dean of the Sloan School of Management in 1964. He was named a vice president of the institute in 1970.
Mr. Simonides is survived by his wife, Betty; a daughter, Cynthia, of Boston; two sons, Ted, of Palo Alto, Calif., and Philip, of Wellesley, Mass., and a sister, Julia Cotsarides of Athens.

HEADLINE: Market Place;
After the food poisoning, Foodmaker is still struggling.

BYLINE: By Calvin Sims 

DATELINE: LOS ANGELES

 BODY:
DESPITE a promising marketing campaign that lured many leery customers back to its Jack-in-the-Box restaurants, Foodmaker Inc. is still struggling to recover from an outbreak of food poisoning last year at its fast-food outlets in the Pacific Northwest.
The food contamination, which left two children dead and 400 people ill, sent Foodmaker's sales into a tailspin, as customers avoided Jack-in-the-Box restaurants in large numbers. 
 While Foodmaker's revenues appeared to be making a comeback late last year, aided by an aggressive advertising and pricing effort, securities analysts now say that it may take more than a year to return to the sales volumes before the food poisoning in January 1993.
Shares of Foodmaker closed yesterday at a 52-week low of $7.50, down 12.5 cents, on the New York Stock Exchange. From its recent high of $12.625 last May, the stock is down 41 percent.
Foodmaker, based in San Diego, reported a net loss of $4.4 million, or 11 cents a share, in the first fiscal quarter ending Jan 23, compared with net income of $11.5 million, or 29 cents a share, in the corresponding period last year. Revenues were $381.6 million, down from $403.3 million.
Average sales at company-owned restaurants were 9.2 percent below the level achieved in the corresponding period last year, prompting several analysts to lower their earnings estimates.
The restaurant chain finished its second fiscal quarter on April 17 and plans to announce those results in mid-May. Foodmaker officials said preliminary results indicated that sales were down about 9 percent for the second fiscal quarter.
"They have not made the recovery that people thought they would have by now," said Laurie Lively Smith, an analyst for the Seidler Companies here. "It's a reflection of the really competitive nature of the fast-food restaurant business and the poor economy here in Southern California. People still remember the food poisoning and it gives them a reason not to go to Jack in the Box."
Ms. Smith has reduced her earnings estimate to 10 cents a share from 30 cents a share for the 1994 fiscal year, which ends on Oct. 2. She now projects that Foodmaker will earn 35 cents a share for the 1995 fiscal year.
John W. Weiss, a San Francisco-based analyst for Montgomery Securities, has lowered his earnings estimate for Foodmaker to 20 cents from 60 cents a share for the 1994 fiscal year. Mr. Weiss said in a recent report that while it was still possible for Foodmaker to earn the $1 a share he had projected for the 1995 fiscal year, he is now forecasting earnings of 60 cents a share.
Even less optimistic is Eric Wasserman, who follows Foodmaker for Lehman Brothers. He now predicts that the fast-food chain will lose 5 cents a share this fiscal year, compared with his previous estimate of earnings of 30 cents a share.
Jack in the Box ranks fifth among the nation's fast-food hamburger establishments, behind McDonald's, Burger King, Hardees and Wendy's, according to the Nation's Restaurant News, an industry publication. About 60 percent of the 1,174 Jack-in-the-box restaurants are company owned.
Charles Duddles, Foodmaker's chief financial officer, acknowledged in an interview that the company's sales were below where management had expected them to be. "We thought by this time our sales would be even or above what they were before the food poisoning," Mr. Duddles said. "It's hard to distinguish if this is due to the poisoning or to California's weak economy."
Mr. Duddles said the company's market research showed that most customers who patronize Jack in the Box did so before the poisonings and that fewer than 5 percent of its customers had some lingering concerns about food safety.
For now, Foodmaker plans to continue its strategy of introducing three to four new products a year and offering at least one entree item for 99 cents, Mr. Duddles said. The company is also continuing to open new restaurants and remodel older establishments.

HEADLINE: Memberships Vote to Merge 2 New York Futures Markets

BYLINE: By ADAM BRYANT 

 BODY:
The leading exchanges for trading oil and gold futures consummated their on-again, off-again courtship last night as their members voted to merge.
The New York Mercantile Exchange will pay more than $60 million to members of the Commodity Exchange of New York. The merger will enable the two exchanges to cut expenses by reducing duplication in computer systems and in the work force that handles marketing, accounting and product development. 
 The merger calls for the Commodity Exchange, the world's largest precious metals futures exchange, to operate as a separate exchange owned by the New York Mercantile Exchange, which is the world's leading futures market for oil.
The merger required approval by two-thirds members of the Commodity Exchange, or Comex, and a simple majority by members of the New York Mercantile Exchange, or Nymex. Comex members approved the deal by a vote of 477 to 181, while Nymex members voted 468 to 94 in favor of the merger.
Approval of the merger agreement was not entirely expected. The Comex has seen its fortunes flag in recent years as investors have increasingly turned to financial instruments rather than gold and other precious metals as a hedge against inflation. As a result, Comex's trading volume has fallen, with net income dropping to $674,000 in 1992 from $3.29 million in 1991. But in recent months, the Comex has recovered sharply, leading many members to question whether a merger was necessary.
Many Comex members lobbied their counterparts in recent months with letters arguing that considering the exchange's renewed vigor, the Nymex's proposed payment was inadequate, especially in light of the control that Comex members would lose in the combined exchange.
"The merger will transform Comex into an essentially colonial backwater of Nymex," wrote Andrew F. Brimmer, a public governor of the Comex board, in an April 1 letter to Comex members.
Under the terms of the deal, Comex will become a wholly owned subsidiary of Nymex, in return for its members receiving $42 million at closing and $20 million over the following four years. Members of each exchange will also gain some rights to trade futures on both exchanges.
 
Other Mergers Discussed
 The merger, which requires the approval of the Commodity Futures Trading Commission, also raises the stakes for the home of the exchange. Nymex has long said it will move from its quarters at 4 World Trade Center, and sites in both Manhattan and New Jersey have been wooing the businesses. Possible locations include Battery Park City, TriBeCa, 55 Water Street and a site owned by Colgate-Palmolive in Jersey City.
For years, Comex has discussed a possible merger with other exchanges, including the Chicago Board of Trade, the Coffee, Sugar and Cocoa Exchange, the New York Stock Exchange and the Chicago Mercantile Exchange.
But many experts believe the long-term decline in value of a seat on the Comex has prompted members to set aside their historical reluctance to merge. About 20 years ago, a seat on the Comex sold for about $325,000; today, it sells for about $100,000.
Daniel Rappaport, chairman of Nymex, has predicted that a merger of the two exchanges could cut expenses by as much as $6 million, or roughly 8 percent of their combined budgets.
Talks in 1991 between Comex and Nymex broke down over disagreements about sharing access to one another's contracts. Some Comex governors have said the new agreement is more favorable than other offers last year from other exchanges besides Nymex.

HEADLINE: Dow Jumps As Bonds Post Gains

BYLINE: By ANTHONY RAMIREZ 

 BODY:
In a surprising show of vigor, traders bid up stocks yesterday in response to a sharp drop in interest rates and better-than-expected earnings for companies whose stocks are in the Dow Jones industrial average.
Stocks also got a broad lift from recommendations by a respected investor, Peter Lynch, who spoke favorably about dozens of what he called undervalued stocks, ranging from giants like General Motors to obscure concerns like the Quixote Corporation, a maker of specialized equipment ranging from compact disks to highway-safety products. The stocks of nearly all of the companies he mentioned rose yesterday.
 
Anticipating Statistics
 Moreover, looking to this week's economic reports -- wage statistics due out today, durable-goods figures tomorrow and gross domestic product data on Thursday -- some analysts were saying that if the numbers encouraged a continuation of a rally in bond prices, then stocks might also show further gains. 
 The stock rally yesterday was closely tied to strength in the bond market. The yield on the benchmark 30-year Treasury bond dropped to 7.15 percent, from 7.22 percent on Friday. [Page D16.]
Still, analysts were quick to point out that trading volumes in both the stock and bond markets were below normal, and price swings thus tended to be exaggerated. Volume on the New York Stock Exchange was 263.6 million shares, or more than 13 percent below this year's daily average of 305 million shares.
Many stock and bond traders did stay on the sidelines yesterday, made even more cautious by the closing of financial markets tomorrow to observe a day of mourning for former President Richard M. Nixon. That means the markets will be unable to react immediately to the report on durable-goods orders for March, which will be released tomorrow.
 
Looking for Signs
 The data will be closely watched for any signs that higher interest rates are curbing demand for durable goods, which are relatively expensive items like furniture and appliances that are often bought on credit. While the recent rally in the bond market has brought long-term interest rates down about a quarter of a percentage point, rates are still well above where they were when the Federal Reserve began raising short-term interest rates in February in an effort to head off a jump in inflation in the future.
Yesterday, the Dow Jones industrial average rose 57.10 points, to 3,705.78, and the broader Standard & Poor's 500-stock index rose 5.08 points, to 452.71. The smaller-company Nasdaq composite index rose 8.24 points, to 730.80.
Yesterday's gain was one of the largest for the Dow this year and the third sizable surge this month. There was an 82-point climb on April 5, in response to a bond rally and hopes that quarterly earnings reports would be strong, and there was a jump of 54 points last Thursday, following gains in the bond market and higher-than-expected profits from I.B.M.
As for the bond market, some analysts said that if the economic reports this week were encouraging, the yield on the benchmark 30-year bond could fall to 7 percent.
But why the bond market rose yesterday remained a mystery to some traders. Christopher J. Madell, head of government bond trading at First Chicago Capital Markets Inc., said, "Part of the problem I have with this market is that people always gather around at the end of the day and say, 'Now why did we do what we just did?' "
Kathleen M. Camilli, chief bond economist at Maria Fiorini Ramirez, an economic consulting firm, said managers of bond funds had probably sold off too many of their assets in anticipation of a rush of mutual fund redemptions. Since that has not happened to a great extent, they now have a sizable amount of cash, which they can use to buy bonds.
Industry estimates are that the cash portion of fixed-income fund assets is now 12 percent, up from 5 percent in January, she said.
Ms. Camilli listed what she regarded as negative factors -- trade relations between the United States and Japan remain troubled, the Clinton health care plan could hurt business, the economy might slow and the Federal Reserve Board continues to raise interest rates. "This is still just a rally within an overall bear market for bonds," and therefore for stocks, she said.
The New York Stock Exchange composite index rose 2.52 points yesterday, to 250.47. Advancing stocks outnumbered those declining by 1,552 to 667. The American Stock Exchange market value index rose 2.66 points, to 435.97.
 
Aided by Earnings
 The market was given an early lift by relatively strong earnings for such Dow component stocks as Du Pont, Goodyear Tire, Boeing and Texaco.
But investors appeared to respond more to the stock advice of Mr. Lynch, the former manager of the Fidelity Magellan Fund, the nation's largest and one of its most successful mutual funds.
Of 47 stocks (including one Canadian company) recommended by Mr. Lynch in an interview published in Barron's magazine over the weekend, some were up strongly, only two declined and one closed unchanged yesterday. The three that did not post gains were Philip Morris, the tobacco and food company, which fell 3/4, to 52 1/4; Designs Inc., an operator of Levi Strauss clothing stores, which was down 1 1/4, to 14 1/4, and Mirage Resorts, a gambling company, which closed unchanged at 19 1/2.
Under the headline "A Legendary Investor Finds the Street Paved With Bargains," the Barron's article quoted Mr. Lynch as saying that "in the last three or four months, a lot more stocks have fallen into a buy range."
