HEADLINE: Confession Used to Portray Rifkin as Methodical Killer

BYLINE: By JOHN T. McQUISTON, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: MINEOLA, L.I., April 25

 BODY:
As jurors heard the details of Joel Rifkin's confession to killing 17 women, the prosecution today sought to portray him as a methodical killer who knew that he had done wrong and who was remorseful.
Mr. Rifkin does not deny killing the women, but says he was mentally ill and acted out of a perverse sexual passion that he could not control. 
 The prosecution's line of questioning today was intended to undercut that argument.
"I'm in a lot of trouble," Mr. Rifkin told Trooper C. Thomas Capers, when he confessed on June 28 to killing Tiffany Bresciani and 16 other women he said were prostitutes. He told Trooper Capers he was concerned that his mother would be upset by what he had done.
Trooper Capers, appearing as a witness for the prosecution, recalled the six hours in which Mr. Rifkin detailed the 17 murders in a hand-written confession. He told Trooper Capers that he would often pick up prostitutes in New York City and on Long Island.
 
Suspect's Remorse Described
 Trooper Capers said Mr. Rifkin said he was sorry that he had killed Miss Bresciani and that he appeared "calm, matter-of-fact and responsive" during the interrogation.
On one occasion, Mr. Rifkin said, he took a prostitute to his home in East Meadow when his mother and sister were away. The prostitute not only left with her life, but took some jewelry that belonged to Mr. Rifkin's mother, Trooper Capers said.
"Mr. Rifkin said he did not report the theft to the police and that his mother was very upset by the incident," Trooper Capers said under questioning by the prosecutor, Fred Klein. "So he said he behaved himself for a while."
Trooper Capers also said Mr. Rifkin, who was receiving unemployment at the time of his arrest, expressed concern about his income, the cost of prostitutes and giving money to his mother.
"He said he felt guilty collecting money from unemployment and not contributing to household expenses, while spending money on prostitutes," Trooper Capers said.
 
Defense Claims Mental Illness
 Mr. Rifkin's lawyer, John Lawrence, has described Mr. Rifkin's actions as those of a mentally ill person who was not responsible for his behavior.
During his interrogation, Trooper Capers said, Mr. Rifkin asked for a pen, paper, a calendar and a map and proceeded to list all his victims, when and where he had met them, how and where he disposed of their bodies and what items of clothing and jewelry he kept for himself.
Although Mr. Rifkin, a 34-year-old unemployed landscaper, faces only one murder charge in Nassau County Court, for the slaying of Miss Bresciani, 22, all his victims have become a part of the testimony because of his insanity defense. He also faces murder charges in Suffolk County, Brooklyn, Queens and the Bronx.
Mr. Rifkin told Trooper Capers that he had picked up Miss Bresciani on Allen Street in lower Manhattan on June 24 and had paid her $20 for sex in the back seat of his mother's car, where he strangled her.
He later wrapped the body in a blue tarp, put the body in the trunk of the car and then drove his mother to the Nassau County Medical Center, where she worked one day a week.
 
Body Stored in Garage
 While his mother was at work, he took the body home, placed it in a wheelbarrow and stored it in the garage for two days. After 2 A.M. on June 28, he placed the body in the back of his pickup truck, let the truck roll down the driveway so he wouldn't wake up his mother, then headed off toward Republic Airport in Farmingdale where he planned to dispose of the body, he told Trooper Capers.
But as soon as he drove onto Sunrise Highway, two state troopers saw his truck, noticed that the rear license plate was missing and tried to get him to stop. He was arrested after a 20-mile chase that lasted about 25 minutes and ended when he crashed into a utility pole in Mineola.

HEADLINE: CREDIT MARKETS;
Long Bond's Yield Falls To Lowest This Month

BYLINE: By Bloomberg Business News 

 BODY:
Interest rates fell yesterday to their lowest levels since March 31 as Treasury prices rose on expectations that the worst of the bond market's three-month rout might be over.
The postponement of a sale of Treasury notes to Thursday from tomorrow also encouraged some traders to buy back securities they sold earlier on a bet that prices would fall before the sale. 
 Yields on the 30-year Treasury bond had risen so much recently that a rally was likely, said Frank Riccardi, who oversees $2.5 billion in fixed-income investments at Society Asset Management Inc.
 
Buying Again
 As a result, Mr. Riccardi has cut his cash position in half in recent days, buying 5-year notes and 30-year bonds. "It felt comfortable to put a little money to work," he said.
In trading yesterday, the Treasury's 30-year bond rose 26/32 of a point, while its yield fell to 7.15 percent, its lowest point since March 31, and down from 7.22 percent on Friday.
After hitting a record low of 5.78 percent in mid-October, the yield on the 30-year bond has surged since the Federal Reserve began raising interest rates on Feb. 4 in an effort to curb future inflation. Last Tuesday, the 30-year bond's yield rose as high as 7.46 percent in intraday trading, its highest point since January 1993.
In when-issued trading yesterday ahead of this week's Treasury auctions, the two-year Treasury note that goes on sale today was quoted at a price to yield 5.63 percent. The new five-year note was being offered at a price to yield 6.49 percent; the five-year note will now be auctioned on Thursday rather than tomorrow, which has been declared a day of mourning for former President Richard M. Nixon.
By late in the day, the discount rate on a one-year bill, also being auctioned on Thursday, was quoted at 4.64 percent.
 
Reassessing the Outlook
 Many traders and investors now say that 30-year Treasury bond yields above 7.4 percent are not warranted with inflation so benign.
Alan McClymonds, head of CS First Boston's bond trading for its own accounts, said customers and other departments at the firm were buying 5-year and 10-year Treasury notes.
"I still think the market can go up a lot higher," Mr. McClymonds said. "The inflation rate that we've seen doesn't support" bond yields as high as they are now, he added.
So far, the evidence indicates that inflation is subdued. Consumer prices rose three-tenths of 1 percent last month and for the 12 months that ended in March the rate was 2.5 percent, below the 2.7 percent recorded for 1993.
Last week's drop in yields caused investors to become interested in buying again, said Ken Sullivan, a trader at First Chicago Capital Markets. "People we have not seen in months are now dipping their toes in," he said.
 
Altering Schedules
 Most American financial markets will be closed tomorrow, the day of Mr. Nixon's funeral, and the Public Securities Association recommended that the Treasury market close.
Treasury Secretary Lloyd Bentsen announced that the $11 billion auction of five-year notes would be postponted until Thursday.
That means the five-year sale will occur after Thursday morning's release of the Commerce Department's estimate of first-quarter economic growth. The report's potential to move the market probably had traders buying when-issued five-year notes yesterday, Mr. McClymonds said.
Anyone betting that the yield on the when-issued five-year note would rise before the auction now has to reconsider since the auction will be held after the Commerce Department release, Mr. McClymonds said.
Following are the results of the Treasury's regular weekly auction of three-month and six-month bills:

HEADLINE: FINANCE BRIEFS

 BODY:

Debt

* Ameritech Capital Funding, a unit of Ameritech Corp., is selling $225 million of floating-rate notes due in 1997 at a price of 100 through Goldman, Sachs & Company. Rated Aa-2 by Moody's Investors Service, AA+ by Standard & Poor's, and AA by Duff & Phelps, the bonds are noncallable and pay interest at the three-month London interbank offered rate. 

* Federal National Mortgage Association, Washington, is selling $500 million of 10-year notes through various securities dealers. The debentures have a 7.65 percent coupon and are priced at 99.75 to yield 7.686 percent, or about 77.5-hundredths of a percentage point more than Treasury securities with a comparable maturity.

* Port of Port Arthur Navigational District, Texas, is selling $34 million of tax-exempt port improvement revenue bonds initially priced to yield a maximum 6.20 percent for a bond due in 2019 through Rauscher Pierce Refsnes. Serial bonds are priced to yield from 4.30 percent in 1996 to 5.10 percent in 2000, 5.60 percent in 2005 and 5.90 percent in 2009. Another long maturity is priced to yield 6.15 percent. Because the bonds are insured by the Ambac Indemnity Corporation they are rated triple-A by Moody's and S.& P.

HEADLINE: Cheap Moon Mission May Offer Glimpse Of Future for NASA

BYLINE: By JOHN NOBLE WILFORD 

DATELINE: ALEXANDRIA , Va.

 BODY:
OUTSIDE the nondescript brick building on a street like many others, life was elementally terrestrial. People hurried in and out of a post office around the corner. A yellow school bus brought children home. Two little boys played in a yard, where laundry dried on the line.
Inside, in a small, darkened room, a few men and women sat at computer consoles and watched scenes from another world. One after another in rapid succession, pictures of the Moon, gray vistas of lifeless plains and yawning craters, appeared on the wall screen. The spacecraft Clementine was reporting home last week, as it has since it went into lunar orbit in February, sending the first close-up pictures of the Moon in more than two decades. 
 It is entirely in character for this modest mission of space exploration to be conducted in such ordinary circumstances, only a few red bricks away from the mundane. It is reminiscent, too, of the scrambling, makeshift early days of space flight before elaborate control rooms surrounded by acres of office buildings and design laboratories came into being to run billion-dollar missions sometimes taking a decade to get off the ground.
In many ways, in concept and execution, in its swaggering assault on business as usual, the $80 million Clementine mission, a joint military-civilian venture, is a glimpse of the past and, perhaps, into the future. Its success is forcing space officials and scientists to temper their ardor for the big and expensive and think seriously about how to maintain a vigorous program of exploration with smaller, simpler and less expensive spacecraft.
From a glass booth off the control room, Lieut. Col. Pedro Rustan of the Air Force, the program manager, looked at the latest lunar pictures with satisfaction. "It's working like a charm, and we were on time, on schedule and on budget," he said.
The 500-pound spacecraft, less than four feet wide and slightly more than six feet long, was launched without fanfare on Jan. 25 and went into lunar orbit on Feb. 19. The last time an American craft orbited the Moon was in December 1972, when the Apollo 17 astronauts walked on the lunar surface. The last Russian mission was in August 1976, when the unmanned Luna 24 landed on the Moon, scooped up rock samples and returned them to Earth.
On Friday, Clementine's miniaturized cameras finished taking mapping pictures of the entire Moon. Traveling around the Moon at its poles, coming as close as 250 miles to the surface, the spacecraft was able to photograph many regions seldom surveyed, and never in such detail. The spacecraft took, recorded and then transmitted more than 5,000 pictures on each five-hour orbit.
In one of the more esthetically appealing pictures, the planets Mercury, Venus and Saturn can be seen as tiny globes in a line across the darkness of space, with the brooding Moon in the foreground.
Upstairs from the control room, Dr. Eugene M. Shoemaker, Clementine's chief scientist and a planetary geologist assigned to the project by the National Aeronautics and Space Administration, pored over the mosaics of pictures of such striking lunar craters as Tycho and Orientale Basin. The cameras, operating in different wavelengths and with an assortment of filters, were revealing sedimentary layers in the crater walls, he said, showing that the crust is not nearly as vertically homogenous as had been thought.
"It's beautiful stuff," said Dr. Shoemaker, who had spent much of his career analyzing earlier photography from the Surveyor and Lunar Orbiter flights, unmanned scouting expeditions that preceded the Apollo landings. "We will be able to identify rock types. Basically, we will re-do the geology of the Moon."
Because one of the mission's scientific objectives is to prospect the mineral content of the Moon, the spacecraft was given the name Clementine, after the miner's darling daughter in the old Gold Rush ballad.
Details of dark, deep craters at the polar regions were photographed for the first time. Several times the spacecraft transmitted special radio signals into the polar crater depths. The signals were deflected and received by antennas on Earth. Analysis of the modified signals is under way to see if they reveal the presence of any ice at the lunar poles, presumably deposited there by comet impacts and sheltered from sunlight.
Dr. Paul Spudis, deputy leader of the science team who is from the Lunar and Planetary Institute in Houston, said that ground-based observations had provided some tantalizing clues of possible polar ice. "If we do confirm the presence of ice," he said earlier in the mission, "it will be a very interesting -- if not major -- discovery."
Next Tuesday may 3, Clementine's rocket is to be fired to depart lunar orbit and head for a rendezvous at the end of August with a small asteroid, 1620 Geographos. On the way, the craft should be able to observe Jupiter as it is being struck by a cluster of cometary fragments in July. If fuel reserves remain high, said Dr. Donald M. Horan, a scientist for the Naval Research Laboratory, which built the craft, Clementine could continue to another asteroid rendezvous in October 1995.
After that, Clementine would be "lost and gone forever," another reason for its name.
But Clementine's influence on future space operations could be more enduring. The mission, Colonel Rustan said, "is demonstrating every day that the cheaper, faster, better approach can pay off when it is applied to a small, well-managed project."
He chose his words to make a point. "Cheaper, faster, better" has become the favorite phrase of Daniel S. Goldin, the NASA Administrator, in admonishing scientists and engineers to come up with missions that cost less and could be flown in less time, or else face a long drought in space science. The embarrassment of having another group go out and set the example, some Government space officials say, is one reason NASA has been cool to Clementine, even while taking a small part in it.
Similarly, the Clinton Administration has been ambivalent toward the program, said Dr. John M. Logsdon, director of the Space Policy Institute at George Washington University. While some science advisers in the White House praise the mission as the way things should be done, he said, others are uncomfortable with the project's roots in the "Star Wars" project begun by President Ronald Reagan.
Clementine does have an unusual pedigree. It was the idea of the Ballistic Missile Defense Organization, the scaled-down successor to the Star Wars organization in the Pentagon. Military engineers wanted to launch a spacecraft for testing new lightweight materials and electronics designed for "brilliant pebbles," the fleet of small space-deployed craft once proposed for intercepting and destroying missiles in flight.
In late 1991, with the end of the cold war and diminished support for such anti-missile research, military officials were happy to declassify the project and have the civilian space agency join in, providing a team of planetary scientists and its deep-space tracking network. The military officials modified the mission to use the Moon and an asteroid, instead of man-made objects, as targets for the sensor tests. "Trying to intersect a tiny asteroid is like trying to hit a missile in midcourse," Colonel Rustan said.
 
Operating on a Shoestring
 Construction was begun early the next year with a shoestring budget of $55 million for the spacecraft, $20 million for the launching rocket, a refurbished Titan 2 missile, and $5 million for mission operations. A comparable mission developed specifically for lunar and asteroid exploration might have cost at least $400 million, space experts estimated.
Costs were kept low by limiting the number of people working on the project to about 55; they designed, built, tested and controlled Clementine. Management was streamlined, officials said, and many time-consuming design reviews were eliminated. A contractor, Allied-Signal Inc., further reduced overhead costs by setting up the low-budget control room here in downtown Alexandria.
Another cost-saving measure was to use the latest miniaturized components, based on the most advanced technologies but acquired off the shelf, often from commercial vendors. The set of cameras -- two star-tracking instruments, a ultraviolet-visible camera, a high-resolution visible camera and two infrared cameras -- weighed no more than 18 pounds. Some of these instruments were less than one-fiftieth the size of those flown as recently as the 1980's.
"We have reduced the size of state-of-the-art technology across the board," Colonel Rustan said.
Whether the Clementine model would work for other projects is debatable. Dr. Logsdon said, "They did it in a way that would be very difficult to duplicate in toto in a large organization like NASA."
But with its dwindling budgets, NASA is being forced to think small and borrow ideas from Clementine. The initial result is a planned series of Discovery spacecraft for projects that cannot exceed three years of development and $150 million in costs -- not Clementine economics but more restrained than recent planetary missions costing $1 billion or much more.
The first Discovery mission, the Near Earth Asteroid Rendezvous, will be conducted by the Applied Physics Laboratory of Johns Hopkins University, with launching scheduled in early 1996. The second mission, Mars Pathfinder, is being developed by the Jet Propulsion Laboratory in Pasadena, Calif., for launching in late 1996.
Eskercq K. Davis, who manages Discovery mission planning at the laboratory, said scientists had been invited to submit proposals this summer for future Discovery-class projects to explore the inner planets, asteroids and comets, interplanetary dust and the Sun. The spirit of innovation, he said, referring to the Jet Propulsion Laboratory, "is kind of like being back where we were at J.P.L. in the old days of Surveyor."
 
Need to Scale Down
 At an international conference on low-cost planetary missions, held two weeks ago in Laurel, Md., scientists seemed to accept the need to scale down their aspirations, agreeing that the $1 billion Cassini mission to Saturn, now being developed, may be the last large program of planetary exploration for a long time.
Even the Clementine managers have some more ideas of their own. Dr. Stewart Nozette, deputy mission manager, is trying to sell Congress on the idea of a second Clementine mission, perhaps to put small roving vehicles the size of shoeboxes on the lunar surface. With characteristic whimsy, Clementine planners are thinking of calling the next mission Alice, for Ralph Kramden's wife in "The Honeymooners" who is always being threatened with a swift departure to the Moon.
Congress has expressed an interest, but so far there is nothing in the budget for Alice.

GRAPHIC: Photos: The full Earth over the north lunar pole, in a composite photograph taken by Clementine on March 13.; The face of the Moon lit by light reflected from the Sun by the Earth. The Sun's corona is visible at the lower right. The dark circular area at the center is the Mare Humorum. The Orientale Basin is at the bottom. (pg. C8) (U.S. Navy)
 
Diagrams: "Mapping the Moon"
 The $80 million Clementine mission, a low-budget military-civilian venture, was designed to test lightweight components and, as a scientific bonus, map the Moon and visit a tiny asteroid, Geographos. In the first American lunar flight since 1972, the spacecraft orbited the Moon, transmitting a million stunning pictures, showing its entire surface in greater detail than before.
 
On to Geographos
 After the success at the Moon, Clementine is set to head for its asteroid rendezvous. The 500-pound craft is to swing back by Earth, using terrestrial gravity to st its four-month course toward Geographos. Scientists expect to get their close-up views of one of the dozens of asteroids that cross Earth's orbit.
 
Mosaic of the Moon
 Clementine took more than 5,000 photographs on each of its five-hour orbits, which were then assembled to give panoramic views, in this case of the Moon's south pole.
 
Science on a Shoestring
 Proving that a space mission could be mounted in only two years and on a modest budget, designers used relatively enexpensive off-the-shelf technologies. The set of miniaturized cameras weighs only 18 pounds. The images were stored in advanced solid-state data recorders for playback to Earth.
 
(Sources: U.S. Navy; Aviation Week and Space Technology)

HEADLINE: Managing the Whiz Kids

 BODY:
The story was perversely entertaining. A 36-year-old techie is accused of outsmarting his eminent Wall Street employer, racking up a $9 million bonus by faking bond trades. Joseph Jett, head of Kidder, Peabody's government bond trades, victimized no customers. He did allegedly burn Kidder's shareholders, although few of them will lose much sleep over their modest losses.
But the accusations followed scary plunges on financial markets and splashy losses by aggressive investment funds, corporations and banks that traded derivatives -- fancy financial instruments whose value "derives" from underlying stocks, bonds or other assets. The series of events raises the troubling question of whether financial whiz kids have gotten eerily out in front of their managers, let alone Federal regulators. 
 Mr. Jett's alleged larceny involved selling "stripped" bonds -- bonds that pay no interest but rise in price as they mature -- for delivery in the future. But before the sale date would arrive, he would roll the contract further into the future. Because of a flaw in Kidder's accounting system, the firm would record a profit even though the perpetual series of trades put no money into Kidder's coffers. But the appearance of profits qualified Mr. Jett for stupendous bonuses. Kidder's managers were not savvy enough to keep up with his shenanigans.
A similar issue arose with corporations that bought derivatives designed to protect their profits from ups and downs in bond and stock markets. But several clients of Bankers Trust bought derivatives that, instead of protecting them, triggered huge losses when interest rates went up. Two of these companies plead ignorance of the risk they undertook.
Derivatives are also used by speculators. That becomes troublesome if banks are the ones speculating because derivatives can quickly generate huge losses of government-insured deposits that might have to be picked up by taxpayers.
Investors who trade derivatives to manage risk or speculate do so at their own peril. There is nonetheless good reason for Congress and Federal regulators to scrutinize their use by banks so that the Mr. Jetts of this world will not threaten unwitting taxpayers.

HEADLINE: Rare Butterfly Consigned to Extinction

BYLINE: By CAROL KAESUK YOON 

 BODY:
SCIENTISTS say the country's most recently discovered species of butterfly is about to become extinct. But rather than taking what they call "heroic measures" to save it, they are advocating a more hands-off policy that they predict will soon have biologists counting one less species among North America's butterflies.
Like concerned family members gathered around a loved one's death bed, the biologists who studied this small alpine insect, the Uncompahgre fritillary butterfly, have begun the mourning process, already sadly shaking their heads. They say there is no clear way to save the butterfly, so rather than spending precious conservation dollars to try to resuscitate the species, they suggest letting it go. 
 "This is probably a fairly heretical stance to some people," said Dr. Hugh Britten, a conservation biologist at the Nevada Biodiversity Research Center in Reno who is an author of a paper about the species. "It's not very easy for me to say I am presiding over the extinction of this species. But in the world of science, so what? Your personal feelings are not relevant and they shouldn't be."
While Dr. Britten and his co-authors, Dr. Peter Brussard, president of the Society for Conservation Biology, and Dr. Dennis Murphy, a conservation biologist at Stanford University, recommend continued monitoring and guarding of the survivors, their ominously titled study, "The Pending Extinction of the Uncompahgre Fritillary Butterfly," makes clear their vision of the future.
"Remember that the whole funding for endangered species is one pie," Dr. Britten said. "We're saying there's not much we can do that's sure to work and we ought to leave those funds in there to be applied somewhere else where they can work."
Among conservationists, the recommendation on this butterfly, which measures barely an inch across its wings, has already elicited everything from hearty approval to anger. Some say the researchers are making exactly the kind of hard decision they need to make and have been promising to make, forgoing expensive last-ditch efforts to conserve doomed single species.
"I think they're right on target with no heroic measures," said Dr. Paul R. Ehrlich, the Bing Professor of Population Studies and president of the Center for Conservation Biology at Stanford. "I can't get excited about captive breeding of an alpine arctic ecosystem species that may go extinct on its own. It'd be much better to work much harder to save the species of the Northwest -- to save whole huge habitats."
But others warn that the recommendation may be typical of many examples to come in which biologists subscribe to a triage mentality, too quickly writing off one species in favor of others in the name of economic prudence.
"Personally, I find the triage idea ethically repugnant," said Dr. Reed Noss, editor of Conservation Biology, the journal in which the butterfly report was published. "We too readily assign species to the category of hopeless. It's too easy for political reasons to say we can't do anything about that species, that it would put too much of a burden on our economy. I probably wouldn't have concluded the same thing."
Dr. Jeffrey Glassberg, president of the North American Butterfly Association, which has its headquarters in Morristown, N.J., suggested that the constraints of the triage mentality may in some ways be self-imposed. "I think these are self-fulfilling prophecies: 'I have limited resources and we're not going to do anything.' I don't know that I'd want to give up so easily."
 
Always a Rare Sight
 Named after the site in the San Juan Mountains of Colorado where the species was discovered in 1978, the Uncompahgre (pronounced un-kum-PAH-gray) fritillary -- an unremarkable-looking black and brown mottled butterfly -- has always been rare, often numbering in the hundreds at each of its two known locations. But more recently, the species, seemed in precipitous decline, virtually disappearing from Uncompahgre Peak and declining at its other known site, Red Cloud Peak.
While acknowledging that the exact reasons for the populations' year-to-year ups and downs remain mysterious, the researchers blame the general decline of the species on grazing that once was allowed in the butterfly's alpine meadows, on overzealous collecting by those anxious to have this newest and very rare species, and -- most important -- on a warming climate.
Thought to be a relictual arctic species left behind on Colorado's mountaintops when the last glaciers retreated some 10,000 years ago, the species, Boloria acrocnema, has been trapped in what these scientists say has been more than a decade of uncomfortably warming weather.
"As far as management implications go, there's essentially nothing we can do about it," said Dr. Britten, who is a member of a team appointed by the Government to write a recovery plan for this endangered species. "When you turn the heat up, it causes problems and the butterfly has to move upslope, and there is no more upslope. It's being ecologically squeezed off the top."
All the experts seem to agree that if the species is naturally doomed, then the only responsible action is to let it go and allocate limited resources elsewhere. The problem comes in deciding when to say when.
Many expressed concern that the researchers may have been a bit too hasty in tolling the death knell for this species. Knowing the butterfly is indeed doomed, they say, is not as simple as it might appear to be. Researchers point out that just last month in Southern California, the Palos Verdes blue butterfly, long presumed extinct, turned up very much alive.
Amy Seidl, a graduate student at Colorado State University who is the only researcher now studying the Uncompahgre fritillary, said her recent work indicated that the situation was not as dismal as the researchers suggested. Since the downward trend observed until 1991, Ms. Seidl said she had witnessed a stabilization and even an increase in the butterfly's numbers.
The butterfly's population numbered nearly 2,000 last year at Uncompahgre Peak, according to Ms. Seidl's study, up from estimates of roughly zero in 1990 and 1991. She also estimated a population of 1,300 at Red Cloud Peak, up from 400 in 1990 and 400 to 1,000 in 1991. These are considerable increases for a butterfly thought to be on its way out.
Given the butterfly's apparent comeback, some researchers suggested that attempts at the heroic measures of captive breeding and introduction to additional mountain meadows were in order.
"Nobody can see into the future that far," Dr. Larry Gall, a curatorial affiliate at the Peabody Museum at Yale University, said of the predictions of pending extinction. "I think it would be useful for them to attempt an introduction."
Dr. Gall and Dr. Felix Sperling of the University of Ottawa discovered the Uncompahgre fritillary, the only new butterfly species found in the United States in the last three decades.
Dr. Britten and his colleagues, however, said they were not hopeful about captive breeding or introductions of wild butterflies to new areas, citing poor success rates in the past.
Dr. Gall responded: "Sometimes it's awful but sometimes it's good. It's not unreasonable to try."
 
Annual Cost Put at $5,000
 Despite arguments against the costliness of such ventures, Terry Ireland, a Fish and Wildlife Department biologist who is a member of the butterfly recovery team, said that for this species, even the most heroic of measures -- captive breeding -- is estimated to cost no more than $5,000 a year. According to a Fish and Wildlife Survey, in 1991 Federal and state agencies spent an estimated $177 million on the recovery of federally listed endangered species alone.
But so far, the recovery plan closely follows the hands-off recommendations of the researchers, a proposal of simple monitoring of the population, modest attempts to search for new populations and studies to learn more about the biology of the insect.
Even if funds were available and researchers willing, heroic measures can have their risks. Dr. Britten, well aware of Ms. Seidl's findings of increased numbers, remains wary of disrupting the butterflies with removals for breeding or introduction elsewhere. He cautioned that insect populations often fluctuate greatly, with apparently healthy populations quickly dropping back to low numbers for no apparent reason. The problem is that when butterfly numbers drop down now and again, bad weather or other harsh conditions can keep the vulnerable populations from bouncing back.
While the more pessimistic researchers argue that such fluctuations make it easy for the Uncompahgre fritillary to be permanently knocked out, others counter that it also makes it more difficult to predict extinction.
Adding more uncertainty to the status of the butterfly is the specter of yet undiscovered colonies. Ms. Seidl is optimistic that there are still unknown populations of the butterfly. As part of her continuing study, she plans to scale the wet, northeast slopes of the San Juans, the sort the butterfly would most likely favor.
 
Limited Sighting Period
 Even Dr. Britten, who has made numerous searches, admits it is impossible to be sure whether or not there are hidden colonies in the wilderness of the San Juans. It is particularly difficult because the butterflies are visible and in flight for only about three weeks a year, in July.
"All this means we can't be very confident that we've looked everywhere at the right times," he said. "There have been reports of additional colonies by one other lepidopterist, who is refusing to reveal where they are. You get reports like that and you think, maybe this person's right."
Amid the difficulty of assessing the health status of an endangered species, conservationists continue to struggle with making what may be life-or-death decisions for the animals and plants they study.
Ann Swengel, international co-editor for the annual July 4 butterfly count run by the Xerces Society and the North American Butterfly Association, said: "When is it O.K. not to try to save a species? I think conservationists are groping with this now.
"In the end, it's an opinion, a judgment call just like everything in conservation. You never know what would have happened if you'd done something else."

HEADLINE: COMPANY NEWS;
Spinoff and a Sale Loom in Tractor Business

BYLINE: By KATHRYN JONES 

 BODY:
J. I. Case and Massey Ferguson -- two well-known names in the farm and construction equipment business -- may soon be split from their parent companies.
Tenneco Inc. is expected to end months and even years of speculation about a possible spinoff of its Case unit at a meeting today with securities analysts in New York. Some analysts said that at least a partial spinoff would make sense since Case was strong enough financially to stand alone. 
 Meanwhile, the Varity Corporation of Buffalo said yesterday that it was in the "latter stages" of negotiations for the sale of its Massey Ferguson business to the Agco Corporation. A person close to the negotiations said that an agreement to buy Massey Ferguson could come as early as tomorrow.
The farm and construction equipment industry is emerging from several years of a global recession, heavy losses and deep work force cuts. Companies like Case and Massey Ferguson, which incurred heavy losses and went through several revampings, have returned to profitability.
"The market is very, very strong right now," said Eli Lustgarten, an analyst at Paine Webber Inc. "We are clearly looking at cyclical improvement."
 
Shares in Rise
 Shares of Tenneco surged $1.875 yesterday, to $52.25, on the New York Stock Exchange. Varity shares rose $1.25 to close at $39.50, while Agco shares jumped $2.125 to close at $35.25 on the Big Board.
Tenneco executives declined to comment yesterday on the speculation that Case would be spun off.
Dana Mead, the recently elected chief executive of Tenneco, is expected to brief analysts today on the company's plans for Case and to release first-quarter earnings that will show big improvements at Tenneco's largest subsidiary.
If Tenneco does plan a divestiture, analysts said that the company would likely retain a stake or even a controlling interest in Case, which is based in Racine, Wis., and is the nation's second-largest farm machinery manufacturer after Deere & Company.
Analysts said they expected Tenneco to report significantly higher net income in the quarter, with most of the increase as a result of improving results at Case.
Tenneco, based in Houston, earned $74 million, or 46 cents a share, in the first quarter last year. Most analysts have estimated that first quarter earnings will range from 60 cents to 65 cents a share.
"Case is the reason for the real strong comparison," said David Fleisher, an analyst at Goldman, Sachs & Company.
Case's 1993 revenues of $3.75 billion accounted for more than one-fourth of Tenneco's total revenues last year. Tenneco's other businesses are natural gas, shipbuilding, chemicals, packaging and auto parts manufacturing.
Massey Ferguson would be the latest deal for Agco, which is based in Atlanta and has been expanding in the farm equipment business in recent years through acquisitions. In November 1992, it paid $110 million to buy the North American rights to distribute Massey Ferguson equipment and acquire 50 percent of its Agricredit financing business. In February, it acquired the rest of Agricredit for $23 million and is said to be offering $330 million to acquire the rest of Massey Ferguson it does not already own.

HEADLINE: COMPANY NEWS;
In Surprise, Sales Chief Quits Digital After Loss

BYLINE: By GLENN RIFKIN, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: MAYNARD, Mass., April 25

 BODY:
Edward E. Lucente resigned today as senior vice president of worldwide sales and marketing at the Digital Equipment Corporation. The move, which surprised industry analysts, came a year after Mr. Lucente, 54, joined the beleaguered company.
Mr. Lucente's resignation came within days of Digital's announcement of a $183 million loss for the third fiscal quarter, a drop that surprised both Digital executives and financial analysts in its severity and caused the company's stock to plunge. Analysts said today that they believed the loss had led to Mr. Lucente's departure. 
 Enrico Pesatori, 53, vice president and general manager of Digital's personal computer business unit, was named to take over Mr. Lucente's responsibilities,
"Somebody had to take the fall and it's probably better that it was Lucente than" Robert B. Palmer, Digital's president and chief executive, said George Colony, a consultant at Forrester Research in Cambridge, Mass.
Shares of Digital fell 12.5 cents today, to $19.25, on the New York Stock Exchange.
Mr. Palmer sent shareholders a letter today saying that Digital would would now enter the second phase of its turnaround, a period that he expected to last 24 months.
In an interview last week, Mr. Palmer acknowledged that Digital was experiencing severe problems in getting accurate reports from its sales organization on quarterly results. He said he believed Digital had been on track to meet analysts' expectations for the third quarter as late as the Easter weekend.
Digital's board met on Thursday and apparently expressed confidence in the plans of Mr. Palmer to continue his strategy for turning around the computer maker.
David Wu, an analyst with S. G. Warburg & Company, said he was concerned about the two more years needed for Digital's turnaround. "That is way too long for investors," Mr. Wu said. "I'm amazed the board would allow him to say such a thing after he's been on the job 18 months. If he was brand new, two years is reasonable. But after 18 months, he is asking too much."
Although Digital has seen fast growth in personal computers, most of its business remains in larger systems.
Mr. Lucente will become executive-in-residence at the Carnegie-Mellon Graduate School of Industrial Administration in Pittsburgh, the company said in a statement.
Mr. Palmer declined today to comment on the executive changes. Mr. Lucente is on vacation, a Digital spokesman said.
Mr. Lucente had joined Digital after a 31-year career in sales and marketing at I.B.M. He joined Digital from Northern Telecom, after a short stint there as executive vice president.
His arrival at Digital was heralded by analysts as what Digital needed to bolster its marketing efforts. He moved quickly to revamp the company's field operations, including changing Digital's compensation plan from a salaried to an incentive-based one. But analysts and Digital executives said Mr. Lucente's aggressive manner often clashed with the company's culture.

GRAPHIC: Photo: Enrico Pesatori, a vice president at the Digital Equipment Corporation, who was named to assume Edward E. Lucente's responsibilities as vice president of worldwide sales and marketing. (Associated Press)

HEADLINE: BUSINESS TECHNOLOGY;
U.S. to Aid Industry In Computer Battle With the Japanese

BYLINE: By KEITH BRADSHER, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, April 26

 BODY:
In the biggest Government help for private industry since an effort to assist computer chip makers began seven years ago, the White House has approved a proposal to spend up to $1 billion on American companies competing with Japan in making advanced flat-panel computer display screens.
The Defense Department has scheduled a news conference for Thursday to discuss flat-panel displays, and the initiative is expected to be announced then. Administration officials began notifying Congressional aides today about their intentions. 
 The thin computer screens, which are mainly found in laptop computers and other portable devices, are increasingly used in military equipment acquired by the Pentagon. A handful of tiny American companies currently hold less than 3 percent of the world market in such screens, while several big Japanese corporations, led by the Sharp Corporation, control the rest and have been reluctant to sell to the Pentagon. The Administration's plan calls for heavy Defense Department spending to build up domestic suppliers instead.
The strategy to help develop the American industry goes far beyond the Government-industry consortium known as Sematech, which was set up in 1987 to bolster the computer chip industry. Sematech limits itself to developing and sharing production techniques but does not sell chips. For flat-panel displays, the White House wants not only to help with research and development, but with the construction of commercial factories and even marketing.
The proposal would provide matching Federal grants to companies that invest in flat-panel display research and production. The plan builds on current research efforts paid for by the Departments of Defense and Energy, for which Congress has already authorized $100 million. Further spending will be included in future Administration budgets that are subject to Congressional approval.
The plan, which is summarized in an interagency report and described today by officials who reviewed it, represents an effort to back an industry that seems to be particularly promising, and to short-circuit market forces by subsidizing it. This is an approach that Japan has followed, often -- though not always -- with considerable success in developing important industries. Whether the United States should do more of this has been a matter of much debate.
The Administration justifies pursuing such an approach -- which some call industrial policy -- as necessary for the nation's economic security. If the program to help the flat-panel display industry is a success, similar Defense Department spending programs may be undertaken to increase the nation's self-reliance in other industries, the report said, according to Government officials who insisted on anonymity.
Computer display screens were chosen for the experiment because they are critical to many high-technology weapon systems and because the lack of a display screen industry could weaken the nation's telecommunications and computer industries, for which the Defense Department feels protective.
 
Help to Build Factories
 The feature of the plan most likely to prompt debate is its call for the Government to pay part of the cost for companies to build four giant factories that would supply a sixth of the $4 billion world market for flat-panel displays. Some Congressional Republicans have objected to commercial research subsidies as unjustifiable interference in the marketplace.
The plan calls for the Defense Department to contribute $50 million in already authorized general research money for the immediate construction of a high-capacity pilot plant. The Defense and Energy Departments would also jointly spend $450 million to subsidize research and development over the next five years. The Pentagon is already subsidizing the construction of a low-capacity pilot plant in Troy, Mich. Up to $500 million more would be spent as Federal matching funds for corporate construction of up to four giant factories.
The strategy is at odds with efforts by a succession of Administrations to seek tighter international limits on such commercial subsidies, particularly European subsidies for commercial aircraft production. Mickey Kantor, the United States trade representative, signed a new global trade pact less than two weeks ago that put some new restrictions on subsidies, although he agreed to many fewer restrictions than the Bush Administration had sought.
 
Planned as Research Grants
 But the new restrictions do not cover matching government grants to companies for research and development. The assistance to the giant new factories is planned as research grants for the development of the equipment within -- an interpretation of global trade rules that could be challenged by foreign trading partners before an international arbitration panel in Geneva.
The Council of Economic Advisers and the National Economic Council, which developed the idea, have endorsed it, as has the Defense Department.
Big American corporations like the AT&T Corporation, Motorola and Xerox have all publicly expressed interest in the last year in entering the flat-panel display market, but not one has yet taken the risk of building a full-size production factory, which would cost about $400 million.
The White House also envisions an interagency program, including the Justice and Defense Departments, to keep track of foreign computer display companies that may try to squeeze out the small American computer companies like OIS Optical Imaging Systems Inc., which is building the pilot plant in Michigan.
Another part of the plan calls for the Office of the United States Trade Representative to crack open the computer display screen market in Japan, which imports virtually no screens, and for the Commerce Department to help American companies market their screens overseas.
The report also calls for the National Economic Council to name an official to coordinate all Government computer display screen purchases.

HEADLINE: THE SOUTH AFRICAN VOTE: EXPATRIATES;
In New York, It's a Dream Come True

BYLINE: By MICHEL MARRIOTT 

 BODY:
Far from the squalor of her native Soweto, history caught up with Moli Ntuli yesterday as she nervously inched toward a tent pitched on the north lawn of the United Nations Plaza in Manhattan. Once inside, the 49-year-old Ms. Ntuli did something she had only been permited to do in her waking dreams.
She voted. 
 "I was shaking so much I didn't believe this was me," said Ms. Ntuli, a high school math teacher, seconds after casting her absentee ballot. "I hope they can read my X."
As she spoke, one of her students from the Sarah J. Hale High School, in Cobble Hill, Brooklyn, swept into her arms, overcome with emotion that "Miss Ntuli finally got to vote."
After years of activism, weeks of detention and then self-imposed exile in New York for the last 20 years, Ms. Ntuli joined thousands of other South Africans living, working or traveling in the United States who wanted to exercise their franchise.
A white South African yacht team made its way to the polls at the United Nations, as well as a group of South African Airways flight attendants. There were also white South Africans who popped out from luxury sedans to vote while their drivers waited.
For many others, like Ms. Ntuli, their participation afforded them for the first time an equal voice in their country's affairs.
Some got up as early as 4 A.M. to drive or take trains from southern New Jersey and Connecticut to vote in New York, one of some two dozen polling places in the United States.
 
'Bring Peace to Africa!'
 In Chicago, where as many as 1,000 voters were expected to cast ballots, crowds chanted "Vive democracy!" and "Bring peace to Africa!" Others danced the traditional black toitoi, often seen at protest marches, and sang "Nkosi Sikelele iAfrika," or "God Save Africa," their homeland's equivalent to "We Shall Overcome."
Maanda Mulaudzi, aged 35, of Makuya, in northern Transvaal, traveled to Chicago from Minneapolis, where he is studying history at the University of Minnesota. A supporter of the African National Congress, he said he had voted for the future. "For me to vote here is the first major step toward the democratization of our country," he said.
Five thousand absentee ballots were printed for New York, said V. R. W. Steward, the chief South African representative at the United Nations, who briefly visited the polling place, which opened at 7:40 A.M., about 40 minutes late. Voters could also cast ballots at the South African Consul General's office, on East 38th Street.
 
Voting in Surges
 Throughout the day voters came in surges, sometimes creating up to an hour's wait at the consul's office, said the Consul General, Joe Stauch.
"There is an excitement," said Sthembisco Kume, a black South African and an Independent Election Commission observer at the United Nations polling place. "I had to keep pinching myself to remind me that this is actually happening."
Mr. Kume, 31, said he had never been certain that he would live to see non-white South Africans vote. "Now," he said with a broad smile, "I feel like I'm 18."
Diana Scott, a white South African who had worked for the African National Congress, said taking part in the vote was "one of the best days in my life."
"It is such a fulfillment of so much work for a long time to get rid of apartheid," she said, adding that when she left South Africa nine years ago, she believed it was "wishful thinking" that such an election could take place at this time.
Despite the festival bustle that permeated the polling places, a sense of reverence and awe stirred many of the voters, black and white.
But for many South Africans -- even from afar -- the elections have not calmed all fears.
A visitor to New York from suburban Johannesburg, Amanda Ind, said she began stocking up on canned goods as election day neared. "It was just something people started doing," said Ms. Ind, 56, who is visiting her daughter, Frances Balachandran.
While cautiously optimistic about the future, Ms. Ind said she was concerned about the expectations of apartheid's most wounded victims, the potentially volatile "lost generation" of young blacks who left their schools to press for changes in their country.
She said that if they pressed for a radical redistribution of South Africa's wealth, "white South Africans can't possibly finance what's needed."
"The only hope is for the economy to grow," she said.
 
Daughter More Hopful
 Her daughter was more hopeful, pinning South Africa's hopes clearly on democracy. She noted that at the polling place, dozens of South Africans, black and white and of mixed race, gathered easily in the shadow of the huge statue of St. George slaying a dragon that is made of pieces of a ballistic missile. Many chatted and socializing without incident.
There was little talk about party politics. And bowing to election rules, there were few images and flags representing the factions that have been tearing at South Africa's soul in recent months.
"This is history, fantastic history," Ms. Balachandran said as she looked at groups of blacks and whites waiting to vote. "It has to work. It has to work."
Hugh Masekela, the South African musician, milled through the crowd at the United Nations after he voted. Looking around, he said, "We're facing undoing 350 years of impoverishment and 50 years of concentrated impoverishment, separation and manipulated conflict.
"It's going to take a miracle to undo all that."

GRAPHIC: Photo: One of the South African expatriates who cast their votes at the United Nations, Thalita Komane wept with emotion yesterday. The native of Soweto had driven down from Albany in the morning. (Don Hogan Charles/The New York Times)

HEADLINE: At Least 4 Die In Tornadoes Near Dallas

BYLINE: By SAM HOWE VERHOVEK, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: LANCASTER, Tex., April 26

 BODY:
A storm system that lashed through the central United States late Monday and early today left its most severe trail of destruction here, with three people dead and the brick buildings and majestic live oaks of the Reconstruction-era town square blown to ruins. A fourth person in a nearby town died of a heart attack as he sat out the tornado in his storm cellar.
The system caused tornadoes in two other states, Nebraska and Oklahoma, and brought blizzard conditions and more than a foot of snow to much of the northern Plains, the National Weather Service reported. 
 Tornadoes that touched down in this Dallas suburb and in the neighboring town of DeSoto destroyed at least 525 homes and caused serious damage to hundreds of others, local officials said.
Parts of both towns were transformed today into twisted piles of collapsed homes, broken glass, fallen trees and dangling telephone wires. Swing sets were impaled on fences and in the Lancaster town square, a bank that was robbed in the 1930's by Bonnie Parker and Clyde Barrow, the legendary bank robbers, collapsed into a heap of brick and mortar dust.
 
Sirens' Crucial Warning
 But the police said that siren warnings had given townspeople at least a dozen crucial minutes Monday night to dive for cover, and helped to avert a greater death toll.
"We had five kids, four adults and a very pregnant cat in the bathroom tub," said Gail Peacock, a DeSoto resident who had been entertaining her daughter's family before the tornado struck.
Mrs. Peacock's roof collapsed, but all the people inside survived after recalling that emergency-preparation officials say an interior bathroom -- and even better, the tub inside it -- often proves to be the safest place to wait out a tornado in a house that does not have a basement or underground shelter.
Early this evening, rescue workers were still searching through piles of rubble in the downtown area of Lancaster, about 12 miles south of Dallas, and in a neighborhood just to the west that also suffered severe damage. But even as some of the workers said that there were reports of people still unaccounted for, Lancaster's Police Chief, Mac McGuire, indicated that he believed the death toll would probably hold. The names of the dead, including the fourth victim, in Mesquite, 15 miles from here, were not immediately released.
"We've searched as well as we believe it's humanly possible," Chief McGuire said. "We don't think there are any more bodies in there. But it's possible -- in some places, the piles, the damage, it's just so deep."
In Lancaster, 18 people were treated and released from a local hospital, with two airlifted to Dallas with serious injuries, said Chief McGuire. In DeSoto, a community already economically hard hit when Congress cut off funds last year for the superconducting supercollider, a major physics project, 75 homes were left uninhabitable and seven people were injured, said Police Officer Gene Yates.
Another line of thunderstorms descended on north Texas late this afternoon, dumping baseball-size hailstones in some places. A tornado briefly touched down in Gainesville, near the Oklahoma border, destroying at least seven mobile homes, but there were no reports of serious injuries.
Yet another tore through Talihina, Okla., near the Arkansas state line, destroying about 25 homes and injuring six people. And one in Central City, Neb., ripped the roofs off several buildings.
Tornadoes are most common in the spring months and are most likely to occur in a broad swath of the country's midsection, commonly known as "Tornado Alley," running from Texas to the upper Midwest. But they can happen anywhere -- and the most deadly this year occurred late last month in Alabama and Georgia, killing 40 people.
 
1994 Death Toll
 To date, there have been 203 tornadoes in 1994, leaving 54 people dead, said Tim Henderson, deputy director of the National Severe Storms Forecast Center in Kansas City, Mo. "About 5 percent of the tornadoes do about 95 percent of the death and destruction," Mr. Henderson said.
Here in Lancaster, as is often the case with tornadoes, the trail of damage left behind seemed random, with parts of the town demolished and other parts unscathed. The damage occurred in an arc roughly a half-mile wide and about five miles long.
"It was just like a freight train roaring through," said Charlie Hathaway, a supervisor at a local food-processing plant.
In the town square, much of which had been restored as part of a plan to attract tourists, signs for quaint shops like "The Lovin' Oven" (a bakery) and "Quilter's Corner" lay still on the ground. A huge, old-fashioned clock was stopped at 9:39 P.M., the time that the twister knocked out electricity.
In the aftermath, there were indications of both the despair and the determination that natural catastrophes can engender.
"I just have no idea where to go from here," said Janet McCasland, the owner of the Coach House, a local restaurant, as she picked pieces of silverware from the litter that had been her place of business. "I don't know whether we can ever restore this place," she added, breaking into tears.
But just then the tenant upstairs, Rick Eilers, who ran a photography studio, came in and put his arms around her. They embraced in silence for several seconds, then Mr. Eilers said: "We're going to pull together and build it all over again."
Elizabeth Simpson the director of the city's Main Street Program, which oversaw the restoration, corrected herself in midsentence when she referred to the effort in the past tense. "We're still active, very much so," she said. "

HEADLINE: BUSINESS DIGEST

 BODY:

Business Technology
The Administration approved a proposal to spend up to $1 billion to help American companies compete with Japan in making advanced flat-panel computer display screens, a move that is the biggest Government aid to private industry since an effort to assist computer chip makers began seven years ago. [Page A1.] 
 A young computer company has continued to grow in Armenia, once the Silicon Valley of the former Soviet Union, despite a weakened economy. [D5.]
 
International
Japan's powerful bureaucracy is struggling with the country's political leaders about the stimulus needed to lift Japan out of its two-year-old recession. [D1.]

The Economy
The United States, Mexico and Canada formed an unusual multibillion-dollar fund to stabilize Mexico's currency and protect it from further onslaughts by global speculators. [D1.]
Moderating inflation in medical and other benefit costs helped hold the rise in overall worker compensation to seven-tenths of 1 percent in the first three months of the year, the smallest quarterly advance since 1986, the Labor Department reported. [D1.]
 The Government must insure equal access to technology for the rich and poor, Administration officials and a growing number of educators and lawmakers say. [B6.] Some experimental programs in schools, including one in Harlem, show that technology can put students of different backgrounds on a more equal footing. [B6.]

HEADLINE: Wage Increases Are Small but Confidence Jumps

BYLINE: By ROBERT D. HERSHEY Jr., Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, April 26

 BODY:
American workers are obtaining less in pay and benefit increases from employers these days, but they seem ever more optimistic, two surveys indicated today.
Moderating inflation in medical and other benefit costs helped hold the rise in overall worker compensation to seven-tenths of 1 percent in the first three months of the year, the smallest quarterly advance since 1986, the Labor Department reported today in its employment cost index. 
 And the Conference Board, a New York research group, said its index of consumer confidence jumped in April to the highest level in nearly four years, based mainly on an upbeat assessment of current economic and business conditions.
 
Smallest on Record
 The surprisingly mild increase in the employment cost index left this broad gauge of inflation 3.2 percent higher than its level a year earlier, the smallest rise since the Government began compiling the index in 1982.
Bond prices rallied on the news, then lost their gains when the Conference Board announced the jump in consumer confidence. But bonds recovered, pushing interest rates lower for the day. [Page D14.]
Although wages are generally considered to lag behind inflation, rather than to precede it, analysts said the results confirmed a belief that price pressures remained subdued.
"Businesses have got their costs under control and won't be under a great deal of pressure to push through price increases," said Richard S. Peterson, chief economist for Continental Bank of Chicago. Those that try, he noted, might not be able to overcome strong foreign competition.
Yet the relatively benign reading on wages and benefits is not expected to dissuade the Federal Reserve from further increases in short-term interest rates. The central bank is thought likely to lift rates again in mid-May in a drive against any possible quickening of inflation.
Wages and salaries, today's report showed, rose seven-tenths of 1 percent in the quarter and 2.9 percent for the latest year, barely above the 2.5 percent rise in consumer prices for the 12 months through March.
But consumers seemed to ignore pinched incomes, with the Conference Board's survey showing that overall confidence surged 5 points in April, to 91.7, the highest level since July 1990 and far above the 67.6 that prevailed in April 1993. The index is based on 100, the 1985 average.
Seven of the nine geographical regions posted gains, the exceptions being New England -- down sharply to 46.4, from 62.8 in March -- and the Pacific states, where the decline was negligible.
"Consumer confidence readings continue to be optimistic, running at levels which have historically signaled a strong economy," said Fabian Linden, executive director of the Consumer Research Center at the Conference Board, a corporate-sponsored group. The survey, carried out by National Family Opinion Inc., polls 5,000 households monthly.
Most of the April increase reflected improvement in consumers' estimates of the current situation, which rose to 85.8 from 77.9. The expectations component moved up to 95.6 from 92.6.
The employment cost index, up seven-tenths of 1 percent in the first quarter, has shown a steady easing in its rate of increase since the final three months of 1992, when the index rose 1 percent.
Most of the slackening has involved benefit costs, up nine-tenths of 1 percent in the first three months of the year and 4.1 percent during the latest 12 months, the lowest 12-month advance since a rise of 3.7 percent in the year that ended in December 1987. A year ago, benefit costs showed a year-over-year increase of 5.4 percent.
 
Wages and Salaries Up 0.7%
 Wages and salaries, which account for 72 percent of compensation, rose seven-tenths of 1 percent in the first quarter and 2.9 percent over the last year, the Labor Department report also showed.
Counting only workers in private industry, wages and salaries rose five-tenths of 1 percent in the latest quarter compared with an eight-tenths of 1 percent rise in the final months of 1993. Benefit costs rose nine-tenths of 1 percent, compared with 1.1 percent.
Over the latest 12 months, compensation cost increases were a bit higher in service industries than goods-producing industries and higher for white-collar occupations than for blue-collar and service occupations.
Gains among white-collar workers ranged from 3.3 percent for executive, administrative and managerial workers to 3.8 percent for sales personnel. Among blue-collar people, the gains ranged from 2.9 percent for handlers, equipment cleaners, helpers and laborers to 4 percent for those in transportation and material-moving occupations.
Union workers did better over the year than nonunion workers, 3.5 percent to 3.3 percent, but this pattern did not hold for goods-producing industries, where both categories showed the same 3.2 percent rise.

HEADLINE: Petitions Seek California Vote on Canada-Style Health Plan

BYLINE: By SETH MYDANS, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: LOS ANGELES, April 26

 BODY:
Leading a nationwide push for state-run health insurance systems, a coalition of consumer, labor and doctors' groups submitted petitions today that would allow California citizens to vote this fall for a Canadian-style "single payer" plan.
Cardboard boxes containing more than a million signatures collected by 10,000 volunteers were being filed with the voter registrar in each county today, organizers said, well over the 677,000 needed to put the issue on the Nov. 8 ballot. County voter registrars are required to verify the signatures before the measure may be put on the ballot. 
 The plan, which would remove private insurance companies from health coverage, would be financed mostly by taxes and would guarantee care to the state's 31 million residents. Similar plans, in which the government alone pays health care costs, are being considered in about 30 states, including New York, New Jersey and Connecticut, though no state has adopted one. California, home to one in eight Americans, would become the first state to have placed a proposal on the ballot through a petition drive.
 
Opposition From Insurers
 "Today was all about insuring a chance at voting for and winning single-payer health care reform in this state and pushing single-payer more into the center of the national debate as an option," said Martha Kowalski, organizing director for Californians for Health Security, the group that organized the petition drive.
Insurance companies are leading the opposition to California's plan, arguing that it would be expensive and would place the issue of personal health in the hands of a government bureaucracy, with all its intrusiveness and inefficiencies.
In addition, there are doubts about the effectiveness of a law put together through the voter-initiative process rather than the rigors of legislative debate. For example, an overhaul of the auto insurance system, approved by voters in 1988, has been seriously undercut by court challenges and industry opposition.
Proposing instead to revamp the current public-private system, Richard Coorsh, a spokesman for the Health Insurance Association of America, a trade group, said: "The notion of throwing up the hands and saying, 'Let the government do it' is fairly easy to understand. But the ramifications of turning over a significant portion of the United States economy is likely to subject consumers to higher costs and lower quality health care."
In San Francisco on Monday, Hillary Rodham Clinton criticized a Canadian-style system, saying: "We believe there must be a private insurance market, a mixed system between public and private financing and delivery. We like that competition."
President Clinton's health care plan would give employers primary responsibility for paying for health insurance, with workers' paying a smaller portion and special subsidies for small businesses with low wages. But his plan also offers states the option of adopting their own plans, including single-payer systems.
 
$105 Billion Annual Cost
 The California plan, estimated to cost $105 billion a year when fully in place in 1996, would create an elected health care commissioner who would negotiate fees for a broad range of services, including long-term care, mental health services and prescription drugs. It would allow consumers to choose their doctors, hospitals or health management organizations and would replace Medi-Cal and Medicare as well as private insurance systems.
The plan would be financed by a business payroll tax, an income tax surcharge of 2.5 percent to 5 percent, a $1-a-pack cigarette tax and existing government spending, sponsors said. They maintain that the plan would generally cost individuals and businesses the same amount that they now pay for health insurance.
"This sets up a system that is better than Canada's and has 40 percent more funding," said Glen Schneider, campaign chairman for Californians for Health Security.
But Mr. Coorsh countered, "It makes little sense to borrow upon the world's second most expensive health care system to improve our own." He said Canada's problems included steadily rising costs, a reduction in services and long waiting times for urgent care and surgery.
Mr. Schneider said the California plan would free an estimated $10 billion a year for health services by cutting out "the enormous bureaucracy and waste of the California insurance industry." It would extend health coverage to six million legal state residents who do not now have health insurance, but would not cover undocumented aliens, he said. These illegal immigrants would continue to be entitled to emergency care, as they are now, he said.

HEADLINE: BUSINESS TECHNOLOGY;
Thriving in What Was the Soviet Silicon Valley

BYLINE: By RAYMOND BONNER, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: YEREVAN, Armenia

 BODY:
Even for those who believe in fairy tales, the story of a computer software company in this Caucasus republic can leave one laughing in disbelief.
In the waning years of the old Soviet Union, when it first allowed some experimentation with private enterprise, four Armenian brothers decided to start a computer company here. They had no money and, worse, no computers. 
 Some seven years later, their company, Aragast B, has 100 employees whose accomplishments include writing software for banks in Siberia and selling computerized dictionaries to schools as far away as California. The brothers also staved off local gangsters who demanded a piece of the business.
 
Trying to Be Competitive

 
 Aragast B is hardly a challenge to Microsoft, though the Armenian company contends that its dictionary, which translates English into Armenian, Russian and Arabic, is superior to those offered by American software manufacturers.
But Aragast has grown in a newly independent country where to say the economy is in shambles would be to exalt it. Along any street in the Armenian capital, more stores are closed than functioning and those that are open offer an anemic selection of goods. Industry is operating at 30 percent of capacity, thanks to a fuel shortage that results in electricity being available only two hours a day. By unofficial counts more than half a million Armenians have fled in the last three years, reducing the population of this New Jersey-size country to three million.
"Yes, the country is near the bottom, but still there is a lot of energy," said Gourgen Martirossian, president of Aragast B, and the youngest of the brothers. "Historically, we have seen worse times, times when not only the economy was destroyed but we lost our people -- the genocide."
He was referring to the slaughter nearly 80 years ago of hundreds of thousands of Armenians by Turkish forces. "We know how to recover," Mr. Martirossian said.
It is understandable why the brothers would choose to start a computer company. Armenia was once the Silicon Valley of the former Soviet Union. Forty percent of the mainframe computers for the Soviet military were designed here. At one time 5,000 people worked at the Yerevan Computer Research Institute, a downtown complex of stone buildings so secret that ordinary Armenians did not know what went on inside. The decaying buildings are largely empty now, except for Aragast's offices on the third floor.
But how could a computer software company begin without computers? "That's an interesting question," Mr. Martirossian said with a laugh. "We went to the customers and used their computers."
The brothers were used to having nothing. Their parents were schoolteachers and as children, Gourgen and his next older brother, Babken, had to share a pair of pants for school. If the boy who attended the morning sessions tarried, the boy waiting at home would miss his afternoon classes. While recalling those days, Gourgen Martirossian sat in his office wearing a pin-striped suit, broad-collared white shirt and tie.
His oldest brother, Armen, is the general manager of Aragast, a holding company that includes banking, insurance, agriculture and retailing units. Babken and Karen, the fourth brother, also work for the company.
From the beginning, Aragast B focused on banks as potential software customers. "Even when the economy is bad, banks are the last to die," Gourgen Martirossian explained.
Using the old Soviet-made computers at Armenian banks, the brothers developed software to computerize the daily operations of the banks. When they demonstrated their software at a bank trade fair in Moscow recently, two Russian banks also bought copies.
"The amazing thing was they paid cash right away," Mr. Martirossian recalled with a mixture of awe and pleasure.
Such fledgling capitalists throughout the former Soviet Union have found one of the greatest challenges is getting work out of workers grown accustomed in the Soviet era to being paid for just showing up.
Aragast B pays its employees relatively well by current Armenian standards. But Mr. Martirossian said wages mattered less than a climate of caring about employees' needs.
"In these horrible conditions, money is not the most important thing," Mr. Martirossian said. "We understand this and this is the key to our success. We've created a climate in this company and this climate is more important than wages."
As an example, he spoke of what the company had done for some employees who were among the thousands of Armenian refugees escaping the fighting between Azerbaijan and Armenia. Aragast B hired several skilled refugees, but the apartments they could find were far from the center of Yerevan. With public transportation sporadic these days, the newcomers had trouble getting to work. So the company bought them a car.
Along the way, Aragast faced down organized criminals who are nicknamed "the mafia" and who traffic in everything from stolen cars to weapons. Such criminals come knocking on the doors of legitimate businesses, demanding protection money or in the case of Aragast B, a share of the business.
Aragast B refused and soon found its employees attacked and the company robbed by drug addicts hired by the gangsters. Mr. Martirossian and executives started carrying pistols. The war of nerves lasted more than a year, but Aragast prevailed.
"I think when they saw that we were willing to put our lives on the line, when we were ready to die for our business, they backed off," said Gaegin Chookaszian, Aragast's vice president for strategic planning and marketing.

GRAPHIC: Photo: Seven years after four Armenian brothers decided to start a computer company in Yerevan even before they had a computer, Aragast B is employing 100 people and writing software for banks in Siberia. Gourgen Martirossian, second from left, its president and the youngest of the four brothers, checked in on a worker with Gaegin Chookaszian, the company's vice president for marketing. (Raymond Bonner)
