HEADLINE: Industrial Lands Invite Russian Cooperation on Political Issues

BYLINE: By STEVEN GREENHOUSE, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: GENEVA, April 26

 BODY:
The Group of Seven industrial countries will invite Russia to work formally with them as they develop positions on a range of political issues, Secretary of State Warren Christopher said today.
Mr. Christopher told Russia's Foreign Minister, Andrei V. Kozyrev, at a meeting here that Russia, which is far poorer than the Group of Seven, could take part in the group's meetings on political issues, but not on economic ones. 
 American officials said the move was designed in part to extend a friendly hand after President Boris N. Yeltsin complained that the United States and its NATO allies had failed to notify Russia before bombing Bosnian Serb positions three weeks ago.
 
Aim Is to Reassure Russians
 The new overture to Moscow came a day after Mr. Christopher and Foreign Secretary Douglas Hurd of Britain announced that Russia would be a member of a new coordinating group to help push for a cease-fire and a peace plan for Bosnia. The group also includes the United States, the European Union and the United Nations.
The moves are aimed at reassuring Russians and their increasingly restive nationalist politicians that the wealthy countries do not see Russia as a junior partner in world affairs.
At a news conference in Geneva, Mr. Christopher said, "It's safe to say Russia will have a significant role in the political discussions" at the summit meeting of Group of Seven leaders in Naples in July.
Mr. Christopher said that at today's meeting, he and Mr. Kozyrev had discussed more than a half-dozen issues, including how to promote peace in Bosnia and the Middle East and the importance of pressing North Korea to allow full inspections of its nuclear sites.
American and Russian officials said that if the Bosnian Serbs adhered to the NATO ultimatum calling on them to withdraw from Gorazde, then the United States, Russia and the European Union were ready to send envoys to Sarajevo on Thursday to meet with the Bosnian Government to discuss a peace plan. They would then meet with Bosnian Serb officials on Friday.
Talking about Russian diplomatic efforts with the Serbs, Mr. Christopher said today in London, before leaving for Geneva, "They have been helpful, I think, up to this point, and we want to make sure we are working in close harmony on this."
 
Fear of Offending Moscow
 Mr. Yeltsin's criticism of the NATO bombing, which was enthusiastically supported by many members of Russia's Parliament, caused Washington to move more gingerly on Bosnia for fear of offending Moscow.
The Russians largely lined up with the NATO view after Mr. Kozyrev went to Belgrade two weeks ago and thought he had won the Serbs' reassurances they they would end their offensive against Gorazde. When the Serbs continued advancing on the city, the Russians felt betrayed.
Since then, Mr. Kozyrev has taken a far harder line toward the Serbs, saying their behavior was "criminally defiant" of international norms. Mr. Kozyrev indicated his support for the ultimatum from NATO, which threatens to bomb Serbian positions and assets if they do not withdraw from Gorazde.

 	 	 		--------------------	 	
 
U.S.-Russia Military Exercise
 MOSCOW, April 26 (Special to The New York Times) -- In another gesture to try to placate his political opposition, President Boris N. Yeltsin ordered the Defense Ministry today to reconsider the first joint military exercises with the United States, scheduled for July.
American officials have regularly pointed to the exercises as an important symbol of growing Russian-American trust and partnership after the breakup of the Soviet Union.
But ultranationalist and Communist members of the lower house of Parliament have expressed doubts over the "expediency" of holding the exercises, which are supposed to concentrate on peacekeeping operations, on Russian soil.
This evening the American Defense Secretary, William Perry, called his Russian counterpart, Gen. Pavel S. Grachev, and after a discussion about Bosnia urged him not to cancel the joint exercises.

HEADLINE: COMPANY REPORTS;
B.A.T. Is Buying U.S. Rival

BYLINE: By KENNETH N. GILPIN 

 BODY:
In a move that would strengthen its position as the nation's third-biggest tobacco company, B.A.T. Industries P.L.C. said yesterday that it had agreed to buy the American Tobacco Company from American Brands for $1 billion in cash.
The transaction, subject to regulatory approval, won applause from Wall Street analysts, who said it made sense not only for the two companies, but also for the besieged tobacco industry. 
 "For B.A.T., it's an immediate positive impact on earnings," said Roy D. Burry, an analyst for Kidder, Peabody & Company. "For American Brands, it's $1 billion for a business that has a declining share of the market, with no chance of turning around. And the industry's competitive environment will change. With fewer participants, there is likely to be less aggressive price competition."
 
Shares Up $2.875
 Investors seemed to agree. Shares of American Brands surged $2.875, to $34.625, on the New York Stock Exchange yesterday. B.A.T. shares rose $0.5625, to $13.75. Prices of big competitors like Philip Morris and RJR Nabisco also rose in heavy trading.
In addition to the positive economics of the deal, the acquisition completes an association that began in 1902. That year, the American Tobacco Trust, put together by James Buchanan Duke, controlled about 90 percent of the American market. But Mr. Duke, seeking even bigger profits, visited Britain, where American Tobacco and a newly formed British company, Imperial Tobacco, formed B.A.T. as a joint venture; American Tobacco owned 60 percent and Imperial the remainder.
The arrangement stood until 1911, when American Tobacco was found in violation of the Sherman Act and was broken up. As a result, American Tobacco's ownership of B.A.T. was severed.
Since then, B.A.T. has expanded into the United States. It owns the Brown & Williamson Tobacco Corporation, which Mr. Goldman estimates has about 12 percent of the American cigarette market, with brands like Kool, Capri and Viceroy. Last year the company had total domestic sales of $2.4 billion.
B.A.T. does not break out operating profit information for its United States operations. Worldwide, Brown & Williamson had a total trading profit of $385 million last year.
By comparison, American Tobacco, whose brands include Lucky Strike, Pall Mall, Carlton and Tareyton, has about 7 percent of the market. Last year the subsidiary of American Brands had operating income of $169 million, on revenues of $1.51 billion.
Even with the addition of American Tobacco, B.A.T. will still be behind Philip Morris, which has 43 percent of the United States cigarette market, and RJR Nabisco, which has 28 percent. But analysts said the company that emerged from the takeover would be a far more effective competitor than its predecessors.
"In a mature industry, where there is little if any volume growth, it is nice to have some size," said Emmanuel Goldman, a beverage and tobacco analyst at Paine Webber Group.
Indeed, with cigarette use falling about 2.5 percent a year and profits from domestic sales under pressure, there were probably few companies not already in the tobacco business that would have been interested in buying American Tobacco.
"This is not the sort of market that people who are not already in the business would choose to get into," Martin F. Broughton, the group chief executive for B.A.T., said in a telephone interview from London. "But if you are in it, it is a buyer's market."
It is also not a business that American Brands is leaving behind. Although it has interests in distilled spirits, life insurance, hardware and home improvement products, tobacco will still be its most important business even after the sale. It is hanging on to its British subsidiary, Gallaher Tobacco, which has more than 40 percent of Britain's cigarette market.
In a release announcing both the sale of American Tobacco and its first-quarter earnings, American Brands said yesterday that when American Tobacco's contribution was excluded, Gallaher represented 37 percent of the company's operating income. Distilled spirits was next, with about 20 percent.
American Brands said it would use the proceeds of the sale for share purchases, debt reduction, strategic acquisitions and other corporate purposes.

HEADLINE: Rifkin Linked to an 18th Killing As Prosecution Rests in His Trial

BYLINE: By JOHN T. McQUISTON, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: MINEOLA, L.I., April 26

 BODY:
The prosecution rested its case today in the murder trial of Joel Rifkin, with the final witness offering testimony suggesting that Mr. Rifkin might have killed more women than the 17 he says he strangled.
The witness, an expert on blood analysis, said the blood found in a wheelbarrow used by Mr. Rifkin to move the body of his final victim, Tiffany Bresciani, belonged to someone else. 
 The expert, Dr. Arlene Colon, chief of the serology section in the Nassau County Medical Examiner's office, said the blood in the wheelbarrow had been there for about two weeks before the discovery of Ms. Bresciani's body in the back of Mr. Rifkin's pickup truck last June 28.
In his 12-page confession, Mr. Rifkin admitted killing Ms. Bresciani and 16 other women he said were prostitutes. He said that he killed his 16th victim on April 2, more than two and a half months before he killed Ms. Bresciani.
Investigators say the blood in the wheelbarrow suggests that another victim, not mentioned in Mr. Rifkin's confession, was killed a few weeks before Ms. Bresciani.
The prosecutor, Fred Klein, did not discuss the possibility of additional victims in his opening statement but strongly suggested that there was at least one more victim when he questioned Dr. Colon today.
"I came to the inescapable conclusion that the sample from the wheelbarrow did not come from Tiffany Bresciani," Dr. Colon testified. She said the blood could have been there a few days or as long as a month.
Testimony in the case is to resume Thursday morning. At that time, Mr. Rifkin's lawyer, John Lawrence, plans to call his first and possibly only witness, Barbara Kirwin, a psychologist from Huntington, L.I.
She is expected to testify that Mr. Rifkin, a 34-year-old unemployed landscaper, is a paranoid schizophrenic who has trouble distinguishing fantasy from reality and meets the legal standards for insanity.
The burden of proof in an insanity defense rests with the defense.

HEADLINE: FUTURES MARKETS;
Lumber Prices in Rebound, But Most Trading Is Quiet

BYLINE: By Reuters 

 BODY:
Lumber prices rebounded sharply yesterday from recent declines, but trading on most commodities exchanges was subdued ahead of a one-day market closing to remember former President Richard M. Nixon.
Oil prices declined in what was widely viewed as profit taking before the market closing. 
 The lumber market, which has stumbled in the last month because of well-stocked inventories of timber and rising interest rates that curtail house building, recovered on signs of a springtime pickup in demand.
"The cash market may have a spark of life," Bud Merrill, a forest products industry analyst at Smith Barney Shearson, said.
Cash lumber prices have crashed the last couple of weeks and prices were down sharply at the start of this week. But business was improving at the lower price levels, which bottomed out at around $270 for 1,000 board feet.
At the Chicago Mercantile Exchange, May lumber jumped the daily limit of $10, to $307.50 for 1,000 board feet.
Purchases of spruce two-by-fours would likely grow as more wood is moved to job sites with the spring building season beginning in earnest, said Curt Cunningham of Pacific Futures Trading.
 
 
Cattle and Hog Prices Fall
 At the same exchange, cattle and hog prices fell sharply, continuing a monthlong downtrend.
Traders said the heavy pace of the hog slaughter has not abated this spring, making many analysts skeptical of recent Government estimates that the size of the herd had declined. That expectation also led to stocking of pork inventories held in frozen storage.
Cattle prices were affected by the oversupply of pork and the large numbers of animals being fattened for slaughter, with several contracts posting lows.
June live cattle dropped 0.95 cent, to 71.075 cents a pound, and May feeder cattle lost 1.325 cents, to 78.025 cents. June live hogs fell 1.425 cents, to 51.025 cents a pound, and May pork bellies declined 1.875 cents, to 50.25 cents a pound.
In oil trading, June crude eased 33 cents, to $16.91 a barrel. It hit a five-month high of $17.28 earlier in the session but failed to sustain it. Over the last four weeks, the market has risen more than $3 a barrel.
May unleaded gasoline closed down 0.68 cent, at 50.71 cents, and May heating oil fell 0.86 cent, to 47.37 cents. Both nearby contracts expire Friday.

HEADLINE: Cuomo Details Compromise Proposed in Budget Talks

BYLINE: By JAMES DAO, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: ALBANY, April 26

 BODY:
In an unusual bargaining tactic, Gov. Mario M. Cuomo released details today about a compromise plan intended to revive the stalled negotiations over the $33 billion state budget, which is now 26 days late.
The plan would largely split the differences separating the Democratic-controlled Assembly and Republican-controlled Senate on two major issues: how to make $410 million in tax cuts this year and how to allocate $400 million in additional aid to local schools. 
 But more notable than the actual details of Mr. Cuomo's proposal was his willingness to reveal the inside workings of the stalled budget talks, which have been cloaked in secrecy and obscured by the public posturing of the negotiators. In disclosing his proposal, Mr. Cuomo and his aides said he wanted to show the triviality of the issues blocking an agreement.
"When you look at this, you can see we're not that far apart; you can see the end from here," said Andrew J. Zambelli, Mr. Cuomo's secretary.
 
A Higher Political Price
 Mr. Cuomo, who has been criticized by some lawmakers for not helping close a deal, also seemed intent on deflecting criticism for the late budget onto the Legislature while painting himself as a conciliator. Mr. Cuomo has said, and pollsters agree, that the Governor pays a higher political price for late budgets than does the Legislature.
"Budget woes are always placed at the step of chief executives because they are the most visible political figures," said Lee M. Miringoff, director of the Marist College Institute of Public Opinion in Poughkeepsie.
Privately, lawmakers and their aides said Mr. Cuomo could resolve the impasse by agreeing to find additional money for new spending and tax cuts. "The Governor's got to come in to close this up," said one legislative aide. "If one side says we need more money for something, he ought to find a way to take care of it."
But Mr. Cuomo seemed unwilling to put more money on the table, despite improving economic forecasts. "I cannot allow the Legislature to turn our progress into a pitfall by overspending and excessive tax cuts," he said in a statement.
Though the late budget has had little tangible impact on government services to date, scores of nonprofit agencies with state contracts are expected to face cash-flow problems by next week because their state checks have been delayed. The impasse has also created havoc for villages, school districts and cities, including New York, that are preparing new budgets without knowing how much state assistance they will receive.
In his compromise plan, Mr. Cuomo seeks to steer a middle course between competing Senate and Assembly tax reduction plans, incorporating aspects of both while proposing to phase in many of the cuts to reduce the impact on future budgets.
The plan includes several tax cuts sought by the Senate Republicans, including eliminating by September the state's 5 percent tax on hotel rooms costing more than $100 a night and phasing out a 15 percent surcharge on corporate taxes. But while the Senate proposed eliminating the surcharge by 1996, Mr. Cuomo calls for gradually reducing it to zero by 1999.
"The Republicans' proposed phase-in would leave a large hole in the budget," Mr. Cuomo said.
The Senate majority leader, Ralph J. Marino, an Oyster Bay Republican, called Mr. Cuomo's proposal a "token" cut. "The Governor is sending the wrong message to struggling businesses," Mr. Marino said.
The compromise plan also includes two tax reduction proposals advanced by the Assembly Democrats: one to provide tax credits to companies that hire new employees from neighborhoods with high unemployment and another to reduce taxes on small companies. And it expands on Mr. Cuomo's own proposal to give tax credits to working poor families, though the credits would be smaller than the Assembly Democrats had sought.
The cost of the tax cuts would be $410 million during the current fiscal year, which began April 1, and would increase to $1.53 billion when fully carried out after 1999.
 
Dispute Over School Aid
 To resolve a dispute over aid to local schools, which has pitted suburban and upstate Republicans against New York City Democrats, Mr. Cuomo proposed increasing every school district's aid by at least 3.54 percent. But the proposal would give even more assistance to districts with growing student populations. That would help New York City and many upstate districts, but would do less for Long Island, where few school districts are growing.
Mr. Cuomo's proposal, much of which was presented to legislative leaders late last week, also calls for a three-year plan under which the state would begin assuming a greater share of Medicaid costs from county governments. Mr. Cuomo had advocated a full state takeover of local Medicaid costs, but his proposal was strongly opposed by Senate Republicans who said it was too costly.
"It was clear we weren't going to get the full takeover in this budget," Mr. Zambelli said. "This is a compromise that would at least begin the process."

HEADLINE: Public Unions Challenge Whitman on Pension Security

BYLINE: By JOSEPH F. SULLIVAN, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: TRENTON, April 26

 BODY:
Admitting they're trying to play catch up to the new administration's changing fiscal policies, leaders of unions representing teachers and state employees today attacked Governor Christine Todd Whitman's plan to reduce state payments for pensions by more than $1 billion in order to balance the budget and help pay for tax cuts.
Bob Purcell, area director for the Communications Workers of America, which repesents 50,000 state workers, said Mrs. Whitman "got out of the gate early" and "scared the Legislature with her popularity." He said reports just completed by actuaries hired by the unions showed that the Governor's changes threatened the stability of the pension system and shifted costs onto future generations of taxpayers. 
 Dennis Testa, president of the New Jersey Education Association, said, "Governor Whitman's successor will have to decide whether to raise taxes sky high -- or renege on commitments to retired employees. The Governor herself will have moved on."
At the heart of the problem, he said, are decisions by Mrs. Whitman and Brian W. Clymer, the State Treasurer, to reduce to $176 million from $528 million the state's contributions to the pension system in the current budget. Mrs. Whitman has also decided to put $57 million into pensions in the coming fiscal year instead of the $783 million called for under changes negotiated between the unions and the Republican-controlled Legislature and former Gov. Jim Florio in 1992.
 
'Scare Tactics' Alleged
 Mr. Clymer called the charges by the union leaders irresponsible. "They're using scare tactics," he said, and denied that the pensions were in danger of being underfinanced. "They are currently funded at more than 100 percent and continue to grow," the Treasurer said.
The union leaders said they would remind the lawmakers of their two-year-old agreement, inform their members of the threat to their pension and medical benefits, mount a public relations campaign and stand ready with a "legal component" if they decide they need to go to court.
Today's salvo by the unions represents the first major assault on the Governor's comparatively smooth assumption of power.
Until now, there have been only skirmishes like demonstrations by college students against potential tuition increases and criticism of the budget by some maverick Republicans, including Senator Richard J. LaRossa of Trenton, who has introduced a bill that would roll back the governor's initial 5 percent income tax rate cut.
 
Using Different Assumptions
 Claude Poulin, a pension actuary who has been a trustee of the Connecticut State Employees' Retirement Commission since 1982, examined the Whitman administration proposals at the request of the state employees union. He concluded: "If this set of proposals is adopted, New Jersey will have gone, in just a few short years, from one of the more enlighted pension funding policies to one of the worst, and more importantly, to one of the most irresponsible."
Mr. Clymer said the Governor's proposed policy was sound, given the amount of assets in the pension system. The state is putting enough money into the system to meet current benefit costs, he insisted.
But Mr. Poulin said the state was using outdated mortality tables and an unrealistic estimate of interest rates, 8.75 percent, to make the system appear sound.
Mr. Clymer defended the state's estimates, saying that pension systems were given constant reviews and that, if any problem should arise, it could be addressed in time to avoid any sharp decline in pension and medical system assets.
He said the New Jersey pension assets were higher than the national average. "We shouldn't ask taxpayers to fund any increase in assets right now," he said.
Still, when asked by reporters whether he could guarantee the continuation of pension, medical and cost-of-living benefits for retirees in the future, Mr. Clymer replied, "There are no guarantees on anything."

HEADLINE: Stock Market Is Listless in Slow Trading

BYLINE: By ANTHONY RAMIREZ 

 BODY:
The stock market closed essentially unchanged yesterday despite factors that ought to have prompted a rally, notably a drop in long-term interest rates and fresh employment data that indicated inflation was still under control.
Ahead of today, when most financial markets in the United States will be closed in memory of former President Richard M. Nixon, both the stock and bond markets had below-normal trading volume, probably signaling that many traders were declining to buy or sell. 
 The number of shares changing hands on the New York Stock Exchange totaled 288.1 million, or 6 percent below this year's average of 305 million shares.
The exception was tobacco stocks, where the news that Britain's B.A.T Industries planned to buy American Brands' domestic tobacco business for $1 billion lifted the prices of the leading tobacco stocks, American Brands, Philip Morris and RJR Nabisco, which was the most-active stock yesterday, at 11 million shares. The food and cigarette company rose 3/8 to 6 7/8.
The benchmark 30-year bond continued its rise in price, as its yield fell to 7.10 percent, down 5 basis points, or hundredths of a percentage point, from Monday. In less than a week and a half, the long bond's yield has fallen more than 30 basis points, but traders doubted that the market rally would be long-lived.
The value of United States Government bonds rose early after a Labor Department report that wages and benefits were rising at a slower rate than economists expected. The employment cost index rose 3.2 percent, the smallest gain since 1982, when the department began publicizing the report.
The slight rise meant that workers were having a tough time getting raises and therefore that businesses' operating costs were stable, helping to damp inflationary pressures.
But the value of bonds dropped only an hour later when the Conference Board reported that its consumer confidence index unexpectedly rose. Apparently choosing to interpret the index as a signal of an overheating economy and therefore of inflation, bond traders bid down the price of bonds.
The seesaw action of bonds contributed to seesaw action in stocks, but equities traded within a fairly narrow range. The lowest the Dow Jones industrial average fell, for example, was only 12 points, and the highest it rose was 5 points.
The Dow ended 6.24 points lower at 3,699.54, and the broader Standard & Poor's 500 fell 0.84 point to 451.87. The Nasdaq composite index rose 3.41 points to 734.21.
The New York Stock Exchange composite index fell 0.06 point to 250.41. Advancing stocks outnumbered declining issues by 1,268 to 913. The American Stock Exchange market value index was up 2.23 points to 438.20.
Of the 30 Dow component stocks, 17 fell, led by Chevron and Texaco. Chevron fell 1 3/4 to 90 7/8 following its report that operating earnings fell 16 percent to $424 million, or $1.30 a share, at the low end of analysts' expectations. Texaco fell 1 1/4 to 64 3/8. On Monday, Texaco reported a 27 percent drop in earnings to $202 million, or 69 cents a share.
Of the 500 stocks in the S.& P. 500, 236 fell in price, led by Merck and Exxon. Merck, the drug company, fell 5/8 to 30 1/4 following news that Martin Wygod, chairman of the mail-order drug business recently bought by Merck, would not be a candidate to become Merck's chairman.
Of 304 companies reporting earnings yesterday, 165, or some 54 percent, reported higher earnings, according to Zacks Investment Research. Of these, 79 companies reported earnings that were at least 10 percent higher than analysts' expectations.

HEADLINE: CURRENCY MARKETS;
Dollar Falls on Broad Front Despite Encouraging News

BYLINE: By Reuters 

 BODY:
The dollar fell against most leading currencies yesterday despite good news about the American economy.
Gold prices rose 40 cents on the New York Commodity Exchange, where the June contract, the most active, settled at $375.30 an ounce. A late quote from Republic National Bank put spot gold at $374.15 an ounce. 
 News that the Conference Board's monthly index of consumer confidence jumped to a higher-than-expected 91.7 in April, from 86.7 in March, touched off a short-lived rally for the dollar.
Traders jumped on the positive economic news, interpreting the data as evidence of a possible threat of inflation, which in their minds would lead the Federal Reserve Bank to increase short-term interest rates.
Higher interest rates attract international investment, and the added demand for United States investments in turn pushes up the value of the dollar.
But the rally was undone by news indicating that European interest rates would probably remain comparatively high in the near future.
A British survey showed that country's manufacturing sector grew faster in March than at any time in the last five years, prompting speculation that further cuts in British rates to spur growth might not be needed.
 
Pound Rises Against Dollar
 The news sent the pound soaring more than a cent against the dollar, to $1.5055 in late New York trading, from $1.4905 Monday. In London, it closed at $1.5030, up from $1.4915.
Later, comments by Hans Tietmeyer, president of Germany's Bundesbank, left traders uncertain about the direction of German interest rates. Mr. Tietmeyer, speaking at the American Council on Germany in New York, said inflation was still too high in his country, an indication that the German central bank might slow the pace of rate cuts.
But Mr. Tietmeyer also said the Bundesbank "would continue to explore the scope for reducing interest rates." Dealers said his remarks left a mixed impression.
The dollar dipped to 1.6763 marks in late afternoon New York trading, from 1.6812 on Monday. Earlier in London, it edged up to 1.6792 marks, from 1.6784.
In late trading, the dollar was quoted at 102.80 Japanese yen in New York, down from 103.09. on Monday.
Other late dollar rates compared, with Monday's quotes, included: 1.4310 Swiss francs, down from 1.4345; 5.7610 French francs, down from 5.7705; Canadian $1.3727, down from $1.3755, and 1,614 Italian lire, up from 1,609.

HEADLINE: Computers in School: Equal Access Sought

BYLINE: By STEVE LOHR 

 BODY:
As the nation moves beyond today's computers and communications toward the so-called information highway, the Clinton Administration and growing numbers of educators and lawmakers contend that the Government must insure equal access to technology for rich and poor.
Unless Washington steps in, they warn, the increasing power of technology could become a force that divides schools -- and American society -- even further into the haves and have-nots. 
 The gap is already present -- the difference, say, between a school like the Lodge Grass High School on a Crow Indian reservation in Montana, where high-tech gadgetry is scarce, and the Glenbrook North High School in the affluent Chicago suburb of Northbrook, Ill., which is equipped with hundreds of computers, and where students routinely tap into the Internet, the worldwide network linked to millions of people and to resources like the Library of Congress.
 
Fears of a Widening Gap
 It is this gap that some fear will widen unless steps are taken to make the new digital services available inexpensively to poorer schools and rural schools. At stake, policy analysts say, is whether the United States will be an information aristocracy or a digital democracy.
"Education is where we as a society can do the most good with this technology," said Reed Hundt, chairman of the Federal Communications Commission.
No one contends that technology is an educational cure-all, but educators and policy experts, who predict that high-capacity computer networks will eventually become as basic to classrooms as textbooks and blackboards, argue that Government must insure that access to these networks does not go first and mainly to the affluent.
Legislation proposed in both the House and Senate and backed by the Clinton Administration would require communications companies to provide the nation's 110,000 primary and secondary schools and 20,000 public and university libraries with inexpensive links to the interactive networks.
The legislation, expected to come to a vote in Congress later this year, calls for telephone companies and cable-television suppliers to give schools cheap hookups.
The information highway might be imagined as today's electronic networks on steroids, far faster and more powerful than the state-of-the-art digital pipeline into the Glenbrook North school. It promises to give users ready access to everything from the London Library to Hollywood movies to two-way video links to experts in any field.
The suburban Chicago school clearly has a head start toward that technological future. Glenbrook North has more than 300 personal computers, many of them laptops that students can use at school or at home. In addition to being wired to the Internet, the students have a computer-equipped television studio. The school even has its own technology consultant, Alan November. "This school is a real model of technological affluence," he said.
Much farther back is the Lodge Grass school, whose students come from a region accustomed to unemployment and poverty. Three years ago, Vicki Gale, an enterprising librarian, applied for and won a small private grant, which finally gave her library a telephone and a personal computer able to send and receive electronic messages over the phone line. Since then, the school has added several more personal computers.
Ms. Gale's modest computer-network link was a big step forward, even though only about a half-dozen of the school's 200 students use it. "It's a starting point for us," she said.
The crucial ground rules for building the new high-speed electronic thoroughfare will probably be drawn up in Washington over the next few months. And supplying service to schools is shaping up as the first big social equity issue.
"Schools are the real test case of whether this privately built digital network will be accessible and affordable to the public as a whole," said Jerry Berman, executive director of the Electronic Frontier Foundation, a technology policy group.
Opposition has been muted so far to the proposed legislation. Skeptics are less concerned about the mandated wiring of schools than about the proposal's potential for a broader precedent: Washington meddling in high-technology markets in the name of social equity.
"The push for Government intervention to supposedly help the information have-nots is an area ripe for abuse," said Mark Stahlman, president of New Media Associates Inc., a research firm. "With entitlements come bureaucracy and the Government's agenda."
Despite some resistance from industry, several big phone and cable companies, notably the Bell Atlantic Corporation and Tele-Communications Inc., support the concept of paying to link schools to their planned high-capacity digital networks.
The really thorny matter will probably be pricing the services. The proposed legislation calls for "reasonable" or "preferential" rates for schools, largely leaving it to the F.C.C. to determine what qualifies.
To some lawmakers and educators, reasonable means inexpensive indeed. Representative Edward J. Markey, chairman of the House telecommunications subcommittee and author of a bill that calls for wiring schools, says information services to classrooms should cost as little as a penny a minute.
Some advocates say even a penny is too much. For example, the George Lucas Educational Foundation, a nonprofit group set up by the film maker, recommends mandating that companies provide free services to schools.
Even without Federal mandates, schoolchildren are slowly being introduced to computer networks.
 
A Broad Range of Equipment
 But the range of today's network technology varies enormously. And computer networks are still used only sporadically in most American schools, where only one in every eight classrooms has a phone line and only one in 25 has a computer with a modem to send and receive data.
Even at the low end of the high-tech world, however, teachers often feel encouraged by the effect on students. At the Lodge Grass school in Montana, for example, Ms. Gale says the school's rudimentary electronic-network connection has opened new vistas for some students.
As part of its private grant, the school received free access to the AT&T Learning Network, which links classrooms in small groups. From the library computer, the Lodge Grass students can exchange messages and work on projects with students in Singapore, Canada, Germany and Italy.
Jonathan Deputee, a 17-year-old junior at Lodge Grass, has been impressed by how much students from different countries are alike, from shared anxieties about teenage relationships to common tastes in rock music from Metallica to Meat Loaf. "I thought they'd be more different than they are," he said.
Her students, Ms. Gale says, need more of that kind of technology-aided experience.
"A lot of our kids are have-not kids," Ms. Gale said. "And while the computer link we have may be small and basic, it's made a world of difference for some of my kids in terms of self-esteem and interest in school work."

HEADLINE: CREDIT MARKETS;
Interest Rates Continue to Decline

BYLINE: By ROBERT HURTADO 

 BODY:
Interest rates dropped yesterday for second time this week and the sixth time in seven sessions on continuing evidence that the economy is not expanding so rapidly as some may have thought.
Treasury securities made impressive gains in mostly technical trading ahead of tomorrow's report on the gross domestic product for the first quarter, which will provide the first full picture of the period's economic growth, and March durable goods orders. The market is closed today for the funeral of former President Richard M. Nixon. 
 Analysts and economists will have their hands full assimilating all the data released tomorrow. But many traders are hoping that they will finally get an idea of how fast the economy is growing and whether they can worry less about inflation.
 
Fed Action Expected
 But some dealers and traders expect the Federal Reserve to raise short-term rates again soon, even if the new data indicate that the economy is growing at a moderate pace that would not reignite inflation.
By late in the day the price of the 30-year bond was trading up 16/32 , to 8918/32 , as the yield, which moves in the opposite direction, fell to 7.10 percent, from 7.15 percent on Monday. It was the lowest since March 31, when the yield was 7.08 percent. Despite the rally of the last two weeks, the yield on the 30-year bond is still in the trading range of the last 30 days.
An unexpected surge in April consumer confidence data reported by the Conference Board in the morning caused bond prices to drop sharply before rebounding later.
Yesterday's confidence index, based on a survey of 5,000 households, rose to 91.7, exceeding market expectations, which were for a slight decline to 86.2, from last month's 86.7. Analysts said the jump in confidence initially sparked fears among bond traders and investors that the economy would stay strong in the months ahead, leading some investors to sell. A stronger economy generally pressures credit markets, because it is feared that it will spark inflation and erode fix-income investments.
"Consumer confidence as measured by the Conference Board, is currently just a shade above its historical average," said Donald Fine, the chief market strategist at Chase Securities. "The recent improvement is clearly a sign of an expanding economy, but certainly not a signal of any overheating." The index's low point of the last seven or eight years was set in February 1992, when it hit 47.3; the high point was set three years before at 120.7.
Before the consumer confidence report, the market rose after the report of a smaller-than-expected rise of the Labor Department's first-quarter employment cost index. The index rose 0.7 percent in the first quarter and 3.2 percent in the 12 months ended March 31. The report, which covers wages and benefits for both the public and private sectors, is an important measure of the costs that can drive up inflation.
Mr. Fine said the data showed that "if there is any pressure on inflation, it would not be from the cost side." He added: "Right now the economy is growing steadily and solidly, and at a noninflationary pace. And it's the Fed's job to keep it that way."
Looking toward tomorrow, Mr. Fine said he expected the G.D.P. report to come in at about 3.4 percent, after adjustment for inflation, a little more than the market consensus of 3.2 percent. He expects durable goods will be up 1.5 percent.
 
Treasury Auction
 The bond market cleared its last hurdle for the day when the Treasury auctioned $17 billion of new two-year notes at a high yield of 5.59 percent. Traders said the auction went fairly well, with the yield lower than expected.
Tomorrow the Treasury will be back in business and auction $11 billion of five-year notes and $16.5 billion of one-year bills. In when-issued trading the five-year was offered at a price to yield 6.45 percent, improving from 6.49 percent on Monday. The rate for a one-year bill was unchanged at 4.64 percent.
Elsewhere, municipal bond prices were steady as investors focused on yesterday's $1.7 billion in new bonds. Many investors were reluctant to buy existing issues with most market closed today and a batch of economic reports scheduled for release tomorrow. Most of the week's new issues were sold yesterday.
In negotiated sales yesterday, the Pennsylvania Industrial Development Authority tentatively priced $356.2 million of economic development revenue bonds to yield a maximum 6.15 percent for a bond due in 2014.
Underwriters led by Lehman Brothers also priced shorter-maturity serial bonds to yield from 3.30 percent in 1995 to 5.875 percent in 2009, with another long issue due in 2012 priced to yield 6.125 percent. All the bonds are rated triple-A because of insurance from the Ambac Indemnity Corporation.
The largest competitive municipal deal was done by Washington, which sold $300 million of general obligation bonds through Lehman Brothers, which reoffered the bonds at prices to yield from 3.40 percent in 1995 to 6.10 percent in 2019. The issue is rated AA by S.& P.

HEADLINE: Market Place;
Wall Street is closed to honor a bear market President.

BYLINE: By Floyd Norris 

 BODY:
WHEN Richard M. Nixon was elected President in 1968, there was general pleasure on Wall Street. According to the prevailing wisdom of the time, his election marked a return to traditional economic policies and good news for the stock market.
It was, as it happens, another case of Wall Street crystal balls showing only what the seers were wishing for. Mr. Nixon, whose funeral is today, wound up being the first President since Herbert Hoover, and only the third President of the 20th century, to have the Dow Jones industrial average decline over his term of office. 
While many of the remembrances of Mr. Nixon have focused on the surprise of an anti-Communist warrior opening relations with China, there has been less emphasis on his most surprising economic initiative, perhaps because it proved much less successful. But it is equally true that only Mr. Nixon could have imposed wage and price controls, as he did in 1971, when the inflation rate was above 4 percent a year -- then deemed unconscionably high.
That decision was immensely popular on Wall Street, and the Dow leaped 3.8 percent the first trading day after the announcement -- the equivalent of a 140-point move at today's Dow level. But it was a serious mistake on a longer-term basis, especially when combined with an accommodating Federal Reserve monetary policy as the 1972 election approached.
Instead of dealing with monetary causes of inflation, or with the profligate use of energy that was making the country vulnerable to an oil price shock, the Government set up a bureaucracy that ended up ruling on such vital issues as whether a New Hampshire county fair violated the law when it raised admission prices by 25 cents. (The ruling was that it did.)
Mr. Nixon himself noted this in his memoirs. "The Aug. 15, 1971, decision to impose them," Mr. Nixon wrote of the price controls, "was politically necessary and immensely popular in the short run. But in the long run I believe it was wrong. The piper must be paid, and there was an unquestionably high price for tampering with the orthodox economic mechanisms."
But none of that was clear at first. It was a week after Mr. Nixon was re-elected in 1972 that the Dow first closed over 1,000, and in early 1973 the Dow hit a peak of 1,051.70, a high mark that was destined to last for a decade.
It was the upward spiraling of oil prices, which followed the Arab oil boycott prompted by the 1973 Arab-Israeli war, that caused the recession and the worst bear market of the last half century. It was during that bear market that Mr. Nixon resigned. The Dow, at 935.54 when he was inaugurated for his first term, had fallen to 784.89 by the time he quit. A few months later, it was to bottom at 577.60, a decline of 45 percent from the peak reached less than two years earlier.
Presidents like to take credit for bull markets during their administrations, while distancing themselves from bear markets, but, as was noted by Robert Farrell, the chief market analyst for Merrill Lynch during and after the Nixon Administration, "in truth they often have very little to do with what happens to the markets in their administrations."
Mr. Nixon was elected near the end of a 20-year bull market, which had taken stock prices from undervalued to greatly overvalued and brought on "great speculative excesses," Mr. Farrell noted. The bear market that followed might have been less severe had Mr. Nixon done a better job on inflation, or had investors been less depressed by the specter of a collapsing Presidency, but it would have occurred in any case.
There is a tradition of closing the stock market for Presidential funerals, but this one is different in one way. Every President who died after 1930, save one, prompted the New York Stock Exchange to take a full day off. But Herbert Hoover's death in 1964, like that of William Howard Taft in 1930, led only to the exchange closing early rather than taking a full day of mourning.
The Taft, Hoover and Nixon Administrations were the only ones since the Dow Jones industrial average was invented in 1897 that resulted in a Dow drop. Thus Mr. Nixon becomes the first bear market President to be honored by a full exchange holiday.

HEADLINE: Visions of Weaning Americans From Nicotine

BYLINE: By PHILIP J. HILTS 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON

 BODY:
ANTISMOKING forces in Washington, gaining momentum in Congress in recent weeks, have begun to imagine America with strict regulation of cigarettes.
But what would that mean? Cigarettes by prescription only? Nicotine-free cigarettes? Most agree that simply banning cigarettes is not an option. But one idea often mentioned by advocates is that the Government could gradually reduce the amount of nicotine permitted in cigarettes over, say, two decades. 
 That idea may never fly, but that people are even raising it is a measure of how far the antismoking tide has swept since a year ago.
 
Feasibility of Idea
 It would be national nicotine withdrawal stretched out over an entire generation. But could it work?
"It could be done, and it could be done gradually so that there is not much distress caused by the reductions, especially in the beginning," said Dr. Neal L. Benowitz, a professor of medicine at the University of California at San Francisco who is an expert on smokers and their behavior. "After all, 80 percent of smokers say they want to quit. This would make it easier for them to do that, because over a long time they would just find the cigarettes less and less satisfying."
Dr. Jack E. Henningfield, chief of clinical pharmacology at the National Institute on Drug Abuse, agreed. "Yes, it is possible to wean the public off nicotine and cigarettes," he said.
The idea is one that has been around for some time. But recently, as antismoking advocates began to question what outcome they were really hoping for, it resurfaced. After Dr. David A. Kessler, the Commissioner of Food and Drugs, said he was willing to regulate cigarettes, Dr. Benowitz mentioned this idea to him. Dr. Kessler has since included it when he discusses the options the F.D.A. may consider.
A big advantage would be that cigarettes with lower nicotine levels would make it easier for children who experiment with smoking to avoid becoming addicted. It is only over a year or more of use that nicotine addiction takes effect. Experts say that at least three-quarters of new smokers are children, and that they start smoking for social reasons.
The greatest concern that experts have in contemplating such a national weaning project is that when nicotine is reduced, some smokers may smoke more cigarettes to get the same level of nicotine, taking in larger and larger amounts of tar and other carcinogens in the process.
Dr. Benowitz said, however, that he believed this might not be a major problem in the long run. Studies of people weaned to lower-nicotine cigarettes show that they overcompensate for a few weeks, but most then seem to adjust to a lower level of nicotine intake. He added that "this subject really needs more research."
Many advocacy groups support the idea of a weaning program, including Action on Smoking and Health, a Washington group that uses litigation to press the antismoking cause, and the Coalition on Smoking or Health, a group representing the American Heart Association, the American Lung Association and the American Cancer Society.
 
Little Change in Nicotine Levels
 Dr. Kessler, in supporting the idea, has said it would be important to take into account the way people smoke and why they smoke to make such a plan work.
Over the past century, cigarettes have gained filters, become less harsh tasting and lower in tar. But the amount of nicotine in cigarettes, as a percentage of the weight, has remained very stable since at least 1952, according to the Food and Drug Administration. The percentage of nicotine was about 1.5 to 1.8 in 1952, and now the range is 1.5 to 2.5 percent. On average, smokers take in about 25 milligrams of nicotine a day. Dr. Benowitz says his best estimate is that when nicotine intake drops below about 5 milligrams a day, smokers begin to suffer withdrawal.
Each puff a smoker takes delivers a hit of nicotine that sends the level in the blood shooting upward within 10 to 15 seconds, and then falls back to a more constant level.
It takes 8 to 10 hours for nicotine to flush out of the body, which means that each morning smokers start with a low level of nicotine still in their blood. The first puffs of the day are more satisfying, and drug tolerance builds up on a daily cycle, so the last smokes of the day are less adequate and smokers may take more cigarettes to compensate for the milder hit late in the day.
Nicotine does give a mild high that tends to increase alertness slightly, and gives a small improvement in performance on memory and attention tests.
The absolute amount of nicotine in a cigarette averages 8 to 9 milligrams, in both "light" and regular cigarettes, 5 to 6 times the amount needed to remain addicted. But smokers can vary the dose they get, depending on how deeply and often they puff. The average dose given by a cigarette is one milligram, but smokers can get as much as three milligrams from a so-called "low nicotine" cigarette, or as little as half a milligram from "full flavored" cigarette.
Because of this variation in intake, even if the Government cut nicotine by 5 or 10 percent a year, it would not force smokers into withdrawal for several years, and then only gradually.
Greg Connolly, director of the Massachusetts Health Department's antismoking program, suggested that other tactics would be very helpful in encouraging people to quit and children not to start. "Treat the product like a drug, and do not allow any direct advertising or marketing, for example," he said. "You could use generic packaging, and put cigarettes in the drugstore next to aspirin as an over-the-counter drug, and make sure they are not sold in vending machines or elsewhere."
 
Possibility of Illegal Trafficking
 Restricting the sale of cigarettes in any form raises fears of an increase in black market sales, especially because there is already illegal traffic across the Canadian border to avoid taxes. Experts say that there might be some illegal trafficking if sales were restricted, but that it was unlikely to be a problem if, for example, cigarettes could be bought at pharmacies with a prescription, perhaps multiyear prescriptions like those for birth control.
John Banzhaf, director of Action on Smoking and Health, said this solution would also help keep cigarettes out of the hands of children, and would get smokers to see a doctor once in a while about their habit, while remaining available at every corner pharmacy.
Dr. Kenneth E. Warner, chairman of the department of health economics at the University of Michigan, said there would always be a few people who wanted to smoke, no matter what, and for those people "we should make it possible for them to smoke in the privacy of their homes if they want to."
Dr. Henningfield added that there were already some good treatments to help people quit smoking -- behavioral therapy combined with nicotine patches, for example -- and more were being studied, like nicotine inhalers or lozenges.

HEADLINE: Wine Talk

BYLINE: By Frank J. Prial 

 BODY:
BECAUSE of my position on wine prices over the years, this column is regularly cited by consumers who believe that for too long, wine has cost too much in far too many restaurants.
Invariably, restaurant owners have responded that the public doesn't understand how high their costs are and that a healthy return on wine sales is a must if they are to show a profit. 
 The debate heated up recently when two critics took on the restaurants in a wine magazine only to be challenged in a six-page, point-by-point letter from an articulate -- and successful -- young restaurant owner.
Writing in the March issue of Wine Enthusiast, Nathan Chroman suggested, at least half seriously, that restaurants allow guests to bring their own wine and charge them a corkage fee for opening their bottles. For every bottle brought in, the restaurant could collect $10 and have no further problems with storage, inventory or trained personnel.
Citing instances where wines that cost the restaurant $5 are sold for $20 or more and those that cost $10 are priced at $30 and up, Mr. Chroman asked, "Where else in the world of dining is there such a markup?"
Paul Gillette, a writer and consultant to the wine trade, also argued in the same issue for sensible pricing. If a restaurant gave its wine away free and thereby increased food profits fivefold from new business, he said, it would be foolish to keep charging for the wine.
He cited a client who, on his advice, slashed prices and in six months was selling 40 times as much wine in volume and 20 times as much in dollar value. "There is," he said, "a veritable army" of wine lovers eager to "bring new business to restaurants that offer an attractive wine-pricing policy."
Craig C. Shelton, who wrote the response, is the owner and chef at the Ryland Inn at Whitehouse, N.J., a highly praised country restaurant about 50 miles west of New York City. Markups, Mr. Shelton argued, differ from one type of restaurant to another.
"Diners get two or three dollars for three scrambled eggs that cost 22 cents," he wrote in his letter. Moreover, a $9 or $10 pizza may cost less than $1.60 in materials, while the french fries and sodas at a fast-food outlet "may be marked up 1,000 percent or more."
Some middle-level restaurants make 10 to 15 percent profit on food alone because their cooking is not labor-intensive and their other costs are lower.
These restaurants, Mr. Shelton said, usually mark up food four times the original cost. But luxury restaurants, he continued, lose money on food alone because of huge overhead and labor costs.
On a typical luxury menu for four people, costing $200, he said the restaurant would lose $90 if the guests did not order wine or liquor.
If the guests ordered $100 worth of wine and liquor with the same meal, he said, the restaurant would make $18.73, or a 6.24 percent profit.
Mr. Shelton argued that Americans are spoiled.
For years, he said, they have paid proportionally less for their food than any other industrialized nation in the world. Now, with a global economy in place, top-quality ingredients cost much the same anywhere, he said, but famous European restaurants often charge almost double what American restaurants ask for the same dish.
The true reason for resistance to wine markups, Mr. Shelton insisted, is a price threshold set by consumers who are not aware of the realities of luxury restaurant costs.
For example, he said, many would consider $1,800 an outrageous price on a wine list for a bottle of 1947 Chateau Cheval-Blanc, a famous Bordeaux property, but in fact, that price is cheaper than the replacement cost.
Similarly, he said, many American diners would find it unthinkable to pay $75 for a risotto with an ounce of white truffles, but that price may well be less than the cost of the truffles alone.
Mr. Shelton is young and earnest and may be forgiven for missing the touch of mischief in both Mr. Chroman's and Mr. Gillette's arguments. Certainly, he leaves himself open to criticism, or let's say good-humored ribbing, when he includes in the price of his $200 meal for four "china breakage (Limoges) -- $4.90; lawn care, snow removal -- $4.24, and music -- 45 cents."
Perhaps wine writers have been remiss in explaining, or at least airing the restaurateurs' side. Even so, how often is Mr. Shelton called on to replace this 1947 Cheval-Blanc?
Running a luxury restaurant means more than stocking impossibly expensive wines. Those days are over. Even connoisseurs want bargains.
At the same time, Mr. Gillette's free-wine fantasy has come to life. Cite, in the Time & Life Building, pours unlimited good wine free for guests who turn up after 8 P.M. on weeknights.
As for Mr. Chroman's "corkage" idea, it's provocative, but one can only hope that someday he's invited to a great restaurant and that his host brings along several bottles of some undistinguished stuff.

TASTINGS
Fetzer Petite Sirah Reserve, Mendocino, 1990; about $12.
 
Hollywood film promoters regularly send kits to critics that include reviews of varying lengths, complete with headlines designed to fit almost any newspaper. Favorable reviews, of course. Now, we have Fetzer Vineyards extending its hand to the harried critic with canned reviews of its own wines. Along with a bottle of its 1990 Petite Sirah Reserve comes a release with two flattering descriptions. One, the winery thoughtfully notes, is 10 words long, the other 30. What follows is neither. The wine is excellent, and like just about everything Fetzer makes, definitely worth sampling. Petite sirah is a big rich wine that is often clumsily handled by wine makers. Not here. It's tannic but smooth, concentrated but never heavy in the mouth or hard on the head. Alcohol is 12.5 percent, which is normal even for Beaujolais these days. The pundits say petite sirah is passe. This one could give the variety a new lease on life. It's just right to accompany pungent offerings from the barbecue.

HEADLINE: A Different Take on the 'Myth' of Inflation

 BODY:
To the Editor:
Laura D'Andrea Tyson, chairwoman of the Council of Economic Advisers, makes a case for low inflation ("Inflation: Myth and Reality," Op-Ed, April 15). She does not mention the Federal Reserve, but the article is clearly meant to send a message to the Fed. "The economy has turned in its best inflation performance in decades. . . . Favorable trends are continuing this year." But reasonable people, even supporters of low interest rates like myself, can put a different slant on the data. 
 Most economists will agree that price increases at the level of the consumer have been tamed, for now. But it is important to remember that:
* Consumer prices tend to be a lagging indicator. Industrial commodity prices tend to lead the cycle, and they have been showing strength.
* Monetary policy takes time to have an effect. It will be nine months to a year before interest rate increases have noticeable effects on real economic activity. Policymakers must look ahead to next year. Where will the economy be then?
Fortunately, there are many indications that the economy is improving rapidly. Jobs are being created. If the economy continues to add 200,000 jobs per month, this time next year the unemployment rate may be down to less than 6 percent, at the lower edge of Ms. Tyson's 5.9 to 6.3 percent danger zone. Capacity utilization is also improving and may be approaching 85 percent, causing bottlenecks and price rises in some industries. Evidence of increasing prices in industrial materials is appearing.
The pattern of interest rates is a striking confirmation of what business executives and financial economists are anticipating. From the low point last year long-term rates have gone up 150 basis points, though the Fed has raised short-term rates only 50 basis points. The difference reflects inflationary expectations.
Under the circumstances, gradual adjusting of monetary policy is indeed necessary if Ms. Tyson's projection of "a decade-long phase of steady growth and modest inflation" is to materialize.
 
F. GERARD ADAMS
Professor of Economics
University of Pennsylvania
Philadelphia, April 18, 1994

HEADLINE: A Different Take on the 'Myth' of Inflation;
Raise Taxes, Not Rates

 BODY:
To the Editor:
If the U.S. economy is growing too fast, why, when your Government is running a $200 billion annual deficit, would Americans choose to take the heat out by raising interest rates? 
 As we Canadians have learned to our sorrow, higher interest rates don't actually slow down the economy until they get so high that they bring it crashing down into a recession. In the meantime their main effect is to shift money around from borrowers (including the government) to lenders (including foreigners). If anything, higher rates contribute to inflation in the short term as business people try to maintain their profit margins in comparison with just putting their money in the bank.
Why not have your Government cool things down by raising taxes to reduce its deficit? A 0.5 percent or 1 percent surtax on all new loans would have the same dampening effect on new loan demand as raising interest rates by the same amount.
The fairest way to take out the heat would be to raise income tax rates. But that might mean that the people who control the Fed would have to pay their share of fighting inflation!
JORDAN GRANT
Toronto, April 18, 1994

HEADLINE: THE 37TH PRESIDENT: DIGNITARIES;
List of Overseas Guests for Nixon's Funeral Is Short

BYLINE: By R. W. APPLE Jr., Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: YORBA LINDA, Calif., April 26

 BODY:
Although international relations dominated Richard M. Nixon's long career, no foreign heads of state or government are expected for his funeral Wednesday at the library his friends built on the site of the failed citrus orchard where he grew up.
Mr. Nixon counted as his greatest accomplishments the normalization of relations with China and detente with the Soviet Union. But no high-powered delegation is coming from Beijing, which will be represented by a Deputy Prime Minister, Zou Jiahua. Russia, too, is sending only a Deputy Prime Minister, Aleksandr N. Shokhin. 
 President Clinton will be here, flying from Washington to California and back in 15 hours, and he will be joined by all four living former Presidents -- Gerald R. Ford, Jimmy Carter, Ronald Reagan and George Bush.
Five planeloads of senators and representatives, members of Mr. Clinton's Cabinet and onetime members of Mr. Nixon's, and some of the major figures in the Watergate scandal are also among those invited to the ceremony, for which 2,600 seats have been installed.
 
Agnew Is Expected
 Spiro T. Agnew, Mr. Nixon's first Vice President, who was forced to resign in a financial scandal in October 1973, refused after the former President's death last Friday to make any comment.
But a friend of the Nixon family said Mr. Agnew had asked the Nixon daughters, Julie Nixon Eisenhower and Tricia Nixon Cox, whether he would be welcome at the funeral and was told he would. He is therefore expected, although he has long felt that Mr. Nixon and his Justice Department made inadequate efforts to save his career.
Former Senator George McGovern of South Dakota, the man whom Mr. Nixon defeated in a landslide by carrying 49 states in 1972, is coming to Yorba Linda on Mr. Clinton's plane. Elliot L. Richardson, the patrician politician from Massachusetts who resigned as Attorney General on Oct. 20, 1973, as part of the "Saturday Night Massacre," will also be aboard.
Other veterans of the Nixon Cabinet and White House staff on Air Force One will be James R. Schlesinger, who served as Mr. Nixon's third Defense Secretary; William P. Rogers, Mr. Nixon's first Secretary of State; James T. Lynn, Secretary of Housing and Urban Development; Alexander M. Haig Jr., the Nixon White House chief of staff; Herbert Stein, chairman of the Council of Economic Advisers, and Senator Daniel Patrick Moynihan of New York, Mr. Nixon's innovative adviser on domestic affairs.
 
Watergate Cast to Come
 The chief organizer of the ceremonies here today and on Wednesday has been Ron Walker, Mr. Nixon's star advance man, who planned the former President's ground-breaking trip to Communist China in 1972.
Some of those cast as villains in the Watergate scandal are planning to come, old Nixon hands said, including Charles W. Colson, the master of political dirty tricks who once said he would run over his grandmother for Mr. Nixon. He now heads a ministry for prison inmates.
But some big Watergate names will be absent. Gerald Warren, the editor of The San Diego Union-Tribune, who was the deputy White House press secretary in the Nixon years, said he did not expect to see John D. Ehrlichman, who, with the late H. R. Haldeman, formed what was known as the "Berlin wall" around Mr. Nixon's office. He now lives in Santa Fe and writes books.
"There was bad blood between him and the Old Man," Mr. Warren said. "Some things he said, things he wrote, didn't sit well."
At a Nixon conference at Hofstra University on Long Island a few years ago, Jeb Stuart Magruder, another major Watergate figure, was shunned by other former Nixon staff members. His appearance here was also said to be unlikely.
 
What Might Have Been
 And John W. Dean 3d, the former counsel to the President whose testimony to the Senate Watergate Committee heavily incriminated Mr. Nixon?
Jack Brennan, Mr. Nixon's chief of staff in the first years after his resignation, rolled his eyes and said, "Ask another question." He added, "It would have to be a magnanimous gesture."
Mr. Nixon's two closest friends outside the Government as his Presidency crashed in flames, Robert Abplanalp and Bebe Rebozo, are both expected for the funeral. The former President attended a party given by Mr. Abplanalp in New York two days before he suffered the stroke that killed him.
American politicians of every ideological stripe have promised to attend -- from George W. Romney, the former Michigan Governor who challenged Mr. Nixon for the Republican Presidential nomination in 1968 after having asserted that he had been brainwashed by American briefers in Saigon, to Willie L. Brown Jr., the Speaker of the California Assembly who is one of the nation's leading liberals.
 
85 Nations to Be Represented
 The senior foreign officials who are planning to attend the funeral, the White House said, are Edward Heath, the former British Prime Minister; Toshiki Kaifu, the former Japanese Prime Minister, and former President Chaim Herzog of Israel. A total of 85 countries are to be represented at the funeral.
A number of foreign ministers in Europe and the Middle East who might have come to Yorba Linda were tied up in meetings with Secretary of State Warren Christopher, who is traveling abroad this week, a State Department official said. But he conceded that that did not fully explain why so few high-ranking officials had decided to honor a man with whom they shared the world stage for many years.
Mr. Clinton, who is to deliver one of the eulogies on Wednesday, plans no substantive conversations with any of the visiting foreign officials, a White House aide said. He might have done so, the aide explained, had high-level officials of present governments attended the funeral.
For John F. Kennedy's funeral in 1963, a total of 19 kings, queens, presidents and princes made the journey to Washington, including Charles de Gaulle of France and Haile Selassie of Ethiopia, along with many prime ministers and foreign ministers. President Lyndon B. Johnson held extended conferences with many of the visitors.
Ten years later, at Johnson's own funeral, also in Washington, foreign ministers dominated the overseas guest list. Again, there were talks involving them and State Department and other officials.

CORRECTION-DATE: April 28, 1994, Thursday

 CORRECTION:
An article yesterday about government leaders who gathered for the funeral of Richard M. Nixon misidentified the current hometown of John D. Ehrlichman. It is Atlanta, not Santa Fe.

HEADLINE: Fund Is Set Up to Stabilize Mexican Peso

BYLINE: By THOMAS L. FRIEDMAN, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, April 26

 BODY:
The United States, Mexico and Canada announced the formation today of an unusual multibillion-dollar fund to stabilize Mexico's currency and protect it from further onslaughts by global speculators.
The centerpiece of the new fund is the creation of a permanent $6.7 billion line of credit for the central bank of Mexico, with the money being put up by Mexico's partners in the North American Free Trade Agreement, the United States and Canada. 
 The creation of such a fund underscores the degree to which the United States, Canada and Mexico now see their economies -- and their economic futures -- as intimately intertwined. Increasingly, when countries enter into free trade pacts like the North American Free Trade Agreement they have to become involved in one another's economies. Steady growth in trade and investment among the three North American partners is impossible without a degree of currency stability.
Since Jan. 1, the Mexican peso has fallen 8 percent in value against the Canadian dollar and 6 percent against the United States dollar. That hurts trade, because the cost of everything Mexico or Canada wants to buy from the United States has increased, dampening their demand for American products.
The political turmoil in Mexico in recent months, coupled with the recent global turmoil in the bond markets, has played havoc with the Mexican peso. A peasant revolt in southern Mexico, the murder of a presidential candidate and the abductions of prominent businessmen combined to spur many investors to pull money out of Mexican stocks, bonds and banks, driving down the peso's value.
This, in turn, forced the Mexican Government to raise short-term interest rates to induce people to keep their money in pesos. The rising rates began to choke off the kind of economic growth that Canada and the United States expect from Mexico to make the North American trade agreement a lucrative three-way proposition.
The resulting decision to take some long-term steps to stabilize the Mexican currency was announced at a news conference in Washington this morning, involving the finance ministers and central bank chairmen of the three countries. The creation of a new consultative group, called the North American Financial Group, was also announced. It will oversee economic and financial issues affecting the North American partners.
"We'll establish regular consultations between our countries on economic and financial policy arrangements," said Treasury Secretary Lloyd Bentsen, who went on to announce the new arrangement to shore up the peso.
"It's a multibillion-dollar reciprocal credit facility in the form of a swap," Mr. Bentsen said. "Its purpose is to promote orderly exchange markets."
The swap facility will merge, expand and make permanent three lines of credit already in existence between the three countries. A credit line is a given amount of lending provided by a bank that a borrower can draw on in an emergency.
 
 
Colosio Assassination
 This fund will make permanent a temporary $6 billion credit line that the United States extended to Mexico on March 24 to calm jittery currency markets after the assassination of Luis Donaldo Colosio, the ruling party's presidential candidate. It will also expand a pre-existing credit line between Canada and Mexico to $730 million, from $145 million. And it will merge both of these with another established $2 billion credit line between the United States and Canada. Each country has the right to draw on the others' currency.
"This arrangement is a logical progression from trade and investment cooperation between the three countries to greater monetary and fiscal cooperation," said Robert D. Hormats, vice chairman of Goldman Sachs International.
The mere existence of such credit lines is supposed to reassure markets enough that the money will never actually have to be drawn by either Mexico or Canada. Such funds are psychological tools more than anything else, and they work best when they work least.
"I don't expect it to be drawn on more, but I think it's something that you put in place to have in the event that you get very volatile markets," said Paul Martin, the Finance Minister of Canada.
 

CORRECTION-DATE: April 28, 1994, Thursday

 CORRECTION:
Because of an editing error, an article in Business Day yesterday about the formation of a fund by the United States, Mexico and Canada to stabilize the Mexican peso misstated the decline in the value of the peso. Since Jan. 1, it has fallen 8 percent against the United States dollar, not the Canadian dollar. The Canadian dollar, not the peso, has fallen 6 percent against the United States dollar.
