HEADLINE: THE 37th PRESIDENT: THE OVERVIEW;
Nixon Buried on Note of Praise and Reconciliation

BYLINE: By MAUREEN DOWD, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: YORBA LINDA, Calif., April 27

 BODY:
To the heroic strains of "Victory at Sea," the theme that comforted Richard Milhous Nixon through his darkest moments, the nation paid final tribute today to the former President who fascinated, confounded, infuriated and enthralled Americans for half a century.
Gov. Pete Wilson, who was an advance man for Mr. Nixon's 1962 California campaign for governor, said in his eulogy, "It's hard to imagine a world without Richard Nixon." Indeed, it was hard to imagine that there would be no more comebacks or reincarnations, no more new Nixons, same old Nixons or mellower Nixons. 
 President Clinton, a Democratic President who came of political age amid the scalding years of Watergate and Vietnam, urged Americans to let Mr. Nixon's Sisyphean penance earn him final forgiveness: "May the day of judging President Nixon on anything less than his entire life and career come to a close."
The four living former Presidents, Gerald R. Ford, Jimmy Carter, Ronald Reagan and George Bush, and their wives looked on from the first row of seats at the first funeral for an American President since Lyndon B. Johnson's in 1973. Hillary Rodham Clinton, who early in her career worked as a lawyer for the House committee that investigated Watergate, was seated next to the President.
Mr. Nixon's coffin, covered with an American flag, rested on a catafalque during the funeral at the Richard Nixon Library and Birthplace. The skies were lowering and gray.
Choking back tears during his eulogy, former Secretary of State Henry A. Kissinger said that despite the "gruff pose" of Mr. Nixon, he would have been delighted by the outpouring of concern and praise from friends and adversaries this last week, in what he termed "the culmination of an astonishing life."
President Clinton smiled and nodded in agreement.
Mr. Kissinger described Mr. Nixon as "one of the seminal Presidents" in his conduct of foreign policy. "He stood on pinnacles that dissolved in the precipice," he said. "He achieved greatly and he suffered deeply. But he never gave up. In his solitude he envisaged a new international order that would reduce lingering enmities, strengthen historic friendships and give new hope to mankind. A visionary dream and possibilities conjoined."
The Rev. Billy Graham said in his sermon to the 3,000 guests that Mr. Nixon had given his final wave and thumbs-up sign to his doctor in New York Hospital-Cornell Medical Center after he was felled by a stroke as he was reading the galley proofs of his last book on foreign affairs.
"It was an example of fighting on and never giving up," Mr. Graham said of the man whose darker and better angels cast such a compelling shadow on the American character and consciousness.
The funeral was an oddly animated sort of "class reunion," as one former Nixon White House worker called it.
Peter Flanigan, an economic adviser to President Nixon, said to Clifford M. Hardin, who had been the Secretary of Agriculture, "It was a hell of a ride."
"The old man must be looking down and taking a delicious revenge," said Gen. Vernon A. Walters, a military aide in the Nixon White House, guessing that Mr. Nixon would have delighted in the adulation and lionizing of the last few days.
There, on the lawn in front of the bungalow where Mr. Nixon was born, people who had not seen one another for 20 years gathered to pay their respects before the burial following the service: aides who suffered with Mr. Nixon through the Watergate scandal and others who went to jail for their parts in it. There were secretaries from the Nixon White House press office, advance men for the 1960 campaign and old Hollywood cronies like Buddy Ebsen, Bob Hope and Red Skelton.
 
20 Years of Resentment
 Former Vice President Spiro T. Agnew, who resigned in 1973 after pleading no contest to a charge of tax evasion, was swept up in the vivid mood of what some called reconciliation and others revisionism.
"I decided after 20 years of resentment to put it all aside," Mr. Agnew said before the ceremony began.
Through the eulogies, benedictions and homilies, the word "Watergate" was never mentioned. Even President Clinton only alluded to Mr. Nixon's "mistakes." Names that had faded into the scandalous past of Watergate were back: Herbert W. Kalmbach and Gordon C. Strachan and Charles W. Colson, the Nixon loyalists; and Elliot L. Richardson, the Attorney General who resigned in the Saturday Night Massacre.
Before the funeral began, the guests mingled happily, creating a living tableau of history. Adm. James B. Stockdale, the former prisoner of war in Vietnam who ran for Vice President on the Ross Perot ticket in 1992, rubbed shoulders with Farah Diba, the glamorous one-time empress of Iran. Former Vice President Dan Quayle walked past George McGovern, whom Mr. Nixon defeated in one of the great political landslides, in 1972. Adnan Khashoggi, the Saudi Arabian financier, and G. Gordon Liddy, one of the overseers of the Watergate burglary, his head now shaved, were also there.
In his eulogy, Mr. Clinton quoted from Mr. Nixon's 1968 speech accepting the Republican nomination. He spoke of "the force of a driving dream" that had carried the former President from his humble beginnings here onto the world stage and added: "He made mistakes and they, like his accomplishments, are part of his life and record. But the enduring lesson of Richard Nixon is that he never gave up being part of the action and passion of the times."
He said that judging by their last phone conversation and the letter he had received from Mr. Nixon last month, he was sure that Mr. Nixon's "spirit was very much alive to the very end."
Senator Bob Dole of Kansas, in beginning his eulogy, said he believed that "the second half of the 20th century will be known as the age of Nixon." The American people, he said, "love a fighter, and in Dick Nixon they found a gallant one."
He recalled that Mr. Nixon once told his daughter Julie, "I just get up every morning to confound my enemies." At that Julie, a few feet away, broke into a wide, delighted grin.
Senator Dole's voice cracked as he quoted Mr. Nixon as saying, "In the end what matters is that you have always lived life to the hilt." Right at the end, Senator Dole lost control and sobbed as he walked away from the lectern.
 
'Never Left California'
 Governor Wilson said, "Dick Nixon's heart, shaped by the grit and mores of this small town, never left California."
In the front row on one side sat the Nixon family, including daughters, Julie Nixon Eisenhower and Tricia Nixon Cox, their husbands and Mr. Nixon's grandchildren: Jennie Eisenhower, 15; Alex Richard Eisenhower, 13; Melanie Eisenhower, 9, and Christopher Nixon Cox, 15. His only surviving brother, Edward Calvert Nixon, 63, was there, too.
On the other side of the front row were the four former Presidents: Mr. Ford, who spoke of "our long national nightmare" as he succeeded the disgraced Mr. Nixon; Mr. Carter, whose candidacy to oust Mr. Ford was buoyed by American anger at Mr. Ford's pardon of Mr. Nixon; Mr. Reagan, whose national conventions viewed Mr. Nixon as a political albatross, and Mr. Bush, who as national chairman worked to put the Watergate legacy behind the Republican Party in the 1970's.
A total of 47 senators, 64 representatives and 11 former members of Congress made the journey from Washington today aboard Air Force One and five other Air Force planes.
On Air Force One, Mr. McGovern captured the ambiance, telling reporters: "My own career has been so intertwined with his that I really had the feeling that an old friend has left the scene. That may seem hard to believe in view of the bitterness of that 1972 campaign, but I made my peace with him years ago."
Mr. Nixon's black stone headstone in the garden, placed next to that of his wife, Pat, who died of lung cancer last year, used a quote from his first inaugural address: "The greatest honor history can bestow is the title of peacemaker."
 
Sensing Love in the Heart
 Mrs. Nixon's headstone reads: "Even when people can't speak your language, they can tell when you have love in your heart."
A band from the Marine Corps Air Station at El Toro, Calif., and the United States Navy band Sea Chanters from Washington provided the music, including the "Battle Hymn of the Republic" and "America the Beautiful."
Joining Mr. Graham in officiating was Capt. William L. Perry, the Navy's chief of chaplains. Mr. Nixon served in the Navy in the Pacific during World War II.
At the end of the service, planes from the 388th Fighter Wing at Hill Air Force Base in Utah flew low over Yorba Linda in the missing-man formation. A 50-gun salute was fired by Marine howitzers from Camp Pendleton, near San Diego. Taps was sounded by a lone bugler, Paul Johnson, musician first-class of the Navy.
Two American flags that had covered the wooden coffin, folded into triangles so that the blue field and the white stars were on the outside, were presented by an Army officer to Tricia Cox and Julie Eisenhower. Mrs. Eisenhower wiped away tears with a tissue, and Edward Nixon broke down as the family left the service.
After the funeral, a reception was held in the rotunda of the Nixon library. The crowd quickly dispersed, many for dinner in the Los Angeles or Newport Beach area.

GRAPHIC: Photo: A flag that draped Richard M. Nixon's coffin being presented to his daughter Julie Nixon Eisenhower yesterday. His daughter Tricia Nixon Cox was at left in front of the house where their father was born. (CNN)

HEADLINE: Hata Named Prime Minister of a Shaky Japanese Government

BYLINE: By JAMES STERNGOLD, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: TOKYO, Thursday, April 28

 BODY:
After a two-day delay because of intense infighting, Tsutomu Hata was appointed Japan's 51st Prime Minister this morning by Emperor Akihito in a brief ceremony at the Imperial Palace, but he appears likely to head a weak and perhaps short-lived coalition Government.
Mr. Hata has tried but failed so far to mend his conservative party's rift with the Socialists, who quit the governing coalition in anger early Tuesday morning, depriving it of its majority in Parliament. The Socialists were furious after the conservative parties in the coalition, including Mr. Hata's, hastily joined together to resist any leftward turn in the Government's economic or security policies. 
 Thus, Japan appeared to be moving toward what many here have long dreaded, growing ideological strife and a minority government unable to deal firmly with the country's deep economic problems. Mr. Hata is Japan's sixth Prime Minister in a rocky five years of corruption scandals and a far-reaching political realignment.
Mr. Hata met this morning with Tomiichi Murayama, the head of the Socialists, but they were not able to achieve a reconciliation.
Later in the day Mr. Hata named his cabinet, putting a close ally, Hiroshi Kumagai, the outgoing Minister for International Trade and Industry, into the important job of cabinet secretary, which will make him the Government's spokesman.
Another critical post, Foreign Minister, was given to Koji Kakizawa, a conservative like Mr. Hata who recently defected from the Liberal Democratic Party. Mr. Kakizawa supports an idea the Socialists fiercely resist, Japan's participation in collective security arrangments, including use of its military in overseas peacekeeping operations.
In practical terms, the new Government, which controls just 181 seats in the 511-seat lower house of Parliament, is unlikely to be able to push through any bold legislation on the critical issues, like ending the recession, responding to American demands that Japan open its markets and reduce its trade surplus, and trying to halt North Korea's suspected nuclear weapons program.
Japan begins a week of holidays Friday, after which the Parliament is expected to pass the overdue budget for the fiscal year beginning April 1. The Socialists helped draw it up and so are expected to support it.
After that, many experts have said, the prospect is for growing ideological strife and a quick election -- just before an important meeting of the Group of Seven industrial countries in July and a self-imposed deadline for producing a market-opening package for the Clinton Administration.
The parties underwent a day of bizarre maneuverings today, underscoring the state of flux after 38 years of one-party, conservative rule ended last summer.
The Socialists were wooed ardently by Mr. Hata, who reportedly told his allies: "It is indispensable for us to maintain unity with the Socialists. I will do my utmost to bring the party back into the fold."
The Socialists, however, insisted that the right-wing policies and authoritarian methods of Mr. Hata's key strategist, Ichiro Ozawa, made such a reunion impossible.
No sooner had the Socialists set out their stand than they met and agreed to work closely with the conservative party that was ousted last summer, the Liberal Democrats. It was an unconvincing alliance, but it underscored the bitterness of the rift.
"This is a case of, 'My enemy's enemy is my friend,' " said Asahiko Mihara, whose conservative faction is estranged from Mr. Ozawa.
Even if Mr. Hata, who is known as a conciliator, manages to lure the Socialists back with plum Cabinet assignments, few expect the marriage of convenience to last. If anything, the ideological divide in the Parliament is growing more pronounced. Both those on the far left and the far right are finding themselves increasingly isolated from the realignment of the parties toward the political center.

HEADLINE: Venezuela Proposes Opening Oil Industry to Private Investment

BYLINE: By JAMES BROOKE, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: CARACAS, Venezuela, April 27

 BODY:
Breaking a 20-year taboo, Venezuela's new Government today proposed opening its oil industry, Latin America's largest, to private investment.
Oil "will no longer be an exclusive affair of the state," a new economic blueprint stated, reversing a nationalization policy adopted in the early 1970's. "We propose, therefore, that the oil sector be opened without limitations to private enterprise, national and foreign." 
 Turning to the state's second-largest area of economic activity, the Corporacion Venezuela de Guayana, the Government said that this steel and aluminum complex "should be opened without limits to private investment."
The proposals indicate a clear free-market direction for the new Government of President Rafael Caldera Rodriguez, a 78-year-old politician who was inaugurated in February and who campaigned on a vaguely populist platform.
"We aspire to a free and competitive economy," Mr. Caldera said, hours before the 17-page economic blueprint was released. "We will continue to privatize what is necessary."
Opening the oil sector to private investment would require congressional approval of changes in a 1975 law that created Venezuela's oil company, Petroleos de Venezuela S.A., as a state monopoly.
Business confidence was shaken by the resignation on Tuesday night of Ruth de Krivoy, an economist who had battled for two years to keep the Central Bank independent.
The resignation followed four months of bad economic news from Venezuela, the largest source of imported oil for the United States after Saudi Arabia. In quick succession, the nation's second largest bank, Banco Latino, failed in January, and then the price of Venezuela's oil slumped to $11 a barrel -- well below the forecast level of $15 a barrel.
With the Caldera Government sending conflicting signals about its economic policy direction, the first quarter saw new foreign investment drop by 30 percent and the nation's foreign reserves drop by 17 percent, to $10.5 billion.
"The country is going through its most severe financial crisis in history," said Pedro Palma, general manager of the office here of Booz, Allen & Hamilton, a management consulting firm. "The Banco Latino failure dragged along a lot of other banks. Today 40 percent of the financial system is in the hands of the state."
Today, in a concerted counterattack, economic officials outlined an orthodox free-market recovery plan.
In addition to cutting the budget by 10 percent, the fiscal deficit is to be eased through an aggressive privatization program. During the next year, the state is to sell an airline, hotels, thermo-electric power plants and racetracks.
Attacking a long sacrosanct state fiefdom, most of the Corporacion Venezuela de Guayana is to be converted into joint ventures controlled by private capital.
"Everything at Guayana, except for the mines, will go to mixed companies with private majority control," Julio Sosa Rodriguez, Venezuela's Finance Minister, said in an interview here today. Next Monday, Mr. Sosa, widely considered to be Venezuela's most powerful Cabinet member, is to explain the plan to foreign investors in Washington.
Today, in an address to international business executives here, Mr. Sosa pledged that the new Government "will open new spaces for private foreign and national capital investment, including mining, energy, petrochemicals, tourism, agriculture and financial services."
Although Mr. Caldera only controls about one quarter of the seats in Congress, he is expected to be able to implement much of the program. He has enjoyed public approval ratings of about 75 percent since his inauguration on Feb. 2. Last week, Congress passed a law that gives the President special powers for 30 days to push through tax and economic bills by decree.

HEADLINE: Doctor Convicted of Refusing to Give Aid

BYLINE: By SETH FAISON 

 BODY:
A doctor was convicted yesterday of refusing to admit a woman in labor to Harlem Hospital, forcing ambulance attendants to deliver her baby in the hospital's waiting room. A jury in State Supreme Court in Manhattan also found that the doctor later falsified hospital records to cover up his actions.
The doctor, Peter Anyakora, became the first physician to be prosecuted under a 1983 New York State law that bars hospitals from turning away a patient in an emergency, a practice commonly called dumping. 
 Edward J. Kuriansky, the New York State special prosecutor for medical fraud control, said that while civil lawsuits against hospitals are common in the 26 states that have laws against dumping, criminal prosecutions against an individual doctor are rare. Harlem Hospital was not named in the case.
The jury convicted Dr. Anyakora of turning away Charlesetta Brown early one morning in September 1991 when she was in an advanced stage of labor. Dr. Anyakora said the hospital was too crowded to take her. Emergency Medical Service workers were forced to deliver her son, Jeffrey, on a stretcher outside the hospital emergency room and to wrap him in a paper examination gown because no blankets were provided.
On a more serious charge, the jury convicted Dr. Anyakora of later falsifying business records and tampering with evidence to disguise his actions. Turning away a patient in an emergency is a misdemeanor, with a maximum penalty of one year in prison and a $1,000 fine. But the tampering charges are felonies, and each carries a maximum of four years in prison. Mr. Kuriansky said that because the two felonies stem from the same event, Dr. Anyakora faces a combined maximum of five years in prison. He is scheduled to be sentenced by Judge Bernard J. Fried on July 12.
Dr. Anyakora argued that the Emergency Medical Service was notified that the hospital was overloaded with obstetrics patients and that new patients should have been diverted to other hospitals. Dr. Anyakora also said he had asked a nurse to attend to Ms. Brown because he was being called to treat another patient.
But Mr. Kuriansky said Dr. Anyakora's case was undermined by testimony from a hospital administrator that Dr. Anyakora had altered hospital records months later to make it look as though he had been called away to attend to a medical emergency with a patient who he said was in hypoglycemic shock. Among the clues to the alterations, Mr. Kuriansky said, was an incorrect date on a hospital form.
Dr. Anyakora, 35, was licensed to practice medicine in New York State in August 1991, when he began a one-year residency at Harlem Hospital, one month before the incident. He was the hospital's chief resident in obstetrics and gynecology.
In June 1992, five months after he was charged with criminal wrongdoing in the case, he left Harlem Hospital, where disciplinary action against him was delayed until after a resolution of the criminal charges against him.
While awaiting trial, Dr. Anyakora spent a year as a volunteer at the National Institute of Health outside Washington, was unemployed for another year and began working at a clinic in Avon Park, Fla., last November.
The convictions do not automatically bar him from medical practice, but Mr. Kuriansky said that the New York State Board of Professional Medical Conduct would be formally notified of the court's finding, as will Florida authorities, and each will review his case.
 
Unavailable for Comment
 Dr. Anyakora's lawyer, Robert Zatortski, did not reply to repeated telephone messages left at his home last night.
The anti-dumping law was intended, in large part, to prevent private hospitals from denying admission to patients who could not pay for their health care. Sponsored by former Assemblyman Alan G. Hevesi of Queens, now the New York City Comptroller, the law was enacted in 1983 following a series of deaths of individuals brought to New York City Hospitals and denied admission.
John Ronches, executive director of the Committee of Interns and Residents, a union that represents the doctors at Harlem Hospital, said he thought it ironic that the law was first used instead against a doctor at a public hospital where the medical staff is badly overworked.
While Mr. Ronches said after the initial charges were made in 1992 that he felt Dr. Anyakora was being made a scapegoat for chronic problems at Harlem Hospital, he said yesterday that it would be inappropriate to comment in detail after the jury had made a decision.
 
Overloaded Hospital
 During the two-week trial, Mr. Zatortski pointed out that when Emergency Medical Service staff brought Ms. Brown to Harlem Hospital at 5:25 A.M. on Sept. 22, 1991, the hospital had already notified E.M.S. that it was too overloaded to accept new patients.
But the E.M.S. workers assisting Ms. Brown told the hospital staff that they had not been so informed, according to testimony, and pointed out that it was an emergency because Ms. Brown was bleeding and in an advanced stage of labor. The anti-dumping law specifies that E.M.S. workers can overrule instructions by hospital staff to go elsewhere if it is an emergency, prosecutors pointed out.
Ms. Brown testified that she had received prenatal care at Harlem Hospital and she was scheduled to deliver Jeffrey there. He was born in good health and a half-hour after the birth, both mother and baby were admitted to the hospital.

HEADLINE: Aiming at Cuomo, Pataki Starts TV Ads

BYLINE: By KEVIN SACK, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: ALBANY, April 27

 BODY:
Trying to prove he is the leading Republican gubernatorial candidate, State Senator George E. Pataki unveiled the first television commercials of the 1994 campaign today and aimed his fire directly at Gov. Mario M. Cuomo.
In a pair of spartan 30-second advertisements, Mr. Pataki, 48, a fiscal conservative from Putnam County, blames Mr. Cuomo for a worsening state economy and a decline in public safety. Those are the two issues that polls say most concern New Yorkers. 
 In the commercial about crime, Mr. Pataki, a lawyer and farmer who is a former State Assemblyman, highlights Mr. Cuomo's annual veto of the death penalty and vows to restore it if elected. The ad about the economy charges that Mr. Cuomo raised taxes and state spending and points out that Mr. Pataki has voted against the state budget each year he has been in the Legislature.
 
Rivals for Nominations
 In both commercials, Mr. Pataki describes himself as a "conservative Republican," an apparent appeal to the Republican and Conservative Party advocates who will meet at party conventions in late May and early June. The ad is being broadcast primarily in Republican strongholds.
Mr. Pataki's need to stake out conservative ground is being driven by his competition for the Republican and Conservative nominations with Herbert London, who ran as the Conservative gubernatorial nominee in 1990. There now are four announced Republican candidates and a fifth, Evan G. Galbraith, is expected to declare his candidacy on Thursday.
Mr. Pataki hopes to emerge from the Republican and Conservative conventions with a front-runner's momentum. His advertising strategy seems intended to demonstrate the seriousness of his campaign and his willingness to take on Mr. Cuomo.
"I think the commercial proves that we've got a campaign that's up and running," said Robert H. Ryan, Mr. Pataki's campaign manager.
Mr. Ryan said the campaign would spend about $100,000 to broadcast the ads over the next few weeks. They will begin airing on Thursday on two cable channels on Long Island -- CNN and Channel 12 -- showing them 37 times between Thursday and Sunday, he said. The commercials then will be shown in selected upstate markets starting next week.
Mr. London, who has been battling Mr. Pataki for Republican leaders' endorsements, assessed his opponent's ad campaign as "an effort to establish legitimacy for a campaign that many people mock as a surrogate for Al D'Amato." Senator Alfonse M. D'Amato is backing Mr. Pataki and has pledged $400,000 of his own campaign funds to help him.
Mr. London does not plan radio or television ads before the conventions. But Morton B. Lawrence, a spokesman for Bill Green, a declared candidate, said Mr. Green, a former Congressman, might begin radio advertising in coming weeks. Mr. Cuomo has yet to start an ad campaign.
Mr. Pataki's ads state that "it's Mario Cuomo's fault" that "we're less safe than we used to be" and that "our economy is getting worse." But statistics do not fully bear out Mr. Pataki's claims. For instance, while the violent crime rate increased in New York from 1983, Mr. Cuomo's first year in office, to 1990, it decreased from 1990 to 1992.
Of Mr. Pataki's ads, Mr. Cuomo's campaign spokesman, Joel R. Benenson, said, "It sounds like Howard Stern without the road work." He was referring to the Mr. Stern's Libertarian platform to sign the death penalty into law, improve toll collections and require road construction to be done at night.

GRAPHIC: Chart/Photo: "THE AD CAMPAIGN: Race for Governor: Pataki Out of the Gate"
 
CANDIDATE -- George E. Pataki
 
PRODUCER -- Russo Marsh and Associates, Sacramento, Calif., and Alexandria, Va.
 
VISUAL -- The ad is deliberately simple, featuring a photograph of Gov. Mario M. Cuomo on the left and a photograph of Senator Pataki on the right. The script appears on the screen in white type as it is spoken by a narrator. The picture of Mr. Cuomo falls forward with a creaking thud as the narrator says, "It's time for a change."
 
THE SCRIPT -- "Mario Cuomo vs. George Pataki. Mario Cuomo raised taxes and state spending, driving out jobs. Senator George Pataki voted against Mario Cuomo's tax and spend budgets every time. Our economy is getting worse and it's Mario Cuomo's fault. It's time for a change. George Pataki is the conservative Republican who will defeat Mario Cuomo, cut taxes and reduce spending. George Pataki means jobs for New York. George Pataki for Governor."
 
ANALYSIS -- In his first ad, Mr. Pataki seems to be staking out conservative ground in the battle for support from the Republican and Conservative parties. In an effort to impress the party activists who will vote at the party conventions in late May and early June, the ads seek to demonstrate Mr. Pataki's seriousness as a candidate and his willingness to take on Mr. Cuomo. But Mr. Pataki's assertions that Mr. Cuomo raised taxes, drove out jobs and is to blame for a worsening economy are deceptive and incomplete.
Measured as a proportion of personal income, state taxes stayed virtually the same between 1982-83 ($74.40 per $1,000 in personal income, 14th among states) and 1991-92 ($74.21, 22d among states), according to the Census Bureau. Even when state and local taxes are combined, the burden has stayed about the same -- $153.49 per $1,000 in personal income in 1982-83 (3d among states) versus $151.54 in 1990-91 (2d among states). Some taxes, like those levied on businesses, cigarettes and petroleum have been raised by Mr. Cuomo and the Legislature, which has a Republican-controlled Senate. Others, like the personal income tax, have been cut significantly.
Mr. Pataki is correct that Mr. Cuomo raised state spending. New York's general fund budget increased from $17.5 billion in 1982-83 to $31.9 billion in 1993-94. But it is difficult to assess Mr. Cuomo's share of the culpability for "driving out jobs" during a nationwide recession. The state lost 517,000 jobs between 1989 and 1983, but has started to regain jobs slowly in the first three months of this year. And over the course of Mr. Cuomo's tenure, from 1983 to 1993, the state gained a net of 422,000 jobs. KEVIN SACK

HEADLINE: Christopher Asks Arab Lands To Extend the Iraqi Embargo

BYLINE: By STEVEN GREENHOUSE, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: RIYADH, Saudi Arabia, April 27

 BODY:
Secretary of State Warren Christopher came to the Saudi capital today to rally several oil-producing gulf states behind extending the trade embargo against Iraq, seeking a counterweight to the growing number of countries who want to see the embargo eased.
With France and Russia pushing to ease the 32-month-old embargo, Mr. Christopher told the half-dozen Arab Foreign Ministers that maintaining the sanctions was vital to press Iraq to comply with United Nations resolutions calling on it to recognize Kuwait's borders and eliminate its weapons of mass destruction. 
 Mr. Christopher met with the Ministers of the Gulf Cooperation Council, which includes Saudi Arabia and five other Arab states. "We join today with our gulf partners in unanimously and strongly expressing our common resolve to maintain the sanctions until Iraq complies fully," he said, referring to the United Nations resolutions.
 
Partial Lifting Is Urged
 The United Nations Security Council has imposed a trade embargo on Iraq, except for imports of food and medicine, until the Government of President Saddam Hussein takes several steps: recognizing Kuwait's borders, stop promoting terrorism, scrapping weapons of mass destruction and stop repressing its people.
Even though French and Russian officials concede that Baghdad still falls short in several of these areas, they are urging a partial lifting of the embargo, probably with regard to Iraq's oil exports. The reason is that they believe Iraq has made sufficient progress in eliminating its biological, chemical and nuclear threats. The Security Council is scheduled to debate the Iraq embargo in May.
According to Administration officials, the French want to ease the embargo because they see commercial opportunities in Iraq and want to ease the pain that Iraqis feel as a result of the sanctions. As for Russia, several officials said, it sees eventual export opportunities to Iraq and is eager to help Iraq repay some of its debts to Russia.
Explaining the purpose of Mr. Christopher's visit to Riyadh, a senior Administration official said, "Coming here was important because many of the countries thinking about easing the embargo are very sensitive to the attitudes of the gulf states."
 
A Joint News Conference
 Having seen Iraq invade Kuwait, the gulf states by in large remain hostile toward Baghdad, but they are not deaf to suggestions that the Iraqi embargo hurts their own economies as well as millions of innocent Iraqis.
In a joint news conference with the Saudi Foreign Minister, Prince Saud al-Faisal, Mr. Christopher addressed these concerns, saying: "I want to send a message to the people of Iraq. We have no interest in prolonging your suffering. We would like to see a united and democratic Iraq resume its rightful place among the community of nations. The problem lies with the brutal regime in Baghdad."
A monitoring commission set up by the Security Council is scheduled to report on Thursday on Iraq's compliance with the United Nations resolutions. On Tuesday Iraq agreed with the Commission Chairman, Rolf Ekeus of Sweden, to speed up creation of an arms monitoring program, a move United Nations officials said was positive.
Prince Saud said Arab nations were examining the question of phasing out their boycott of Israel, saying they first wanted to see carried out the framework agreement of last September in which Israel promised to allow limited Palestinian autonomy in the occupied Gaza Strip and in Jericho in the West Bank. Israel and the Palestine Liberation Organization are in intense talks to complete arrangements. Mr. Christopher is scheduled to meet in Cairo on Thursday with the P.L.O. chairman, Yasir Arafat, and the Israeli Foreign Minister, Shimon Peres.
 
Part of Boycott May Be Eased
 If the Arab countries are pleased with how Palestinian autonomy is carried out, a senior Administration official said, they might be prepared to end some aspects of the boycott, specifically ending the boycott of non-Israeli companies and their subsidiaries that do business with Israel.
At an airport news conference punctuated by thunder and lightning and a rare desert storm, Mr. Christopher said he met King Fahd for 75 minutes and they discussed the Mideast peace effort, the war in Bosnia and other topics.
Mr. Christopher used part of his session with the King to pursue President Clinton's favorite type of diplomacy: pocket book diplomacy. He urged King Fahd to award a contract of more than $4 billion to AT&T to modernize the over-stretched phone system in Saudi Arabia and add more than a million new phone lines.
On Tuesday, President Clinton sent King Fahd a letter, saying, "I hope AT&T, which has long been a market leader, will receive every opportunity to establish itself as Saudi Arabia's preferred partner, both for quality and cost."
AT&T is competing against other telecommunications giants, including Northern Telecom of Canada, Alcatel of France and Siemens of Germany.
In February, after an intensive sales pitch by numerous Cabinet secretaries reminding the Saudis of America's pivotal role in the Persian Gulf war, Saudi Arabia ordered $6 billion in passenger jets from Boeing and McDonnell Douglas, which beat the European Airbus Industrie consortium in the competition.

HEADLINE: COMPANY REPORTS;
MINNESOTA MINING & MFG. CO. (MMM,N)

 BODY:
The Minnesota Mining and Manufacturing Company said yesterday that first-quarter earnings fell 7.3 percent after it took a $35 million pretaxcharge to settle breast-implant litigation. The company reported net income of $306 million, or 72 cents a share. The earnings would have totaled about 77 cents a share without the charge. Per-share amounts reflect a 2-for-1 stock split last month.
Revenue rose 3.3 percent, to $3.632 billion. 
 Wall Street analysts had expected Minnesota Mining to earn 74 cents a share from operations, according to Zacks Investment Research.
Minnesota Mining, based in St. Paul, announced the litigation charge on April 11. It will contribute $325 million to a settlement fund in the next three years. The company has not made breast implants since 1984, when it sold McGhan Medical to the Inamed Corporation.
Minnesota Mining's products range from Post-It brand notepads and Scotch tape to sandpaper, computer floppy disks and laser imagers.
"Earnings were helped by a 6 percent gain in sales volume and by productivity improvement efforts," said Livio D. DeSimone, the chairman and chief executive. "However, economic softness in Europe and negative currency effects continued to hold back our earnings growth." (Bloomberg Business News)

HEADLINE: St. Pierre Journal;
A Seagirt Sliver of France Fears for Its Lifeline

BYLINE: By CLYDE H. FARNSWORTH, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: ST. PIERRE, St. Pierre and Mique lon

 BODY:
Twelve miles off Newfoundland's southern coast, a French official in a blue military shirt stamps passports "Port de St. Pierre," and Renaults and Citroens edge up the cobbled streets.
Early morning is fragrant with croissants and baguettes. Brasseries serve up mussels and skate fish wings, and the radio plays the chansons of the local Belle Riviere rock band. 
 France's last foothold in North America is a windswept archipelago of lichen-carpeted rocks, gnarled junipers and well-tended, brightly painted, hillside houses where 6,200 people live, most of them on the French public payroll.
The colony, roughly the size of New York City, consists of three main islands -- St. Pierre, the population and economic center, and Grande Miquelon and Petite Miquelon -- linked by a sandy isthmus. St. Pierre looks and sounds like a French village. The language has the soft lilt of Brittany or the Loire, and the residents identify with France, not Quebec.
Some 230 years after losing Canada, France lavishes upward of $25 million a year on the islanders to keep them happy.
In an earlier day of prosperity, the American Prohibition years of the 1920's, the islands were a center of bootleg liquor operations, and Al Capone frequented the waterfront cafes here. Now the colony lives off the French taxpayer.
The Government, which employs half the colony's working population, pays for electricity, water and medical services. Public employees -- from bureaucrats to the 40 gendarmes whose kepis are as distinctive as the flapping tricolor -- earn 1.6 times as much as their counterparts in France.
The state has spent millions of dollars on a cultural and sports center with an Olympic-size pool, gym, theater and art gallery. More millions have gone into the 1,000-seat hockey stadium.
The Culture Ministry flies in popular Paris singers and actors, sponsors exhibits by Parisian artists and bankrolls production of frothy boulevard comedies.
For most of April, the St. Pierrais savor white lies and amorous indiscretions in a chateau in Perigord, as depicted in the hoary comedy "J'y Suis . . . J'y Reste!" ("I'm Here . . . I'm Staying!"), with music by a 37-year-old local composer, Alain Lafitte.
Yet as they applaud their comedies, dine out in their brasseries and charge their peppy vehicles up and down the Place du General de Gaulle, many St. Pierrais wonder how long the good times will last.
For years, fishing had been an economic mainstay. An international moratorium on endangered stocks of cod and flounder two years ago ended employment for 400 of the local fishermen and 150 workers at the local fish processing plant -- a fifth of the working population.
Paris gives them generous unemployment assistance. But facing its own fiscal problems, Paris has decreed that it will end on May 31.
France keeps the islands as a symbol of past glories. President Charles de Gaulle, visiting St. Pierre in 1967, called the islands "the darling baby of mother France" and assured the St. Pierrais, "We will never abandon you."
It is nearly a quarter-century after de Gaulle's death. Now, whenever French Government officials visit St. Pierre, said Aurelien Claireaux, a local radio journalist, they are bombarded with questions about France's intentions. "They say there is no doubt about the long-term commitment to St. Pierre," he said, "but people are still nervous."
Albert Pen, Mayor of St. Pierre for 23 years and the region's Senator in Paris, predicts a "bad climate" unless new jobs can be found.
"People are used to a certain level of life," the 63-year-old politician said. "Take that away, and there will be trouble in the households."
To put some fishermen back to work, Paris has already approved about $35 million for a new and larger airport, which would employ about 60 people.
The Mayor thinks a casino would help, but the Bishop of St. Pierre and Miquelon, Francois Maurer, argues that a casino would introduce foreign criminal elements and be "disastrous." Most people doubt one will be built.
The Mayor and local businessmen are looking at other options, and they hope to attract foreign private investment by promoting the islands as a tax haven. "There are a number of opportunities, especially in developing new hotels," said Pierre Emmanuel Paturel, 64, a retired hotel owner and business consultant.
Jean-Claude Moraze, the grandson of one of Al Capone's trading partners, hopes to develop a swordfishing fleet, which would not be affected by the cod moratorium.
Many St. Pierrais fear that if the financial burdens get too great, France, preoccupied by events in Europe and demands at home, may cut its North American strings.
Didier Gil, the Algerian-born editor of a weekly newsletter, said: "They could cut us off tomorrow. Remember, our whole population could fit in one section of a soccer stadium in Paris."

GRAPHIC: Photo: Some 230 years after losing Canada, France lavishes millions on St. Pierre and Miguelon, the tiny foothold it retains in North America. Nevertheless, the inhabitants of the three islands off Newfoundland fear France will cut them adrift. An old farmhouse in a lonely setting outside the town of St. Pierre. (Elisavietta Ritchie for The New York Times)
 
Map of Canada showing location of St. Pierre.

HEADLINE: THE MEDIA BUSINESS;
Walt Disney Reinventing Itself

BYLINE: By CALVIN SIMS, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: LOS ANGELES, April 27

 BODY:
Widely considered the most successful entertainment enterprise in a decade, the Walt Disney Company, which once seemed to make money as effortlessly as Mickey Mouse waved his magic wand, does so no longer and has decided that it needs a change.
The makeover is already under way, engineered by Disney's chairman and chief executive, Michael D. Eisner, and its late president, Frank G. Wells, long before Mr. Wells's death in a helicopter accident this month. 
 Faced with a weak economy, declining profit growth, flops at the box office and troubles at its theme parks, notably the Euro Disneyland debacle in France, Disney needed a tuneup if it was to continue to sparkle.
"We were reinventing ourselves before Frank's death, but this tragedy has forced us to speed up the process," Mr. Eisner said in a recent interview at Disney's headquarters in Burbank. "I think you have to reinvent yourself almost every seven years or so, as businesses mature and situations change."
 
Foreign Markets a Focus
 Like other film studios and entertainment companies, Disney will focus on cultivating foreign markets, which represent the highest potential for growth, Mr. Eisner said. Reinventing Disney will also include exploring new technologies and expanding the responsibilities of the company's senior executives, many of whom will assume duties held by Mr. Wells, he continued.
Under Mr. Eisner and Mr. Wells, who were hired in 1984 to revive a languishing Disney, the company saw its annual revenue rise from $1.5 billion to $8.5 billion in a decade and its stock value increase 1,500 percent.
The growth was fueled by the rapid expansion of theme parks, resorts, retail stores and film and television production, but such remarkable gains are now proving harder to sustain than ever before, even with the magic of "Aladdin's" genie.
With Disney's revenues approaching $10 billion a year, and competition growing in the theme park and animation businesses, it will be far more difficult for the company to achieve the 20 percent annual earnings growth that management has projected over the next five years, industry analysts say.
"Walt Disney's success is based on a very simplistic approach: the filmed entertainment division comes up with new characters that are then used by the rest of the company to create a broader line of products," Christopher P. Dixon, an analyst for Paine Webber, said.
"But the big challenge of the 90's will be to take their brands and expand them into markets overseas and different venues," Mr. Dixon said.
That will involve the international expansion of the company's three major divisions: theme parks, filmed entertainment and consumer products. "During the first 10 years of this job we focused predominately on growing the domestic businesses," Mr. Eisner said. "Now we have to start over again in places like China and India and develop new products for these markets."
As an indication of how important international expansion is to Disney's future, Mr. Eisner said that "non-American born" executives would play a much more important role in the management of the company. "I think you will see names in our top management that are hard to pronounce as time goes on," he said.
Disney has already made significant headway in expanding its consumer products, publications, movies, videos and television programs in Europe, Asia, the Middle East and South America. Today, Disney has 16 offices serving 45 countries.
But Mr. Eisner's new international concentration comes after a disaster in Disney's most visible foray into Europe. From its inception, Euro Disney has lost money, hurt by Europe's recession and the company's misjudgments of European tastes.
Disney, which owns 49 percent of Euro Disney, banned alcohol at the park, offered visitors European food and fancy table settings, and expected them to patronize the hotels and gift shops. But many Europeans shunned the park or spent as little as possible there. As a result, Euro Disney lost nearly $1 billion in its first year and Disney's profit fell 63 percent.
Now Disney is allowing wine and beer to be sold, and the company has cut back the number of traditional restaurants while adding American-style fast-food operations. In addition, the company is offering some room discounts to attract visitors to the hotels.
"We know that Americans don't want us to open a French restaurant in New York or Los Angeles that serves a double patty cheeseburger, and that the French don't want us to come over there and do crepe, and the Germans don't want us to serve Knackwurst and Sauerkraut," Mr. Eisner said. "They want us to do what we do."
After Mr. Eisner threatened last year to close Euro Disney, creditors granted him a debt restructuring that will buy the project time to turn around.
"Maybe I am just stubbornly optimistic or arrogantly insensitive on the creative side," Mr. Eisner said, "but I think the park is the most fantastic product ever done by this company. A hundred years from now people will wonder how did this ever get built."
 
Additional Losses Seen
 Still, Euro Disney executives have said that they expect the park to post a loss this fiscal year, and that it will not show any significant revenue growth before 1996.
The theme park business is highly dependent on the health of world economies and consumer confidence. And with a weak economies in Europe and California, operating profit at Disney's theme parks in the first three months of this year fell 3 percent to $152.3 million, mainly because of a decline in overseas visitors to Walt Disney World in Florida and a drop in attendance at Disneyland after the California earthquake.
Judson C. Green, president Walt Disney Attractions, said that despite the recent declines, he expected overall park attendance and revenues to rise, ignited by the planned opening of new gates, rides and attractions. Still, Mr. Green said, Disney is cutting operating costs at the parks and aggressively pricing its hotels.
While no decision has yet been made, Mr. Green said that Disney was seriously considering building a theme park in the Far East, perhaps somewhere in the Pacific Rim. The company has also looked at building parks in Australia, South America and elsewhere in Europe, but "the politics and economics have to be right before we make a major move," Mr. Green said.
 
Consumer Produces Expanding
 Already, Disney's consumer products division, which oversees licensing, retailing, publishing and records, is expanding broadly throughout Europe, the Middle East, South America and Asia. Last year the division had operating income of $356 million, up 26 percent over the previous year.
A major contributor to the success of the consumer products division was the Disney stores, which sell everything from Mickey Mouse watches and Dopey cookie jars to Disney shirts, hats and jewelry.
Barton K. Boyd, president of Disney Consumer Products, said that there are 268 Disney stores, and the company plans to open at least 70 new stores a year. This year alone, Disney plans to open 100 stores in France, England, Germany, Spain, Japan, Hong Kong, Singapore, Canada and the United States. The Disney Store on the Champs-Elysees in Paris is the company's top grossing store.
"We are moving slowly and patiently to China," Mr. Boyd siad. "The grandparents remember the Disney characters like Mickey Mouse, and it won't take long before for their grandchildren to come to know them, too."
 
Gains From Film Division
 Despite numerous box-office flops in the past year, Disney's filmed entertainment division continues to post strong gains, buoyed by the popularity of Disney's animated features and robust home video sales, both domestically and overseas.
Disney Studios produces movies under three labels: Walt Disney Pictures, Touchstone Pictures and Hollywood Pictures. Walt Disney Pictures produces animated features like "The Little Mermaid" and family-oriented features. Touchstone and Hollywood Pictures produce more mature films like "Pretty Woman" and "Sister Act."
Disney is expanding is presence in the international film and television market. Its animated films are a huge success overseas and have been translated into 25 languages. Disney's total box office last year was $644 million.
The company is also pursuing local programming partnerships. It has 35 Disney Clubs and other Disney programs that are broadcast internationally.
Disney's filmed entertainment division is still highly profitable, even though the studios have produced numerous movies of late that have been financial and critical disasters, leading to the removal this week of Ricardo Mestres as president of Hollywood Pictures. Hollywood had some box-office successes, like "The Hand That Rocks the Cradle" and "The Joy Luck Club," but the label had many more bombs, including "Blame It on the Bellboy," "Born Yesterday" and "Guilty as Sin."

HEADLINE: New York Area Braces for Onslaught of Visitors in June

BYLINE: By ROBERT D. McFADDEN 

 BODY:
An extraordinary conjunction of events -- World Cup soccer matches, the Olympic-style Gay Games and a sprawling 25th anniversary celebration of a gay-rights uprising -- is expected to bring vast throngs of visitors to New York City and northern New Jersey during nine days in late June.
If all goes as planned in the frenetic peak of June 18-26, organizers and public officials say, about 1.2 million people from across the nation and more than 60 countries will be housed, fed and moved in a logistical ballet that has been months in choreography, and there will be hoopla aplenty and a huge economic windfall for the region. 
 The events are the first three World Cup soccer games at the Meadowlands in New Jersey, the nine-day Gay Games IV and Cultural Festival in New York City, and Stonewall 25, a Manhattan march and rally commemorating a gay rebellion after a violent police raid in 1969 at Stonewall Inn, a Greenwich Village bar.
Organizers and public officials say the invasion of spectators, athletes and other participants could rival the crowds that gathered in and around New York for the Bicentennial in 1976 and Liberty Weekend in 1986, and could surpass those at national political conventions and other mass gatherings of the past.
While large gatherings have usually focused around a single site like Madison Square Garden or New York Harbor, the events in late June are far more complicated logistically.
Organizers of the Gay Games, for example, have arranged for more than 30 parks, gyms, pools, tennis courts, bowling alleys and other sites for their contests, accommodations for thousands of athletes and a festival of scores of entertainers and shows to take place on a Hudson River pier and on Randalls Island.
World Cup officials have had to arrange shuttle buses to take people from New York to the Meadowlands, accreditation and accommodations for 7,000 members of the news media, housing and meals for hundreds of athletes and their retinues, and hotels, parking and hospitality tents for thousands of fans.
 
Turning Life Askew
 Stonewall 25 organizers, city officials, the police and other agencies are still working out plans for Sunday, June 26, when more than 300,000 people are expected to clog major midtown thoroughfares as a gay and lesbian march moves from the United Nations to a rally in Central Park.
On that day and throughout the preceding week, the array of contests, concerts, parties, protests and ceremonies is expected to turn life askew in some parts of the city as crowds fill arenas, stadiums, hotels, restaurants, bars and transportation lines.
Problems exist. Some Greenwich Village residents are angry at the prospect of street closings, disrupted traffic, outdoor drinking, crowds, noise and litter. Concerns also have been voiced over AIDS, though the Clinton Administration has said the visitors would pose no public health threat and has waived a visa rule to let foreigners with the AIDS virus attend the Gay Games.
Moreover, the possibility of clashes between the police and gay protesters -- an ugly and embarrassing replay of the Stonewall riots of 1969 -- haunts the organizers and has prompted sensitivity training for the police. And negotiations involving organizers, the city and community groups continue on other issues, including the use of Yankee Stadium for closing ceremonies for the Gay Games.
But hotels say there are enough rooms for everyone, transportation officials say the logistics of moving people around are well in hand, and city officials and organizers who have planned for months say they expect the visiting legions to be wined, dined, entertained and protected without major disruptions.
And the officials and organizers estimate that the three events could generate more than $650 million in direct spending by visitors and spinoff economic activity, as well as publicity for tourism and big dollops of gay pride and civic boosterism.
"We're going to have a great many people and some very exciting days in New York," Fran Reiter, the Deputy Mayor for Planning and Community Relations, who has coordinated city planning for the events, said in an interview. "It will be a celebration of what New York stands for -- the world's most diverse city."
Rich Altman, a spokesman for the New York Convention and Visitors Bureau, said that many visitors will stay with friends or relatives and that despite a heavy demand for accommodations, many of the city's 58,000 rooms in 231 hotels are still available. "Our premise is that New York City is never sold out," he said
Not to be outdone, Bob Mulcahy, the chief executive of the New Jersey Sports and Exposition Authority, said that tens of thousands of visitors attending soccer games at the Meadowlands were taking accommodations west of the Hudson River, to be near the action.
 
New Jersey's 'Lion's Share'
 "A fair proportion will benefit New Jersey," Mr. Mulcahy said. "Many people will stay over here. In fact, I'd say New Jersey will receive the lion's share. This is the largest sporting event in the world and we're delighted to have a part of it."
Three of the seven matches to be played in June and July at Giants Stadium are scheduled within the span of the Gay Games: June 18 (Italy-Ireland), June 23 (Italy-Norway), and June 25 (Saudi Arabia-Morocco).
More than 500,000 visitors, many from Italy, Ireland and the other nations competing in the Meadowlands, are expected to attend the seven matches, said John Griffin, a World Cup spokesman. With sellout crowds of 76,000, more than 200,000 would be attending the first three matches in late June.
Eighty-two hotels in New York and northern New Jersey have been designated by World Cup '94 for the fans, but a survey by the Hotel Association of New York City last week showed that many rooms were still available in late June. And with many visitors staying with friends or relatives, even late bookers were expected to find rooms, though not always in the best locations.
Mr. Griffin said organizers had calculated that the New York-New Jersey area would reap $452 million in economic benefits from visitors attending the seven games, suggesting that the benefit from visitors attending the opening three games in late June would be nearly $200 million.
From June 18-25, more than 10,600 gay and lesbian athletes from 40 countries -- more than the number of athletes at the Barcelona Summer Olympics in 1992 -- are expected to compete in the Olympic-style Gay Games IV at parks, gyms and pools all over New York City.
 
Badminton to Water Polo
 Among the 31 sports are badminton, billiards, golf, ice hockey, judo, physique, power lifting, water polo and wrestling. Opening ceremonies are planned for Columbia University's Baker Field, but the organizers, with the support of Mayor Rudolph W. Giuliani, are negotiating with George Steinbrenner for the use of Yankee Stadium for the closing ceremonies on June 25.
On June 26, the day after the games close, a festival of concerts, films, plays and other activities is expected to attract tens of thousands to a pier on the Hudson at 49th Street and to Randalls Island, the site of the track and field competitions.
Marlin Collingwood, a spokesman, said organizers expected 500,000 visitors, 100,000 of them from abroad.
Employees at the Hotel Pennsylvania, the headquarters of Gay Games IV and one of 30 hotels where rooms have been reserved for athletes and spectators, have expressed fears that some guests might carry the AIDS virus. But the Hotel Association and the hotel employees' union said there would be no need for additional training or new policies because there were no significant dangers.
In Greenwich Village the prospect of heavy street crowds has some people upset. Talks are under way between the city and community groups to close Christopher Street, from Seventh Avenue to West Street, on some evenings, and to let gay and lesbian bars set up sidewalk cafes.
Organizers and city officials are also said to be focusing on the often-volatile relationship between the police and homosexuals. Above all, both sides say, they want to avoid violent clashes like those that wracked Christopher Street in 1969 when the police raided Stonewall Inn.
"We've come a long way since the problems of 25 years ago," Ms. Reiter said. "The police are highly professional. They've worked closely with the gay and lesbian community on planning the Gay Pride Parade. The numbers this year will be bigger, but I don't expect any conflict."
Stonewall 25, the June 26 march and rally commemorating the clashes that were a turning point in the gay-rights movement, this year will take the place of the annual Gay Pride Parade, normally held in June.
Organizers say 300,000 to 400,000 people are expected to join the march starting about noon on the East Side and moving past the United Nations, west on 42d Street and north to a rally on the Great Lawn of Central Park. Dances, street fairs, worship services and other gatherings will also take place around town June 24-26.
While organizers gave varying estimates of the expected crowds, Ms. Reiter estimated that 1.2 million visitors would come to the city and would generate economic benefits of about $650 million.
"It's going to be very good for our city," Ms. Reiter said. "It will spotlight the city in the best way, as welcoming the world. These are the kinds of events we want to encourage -- they are good for our economy, good for our spirit, good for our soul."

HEADLINE: Pentagon Tests New Policy In Subsidizing an Industry

BYLINE: By KEITH BRADSHER, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, April 27

 BODY:
The White House's decision to subsidize the flat-panel computer display screen industry is an experiment in changing the Defense Department's relationship with commercial suppliers, Administration officials said today.
For two generations, the Defense Department has paid contractors to build special factories to make high-technology products not available commercially from American companies, like certain kinds of high-speed computer chips. But as foreign companies have won technological dominance in more and more industries, the Defense Department has been faced with a choice of buying from abroad or paying ever more money for goods from the small and often inefficient American factories. 
 In deciding to subsidize development of an American industry producing computer display screens in commercial quantities, the Defense Department is trying to pursue a third course. The hope of Administration officials is that bolstering a new industry will prove cheaper in the long run than making small purchases from special factories and will be better for national security than relying on foreign manufacturers, officials said.
Another rationale is that the Defense Department will also avoid becoming stuck with outmoded equipment from special factories, which tend not to modernize as quickly as commercial factories that must stay competitive or face bankruptcy.
Defense Department reliance on special factories for display screens would have been especially expensive, said an Administration official who insisted on anonymity. "It would have cost an arm and a leg, and the technology is changing so fast in the commercial sector that there's no guarantee that they were getting the latest," the official said.
The experiment with display screens, which the Administration said could be expanded to other industries if successful, ran into criticism from economists today, who said that it could set a bad precedent for encouraging high-technology companies to rely on Government money, and could undermine decades of American effort to win international restrictions on European government subsidies to companies there.
"It is a slippery slope -- the question is how much the President wants to slip down it," said Jagdish Bhagwati, a Columbia University economics professor.
By presenting the issue in terms of national security, the Administration has made it much more difficult to criticize the program, politicians and economists said today. The White House plan does not pass muster by conventional economic measures, said Robert J. Shapiro, a Democratic policy analyst.
But he quickly added, "I can't evaluate the national security policy aspects of it; it does make it a harder policy to evaluate."
The policy's goal of grabbing part of the world market from Japanese companies will further mute possible critics, said Representative Robert T. Matsui, a California Democrat.
"Given the fact that it's the Japanese," he said, "I think it lessens the anxiety we might have -- if this were with Germany, we might have a different attitude."
The Administration strategy for helping the flat-panel display screen industry is unusually broad. The plan calls for the Defense and Energy Departments to provide most of the subsidies while the Commerce Department would provide overseas marketing assistance.
The Office of the United States Trade Representative would protest foreign barriers to American display screen exports; the Justice Department would keep track of any attempts by Japanese electronics corporations to squeeze out their small American rivals, and the National Economic Council would oversee the entire effort and encourage Government agencies to pool their display screen purchases and buy American.
 
Mainly in Laptops
 Advanced flat-panel displays are now used mainly in laptop computers and medical diagnostic equipment. But the Administration foresees their wide use soon in car dashboards, aircraft cockpits, and even portable maps for soldiers and travelers.
One of the biggest problems faced by the Administration in developing its new program was how to avoid violating international trade rules that ban commercial production subsidies. The answer was to provide research and development money as matching Federal grants for companies that undertake flat-panel display research and production, the same Administration official said.
For example, a company that spent $1 on research and invested another $1 in production equipment could receive a matching grant of $1 for further research, the official said. One goal of these subsidies is to prompt American companies to build four high-production factories, according to an interagency report.
But Mr. Bhagwati said that foreign countries could seek arbitration under existing international trade rules to challenge this approach. The rules ban research subsidies if the intent is to help companies' production, although proving intent can be difficult, Mr. Bhagwati said.

HEADLINE: COMPANY REPORTS;
Trading Loss Lowers Profit For Procter

 BODY:
The Procter & Gamble Company, the country's largest maker of household goods and personal care products, said yesterday that its losses from interest-rate swaps pushed down earnings for its third quarter.
The company, based in Cincinnati, said earnings for the quarter ended March 31 slid 4 percent, falling to $482 million from $502 million in the comparable period a year ago. The current figure includes an after-tax charge of $102 million, announced earlier this month, for the trading losses. 
 Interest-rate swaps are among so-called derivatives that companies can use to hedge against sharp market fluctuations or even attempt to profit from them. The investments are called derivatives because they are based on, or derive from, the performance of an underlying asset, like interest rates or stocks.
Without the charge, profits would have improved by 16.3 percent for the quarter. "Pretax operating profit was up 20 percent versus January-March a year ago," the chairman and chief executive of P.& G., Edwin L. Artzt, said in a statement. "This improvement reflects our cost reduction programs along with solid unit volume gains in the U.S. and a continued double-digit volume growth rate in our international operations."
One P.& G. stockholder apparently did not share Mr. Artzt's enthusiasm. On Monday, Elaine Drage of Cleveland sued the company in the Hamilton County Common Pleas court, an Ohio state court, charging that company executives should repay the $102 million charge.
Excluding the after-tax charge, the results for the quarter showed that net sales had risen by 1.2 percent, to $7.4 billion, and net earnings per share rose by 16.7 percent, to 77 cents.

HEADLINE: Dollar Falters Against Yen on Political Turmoil in Japan

BYLINE: By Bloomberg Business News 

 BODY:
The dollar fell yesterday to four-week lows against the Japanese yen and the German mark amid persistent concern that political turmoil in Japan would prolong the country's trade dispute with the United States.
Trading was less active than normal because many traders in New York took the day off. The stock, bond and commodities markets in the United States were closed in memory of former President Richard M. Nixon, who died on Friday. 
 "The dollar took a beating on light volume," Jack Stapleton, senior corporate dealer at Standard Chartered Bank, said. "There's still a lot of nervousness about Japan."
The Socialist Party bolted from Japan's coalition Government on Monday, raising concern about whether Prime Minister Tsutomo Hata can command a majority in Parliament and restart trade talks with the United States.
"The more political discord there is in Japan, the stronger the yen will be," David De Rosa, director of foreign-exchange trading at the Swiss Bank Corporation, said. "The only thing that will drive the dollar higher is a resolution of the trade issues."
Without progress on trade, the Clinton Administration is considered more likely to resume calls for a strong yen to curb Japan's trade surplus by making the country's exports more costly.
 
Dollar at 102.35 Yen in New York
 In late New York trading, the dollar was quoted at 102.35 yen, its lowest level since March 31, down from 102.80 yen on Tuesday. The dollar also fell to 1.6710 marks, also a four-week low, down from 1.6770 marks.
Japan's central bank bought dollars for yen during much of the Tokyo session, traders there said. The Bank of Japan often buys dollars to try to slow the yen's climb, many times to no avail.
Losses against the yen helped drive the dollar lower against the mark and other currencies, traders said. Frustration with the dollar's inability to rise, even when economic reports and interest-rate changes favor it, also weighed on the currency.
Many traders had expected the dollar to gain in the first quarter of this year as American interest rates rose and German rates fell. Higher American rates usually make dollar-denominated assets more attractive to investors, bolstering demand for the currency. Instead, the dollar fell 4 percent against the mark and 8 percent against the yen since Jan. 1.
The dollar failed to rise against the mark yesterday, even though Germany's central bank lowered a short-term interest.
Elsewhere, the British pound rose to a three-month high of $1.5105 yesterday, before falling amid speculation that the Bank of England was selling pounds. In New York, the pound was quoted at $1.5060, little changed from $1.5055 on Tuesday.
The dollar also fell in New York to 5.7395 French francs yesterday from 5.7556 francs on Tuesday and weakened to 1.4257 Swiss francs from 1.4301 francs. And it dropped to 1,608 Italian lire from 1,614 lire. The Canadian dollar fell to 72.67 United States cents from 72.82 cents.

HEADLINE: Market Place;
A small health care company seeks its fortune in New York.

BYLINE: By Milt Freudenheim 

 BODY:
PHYSICIANS Health Services, a managed health care company with a cash hoard of $115 million and a strong base in Connecticut, would like to play with the big boys in the rapidly expanding New York market for managed care.
Although only five analysts follow the company, they all recommend it. The stock price went from a low of $12 in February of last year to $27.50 last month in Nasdaq trading. It fell to $22.25 in the stock market slide of March and early April but has bounced backed, closing at $25.33 on Tuesday. (The stock market was closed yesterday for former President Richard M. Nixon's funeral.) 
 The company, which was taken public last year by Dillon, Read and Smith Barney Shearson, said in March that it was seeking regulatory approval to operate health maintenance organizations in New York City and Nassau and Suffolk counties on Long Island. It is already in Westchester, Putnam, Rockland, Orange and Dutchess counties.
Employers typically pay a set amount in advance to buy coverage from a health maintenance organization and offer financial incentives to employees who patronize the H.M.O. doctors.
Enrollment in all health maintenance organizations in the New York metropolitan area increased by 20 percent, to 1.5 million, in the nine months ending Jan. 1, according to the New York State H.M.O. Council, a trade group. The council said that 18.3 percent of the population was in H.M.O.'s, up from 15.1 percent a year ago.
Despite the surge in H.M.O membership, there is still plenty of room for growth. In market share, New York H.M.O.'s still lag far behind the West Coast and upper Midwest, not to mention Rochester, where close to 65 percent of the population is in H.M.O.'s.
"It could be a real sleeper in the H.M.O. group," Michael M. LeConey a health care analyst at RAS Securities, said of Physicians Health Services, which is based in Trumbull, Conn., and has about 170,000 enrollees. He recommended the stock in a report on Northeast region H.M.O.'s, along with better-known, larger companies, including U.S. Healthcare and Oxford Health Plans.
Art Winston, a money manager at Glickenhaus & Company, an investment management firm that owns Physicians Health Services shares, said the company was well managed and "relatively cheap," compared with the other H.M.O. stocks. He sees it as a natural takeover candidate for a larger managed care insurer entering southern Connecticut, where Physicians Health Services has contracts with a number of blue chip companies.
Not just any merger partner would be acceptable, however, because the Connecticut company is controlled by its physician-founders. "That eliminates a takeover by any H.M.O. with a reputation for being tough on doctors," said Robert M. Mains at First Albany Securities.
For investors, the company's doctor-owners are both an asset and a liability. On the plus side, they are credited with maintaining high quality, which has kept patients happy. The New York State Insurance Department reported that no complaints against the company were found to be valid last year, one of the best records in the state.
But some doctors have also been slow to bring costs down, reflecting the conflict between going all out on the patient's behalf and restraining costs to please the payer. In a recent contract it signed with Pitney Bowes, certain Physicians Health Services doctors were excluded because they were deemed too expensive by the client company.
"The downside for Physicians Health Services is that it is a doctor-owned and doctor-controlled H.M.O.," Mr. LeConey of RAS Securities said. "The risk always is that the doctors have enough influence to maximize reimbursement to themselves." But he thinks that growing competition "is going to finally force economic reality onto the Connecticut physician community," including the doctors at Physician Health Services.

HEADLINE: COMPANY REPORTS;
Xerox Earnings Come In at Low End

BYLINE: By JOHN HOLUSHA 

 BODY:
The Xerox Corporation reported first-quarter earnings yesterday that were at the low end of analysts' expectations.
The company, based in Stamford, Conn., said it had earned $129 million, or $1.05 a share, compared with $189 million, or $1.77 a share, in the comparable period a year ago. 
 Analysts had predicted earnings of $1.04 to $1.25 a share.
But the year-to-year comparisons were skewed by the company's decision to get out of the financial services business and by its sale of 8.1 million shares of stock last June.
In its core document-processing business, Xerox said, it earned $131 million, an increase of 5 percent from last year, but last year's figure was inflated by a one-time tax credit of $17 million. Earnings per share were $1.07, compared with $1.12. The decline was caused by an increase of 10 percent in the number of shares outstanding.
 
Productivity Improvements
 Xerox's chairman, Paul A. Allaire, said, "The increased income in the first quarter was due to significant productivity improvements and strong growth in equipment sales."
With stock markets closed, Xerox's stock did not trade yesterday. It closed on Tuesday at $96.625, up $1.875.
The company said that it had reduced its document-processing work force by 4,200 people worldwide during the quarter through attrition and restructuring and that by early 1996 it would have cut 10,000 workers and closed some operations.
Sales of copiers and other document-processing products increased by 1 percent, to $3.271 billion, including the effect of currency exchange. Adjusting for currency rates, revenues increased by 5 percent in the quarter, the company said.
 
Brazilian Inflation a Factor
 The main cause of the currency exchange adjustment, the company said, was its subsidiary in Brazil, where inflation is about 40 percent a month, compared with 25 percent a month a year ago. This produced a currency translation loss of $61 million in the quarter, compared with $23 million in the comparable period a year ago.
The company said its insurance group, Talegen Holdings Inc., had increased earnings to $37 million, from $16 million, despite losses caused by the earthquake in Southern California in January. The company is one of the few financial services that Xerox still operates, but Mr. Allaire said that "we are currently holding discussions with a number of parties interested in the Talegen insurance groups."
Revenues from the sale of advanced digital products increased by 21 percent during the quarter, and they now represent 19 percent of Xerox's total revenues.
Mr. Allaire said the company was continuing to make "significant" investments in new technology, and he noted last week's announcement of software that would connect computer terminals with digital copiers anywhere.
