HEADLINE: Essay;
RN Off the Record

BYLINE: By WILLIAM SAFIRE 

DATELINE: YORBA LINDA, Calif.

 BODY:
"Guns are an abomination," said Richard Nixon in one of our long talks last year. He added, "You can use that now."
Which I did in a column. The "now" referred to an agreement between my old boss and me: Some of what he said was for immediate quotation, some for private instruction, and the rest to be made public at my discretion "someday" -- by which he meant any time after the ceremony yesterday at which he joined the real Silent Majority. 
 Much of his strategic thinking will be available next month in his last book, "Beyond Peace." But here is Nixon as one admirer had a chance to see him, tossing off aphorisms with candor and purpose ("Bull sessions produce only what bulls produce").
On recent Russian leaders, preceding his last mission to Moscow: "Yeltsin is at his best in a crisis, just as Gorbachev was at his worst in a crisis. We stayed too long with Gorbachev; we mustn't stay too long with Yeltsin. We need contact with the opposition, and Yeltsin won't like it."
Specifically about Yeltsin: "He has bouts of depression, and takes medication for his back problem, like Woodrow Wilson. His close buddies are not very impressive. He feels the army didn't move quickly enough in the coup attempt against him." (Long pause.) "He'll trust only a few people, and will think only those who support him 100 percent are his friends." (I said I knew somebody like that once, and Nixon gave his short bark of a laugh.)
"I gave Clinton an evaluation of Yeltsin: not hard to read, straight out, refreshing, charismatic. But lacks experience in free-market economics, let alone running a democratic republic."
On the Mideast: "I have great respect for Rabin, but I didn't want to go to the dog and pony show on the White House lawn. I told him later that it takes a strong man to make peace; he's able to do what he's doing because Arafat is weak, and far worse than Arafat is waiting in the wings. Paul Johnson had a line that statesmen must differentiate between different degrees of evil."
On Bosnia: " 'Assertive multilateralism' is nonsense. De Gaulle said to Malraux: 'Parliaments can paralyze policy; they cannot make policy.' We need to get out of the arms embargo to get to a correlation of forces that can make peace. I'm more hawkish than Bush or Clinton on this. For now, say I'm more hawkish than Bush; I don't want to lose my effectiveness in foreign affairs."
On the foreign-policy Clinton (whose eulogy was unstinting and eloquent): "Foreign affairs is not a winner for him. He should play to his strength: try to keep the Nafta coalition together, throw the McGovern ites over the side. The left won't start a third party, and there's a big center out there. For the Republicans' sake, I hope he doesn't, but that's what he should do."
On the domestic Clinton: "I told him [a year ago] I gave him credit for a bold program. A leader tells people not where they want to go, but where they ought to go. That's from Burke. Clinton reminded me that with wage and price controls, I did something bold. Yeah, but it was a mistake." Nixon reminded me: "You wrote that speech, don't forget."
On China and M.F.N.: "What gets me is the old China hands who got all excited when I went to China, and who are now anti-China because there's no political freedom. Sure our goal is for democratic government that protects human rights, but it's economic freedom that spells doom for any closed society. M.F.N. is a blunderbuss; we shouldn't use that."
On the Chinese leadership: "Jiang Zemin impresses Americans because he can recite parts of the Gettysburg Address in English; State overestimates him. Li Peng is ruthless, intelligent, wants to turn back the clock, and in a struggle after Deng's death, would have Jiang Zemin for lunch. Zhu Rongji is totally committed to economic reforms, and politically -- as he demonstrated as Mayor of Shanghai -- as reasonable as can be and still be a leader. I'd bet on Zhu; he's China's best hope."
His deep-background handicapping of the 1996 Republican field must await another day.
RN's farewell, as he accompanied me to the door: "We have to build a society that the world can continue to admire. That's the message I would like to leave for the kids."

HEADLINE: KIPS BAY A TOUR;
And to the Left, Is the Niche For Fifi . . .

BYLINE: By SUZANNE SLESIN 

 BODY:
LADIES and gentlemen (but please, no children under 6), welcome to the 1994 Kips Bay Boys & Girls Club Decorator Show House. And what a show house it is! The Sara Delano Roosevelt House on the Upper East Side of Manhattan, spiffed up by 14 interior design firms, 5 decorative painters and a floral designer.
Please note that on this tour items of great interest have been identified with numbers. And if at any time you wish to go back and review the commentary on what you have seen, there is no need to press the rewind button. Simply read the paragraph over again. 
 As you may know, Kips Bay is the doyenne of show houses, now in its 22d year. If interior decorating is your delectation, you are in your element.
The Kips Bay Show House, at 47 and 49 East 65th Street, which opened on Tuesday and continues to May 15, is as important a bellwether as ever -- despite the beating the interior design profession has been taking for the last few years.
The loss of a generation of talented designers to AIDS, the sagging economy, the "I can-do-it-just-as-well-myself" syndrome as well as the rush to flea markets, auctions and discount designer outlets should have made upscale, over-the-top decorating extinct.
Yet, talented designers can still excite and inspire. The show house is their stage, whether it is Paul Siskin's wonderfully intriguing study for a psychiatrist (1), or Ronald Bricke's fairytale master bedroom, where a bed's canopy is equipped with a little high-tech magic (2). But first it's time to face the neo-Georgian facade of the five-story double town house that was built in 1907-1908 by Charles Platt for Sara Delano Roosevelt. Hidden behind the single facade are actually two separate houses: Mrs. Roosevelt occupied half the house and gave other to her son, Franklin, and his wife, Eleanore.
Before going inside, take a minute or so to appreciate the bravura of Ronaldo Maia's suspended boat-shaped planters overflowing with ferns, ivies and apple-blossom branches in full flower.
Pass under the tapestry canopy and proceed past Lina Fransioli's mural panels in the entrance, hung to look like frescoes. Proceed up the steps and turn left into House 47, Mrs. Roosevelt-mere's residence -- and into the first room on the tour, the great hall. It has been decorated in a mannered "Age of Innocence" vein by a Kips Bay newcomer, Anthony Antine, of Palisades, N.J. Noting the tortoise-shell knickknacks on the table and enveloped by the gold and red damask that was woven in Scotland from a Russian design, and used here on the walls and upholstery, and turned inside out on the draperies, let yourself imagine for a moment that you are waiting to be summoned upstairs by Mrs. Manson Mingott. Stop at the spot where the parquet has been painted in a star pattern and buy a $15 ticket that benefits the Kips Bay Boys and Girls Club, an after-school program in the South Bronx.
Proceed toward the curved staircase, passing the Kips Treasure Chest, a boutique of donated items (there will be plenty of time to shop later) and at the top of the stair you will reach our next stop, the Sara Delano Roosevelt library, decorated by Noel Jeffrey.
Let's pause on the threshold of the paneled room that represents a new look for Mr. Jeffrey, a designer show house regular best known for his extravagant efforts. Mr. Jeffrey has taken what he said was "a more casual approach," and "played the whole thing down."
But everything is relative. The furniture, it turns out, is what top-flight decorators call "FFF." That's Fine French Furniture, or, as Mr. Jeffrey put it, "Jacob, Jacob, Jacob," for the 18th-century maitre menuisier, Georges Jacob, the master craftsman of the Louis XVI and Empire styles.
On the left, please note -- though it's hard to miss -- the surprisingly pastel-hued, nine-foot-high celadon leather screen that rises majestically behind the toile-covered sofa. "This is my statement of traditional decorating at this time," Mr. Jeffrey said. "Comfortable, simple and nothing to be afraid of."
Turning around, you pass Bert Westman's brooms on the wall, painted with an array of faux finishes. Yes, leopard is an option.
Now, please pause, for un petit moment, on the threshold of the luminous double drawing room that spans the two houses (mother-in-law was known to come through unexpectedly and rearrange the new bride's furniture on occasion). Today it is under Mariette Himes Gomez's elegant tutelage (3) and has been transformed into a cool, airy arbor with tastefully color-coded objets d'art and textured fabrics that range from pale green to toast.
Matching fireplaces face each other across a wide expanse that has been adroitly furnished with antique Italian consoles, English lanterns, a giant Georgian partner's desk, and a huge muted Savonnerie rug -- restrained by decorator standards.
You may want to take a minute to think about how you can adapt Ms. Gomez's clever window treatment in your own pied-a-terre: tall panels of pale chartreuse-and-white striped fabric looped over brass rods that swing open like translucent fabric shutters. Now turn around and continue up the stairs to the third floor and Scott Salvator and Michael Zabriskie's fabric-swathed guest bedroom (4), with charming twin headboards with arms like an armchair. (2)
"Don't you love them? They're beds that you can sit up and read in," Mr. Salvator informs us.
But please don't leave without noting the small side table, carved in a shell design. Mr. Salvator tells us that this is the last of its kind. "None of the other younger designers remember these things," Mr. Salvator, 34, said. . "You have to be 30 going on 60 to appreciate it."
Our tour continues across the hall, past Michael Tyson Murphy's trompe l'oeil chest in marquetry, in a sitting room designed by Dennis Rolland. . Raise your eyes to the all-grey ceiling painted with graphite -- the material of pencil fame. Before leaving Mr. Rolland's room, envy the remarkable shell-encrusted heads on the bookcase at left. (5)
Our next stop up the stairs on the left is Mr. Siskin's study for a psychiatrist (1). Tension and poetry are in the air. A clock ticks loudly. Mr. Siskin calls his room "It's about time." Not only does this seem to be a reference to the loss of his partner, Perucho Valls, who died last year, but to the strength of his solo performance. With your senses thus heightened, look around the sober, eloquent room that is the most evocative in the house.
Don't leave before admiring the fake leather bookshelves studded with nailheads on your left, or the voluminous one-armed couch, or the natural sisal trimmed with a black band on one side, or the huge rusted roman-numeral clock face.
"If Freud moved to the Upper East Side, this is what his office would look like," Mr. Siskin whispers.
Try and touch the tray of moss plants on the ottoman. It's strangely therapeutic.
Now it's time to walk past Robert Jackson's new murals depicting what he says is "a villa near Lucca, or Montecito, or Greenwich" -- you choose -- to reach Eric Cohler's combination library and kitchen, full of take-out ideas (6). To your right, look for the striped wallpaper-covered refrigerator and the dual wall-hung television sets, one tuned to recipes, the other to a soap opera. The fireplace filled with baguettes masquerading as logs is, as they say, interesting. I urge you to glance at the kitchen cabinetry. A niche provides a spot for a dog bed.
We move on through the door at left, to house 49 -- and walk into atherine Stephens' cozy, red plaid family room and across the landing to Gerald Tolomeo's library and conservatory. You must look up and marvel at a three-dimensional trellis below a painted blue sky (7) decorated with papier mache and painted leaves made by Donna Holsten.
Proceeding down the stairs you will come face to face with Ronald Bricke's bedroom (2), a triumph of style in charcoal and white. Be sure to see the demonstration of the small screen that drops from a canopy, made of bits of baroque molding (the projection TV is concealed at the other end). Do give the draperies -- more like bunches of white fabric knotted into ponytails by Mr. Bricke himself, their 15 seconds of attention. You are now on the fourth floor of House 49 and passing tall cabinets "woodgrained" by Philip Traina. Soon, you are across the hall, basking in the aubergine aura of Billy Francis's sitting room (8). The velvet on the walls has been graffaged, not flocked as we thought, "embossed with hot steam and copper pipes," Mr. Francis said. Before leaving this elegant, cozy lair, stop and smell the roses. They're palpably Zeze's finest.
Then it's down the stairs to Tom O' Toole's library (9), this one named for F.D.R. and all-American neo-classical, the decorating style for these sober times.Covet the folk art ships, grouped around the fireplace to your left, that were hand-carried from a Madison Avenue gallery a few blocks away.
Charlotte Moss's tented sepia boudoir beckons across the landing. Perhaps it's the sight of Madame de Pompadour's 18th century gowns in the closet. Or the lovebirds singing on the toile-de-Jouy.
Our tour continues down the stairs. Just when you would expect a grand finale, you come upon Robert Metzger's out of character, new-for-him Art Deco look (10): "Straight lines. No frills, no fluff," he said. But still plenty of glitz: black and white striped armchairs, an antique Chinese rug and a cache of lamps and armchairs that are Parisian in provenance.
Our tour ends here. But your decorating doesn't need to. Be sure to take home the Designer's Sources pamphlet. It makes great bedside reading.
Goodbye and thank you for joining us. See you next year.

HEADLINE: A War-Weary Serbia Dares to Hope

BYLINE: By STEPHEN KINZER, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: BELGRADE, Yugoslavia, April 27

 BODY:
The spring weather is splendid in Serbia's capital, and as residents stroll through parks and sip coffee in downtown cafes, they dare to hope that the war in neighboring Bosnia and Herzegovina may finally be approaching an end.
With the threat of NATO bombing raids against Serbian positions near the Bosnian town of Gorazde now apparently lifted, and with foreign powers launching a new negotiating push, many Serbs here feel an unaccustomed optimism. 
 "Now the world is going to put pressure on all three sides, and that means peace is possible," said a middle-aged engineer as he waited for a streetcar. "I feel more positive than I have in a long time."
The engineer is evidently not alone. In a survey of 500 Belgrade households taken recently by a local bank, 77 percent of respondents said they believed next year would be better or much better than this year.
 
Shared Sense of Hope
 Foreign diplomats and Serbian political observers share the mood of hope that has settled over Belgrade. Some believe that prospects for a peace settlement in Bosnia may be closer than at any time since the war began more than two years ago.
But while it is widely agreed here that the principal Serbian leader, the Yugoslav President, Slobodan Milosevic, is ready to press his longtime allies in Bosnia to negotiate seriously, two major questions remain. No one is certain how much influence Mr. Milosevic has over the notoriously independent Bosnian Serbs, nor can anyone guess whether the Clinton Administration is ready to insist that the Muslim-dominated Government in Sarajevo accept its military defeat and agree to a negotiated settlement.
Among factors leading analysts in Belgrade toward optimism are the growing war-weariness in Serbia, the increasing willingness of Russia to pressure its Bosnian Serb allies, and the formation this week of a "contact group" that is intended to coordinate Russian, American and European negotiating positions.
"Forming the contact group was a very important step, because it means that the three warring factions can no longer play one group of negotiators against another," said the chief of a European diplomatic mission here. "It is going to concentrate the minds of all parties."
 
A Change in Approach
 Signs of a softening in Serbia's position are visible on Government-controlled television broadcasts from Belgrade. Reports on the Bosnian war here no longer portray Croats as bent on repeating the massacres of Serbs they perpetrated in World War II, nor do they depict Muslims as fanatic mujahedeen intent on establishing a fundamentalist Islamic state on Serbia's borders.
Television reports have also begun portraying Bosnian Serb leaders, particularly Gen. Ratko Mladic, as ambivalent figures rather than as heroic defenders of Serbian nationalism.
"Serbs in Serbia no longer perceive the Bosnian Serbs as victims," said Predrag Simic, director of the Institute of International Politics and Economics, a research center whose clients include government agencies. "How can you have compassion for people like General Mladic and others? They are not people to pity. More and more, we see them as people who involve us in trouble. Antiwar feeling here is very strong now."
The era of hope in Belgrade has also been fed by a remarkable currency reform that has brought the annualized inflation rate down from a world record of 116 trillion percent, as computed by foreign diplomats in January, to zero. This was done by introducing a new currency tied directly to the German mark.
 
Hoarding of Goods Stops
 Encouraged by the newly stable currency, people have stopped hoarding goods, and items long withheld from the market are now appearing again. Smugglers also have new incentives to challenge the United Nations trade embargo on Serbia, and not only are luxuries like Scotch whisky, Swiss chocolates, French perfume and American power tools available, but people seem to have enough money to buy them.
The currency reform has also calmed social unrest that rose precipitously last winter. Laborers who launched a series of strikes are now quiet, and pensioners, who form a key bloc of support for the Government, now are once again receiving their benefits in money that has value.
Economists and ordinary citizens alike, however, understand that this improvement is only temporary. The new currency will remain stable only as long as the Government can back it with convertible reserves, and that will probably be only a few more months. If the Government has not managed to make progress toward lifting United Nations sanctions by then, the inflationary spiral will start again, and public anger at the Milosevic Government will rise as the value of the "new dinar" falls.
"It was a very clever trick, but nobody believes it can last past the summer," said a foreign resident of Belgrade who tracks the economy closely. "Milosevic is desperate to have the sanctions lifted. People's anger at the prospect of another winter of misery could blow him away, or at least confront him with enough discontent that he would be forced to go to a full dictatorship. He is already telling the Serbs in Bosnia that they better negotiate seriously this time around."
 
The Muslims' Attitude
 What no one here can gauge, however, is whether the increasingly self-confident Bosnian Serb leadership will respond to pressure from Moscow and Belgrade.
But even if the Bosnian Serbs can be persuaded to negotiate seriously after two years of mendacity, it is uncertain that their Muslim enemies will do so. As long as the Muslim-dominated Government in Sarajevo believes there is a prospect that the outside world, and particularly the United States, will come to its rescue if it holds out long enough, it seems unlikely to make compromises necessary for peace.
"At the end of the day, it comes down to how much pressure the Americans are willing to put on the Muslims," a foreign diplomat aid. "If the Muslims think they still have a chance of sucking the West into this, there is no incentive for them to go for the plan on offer."

CORRECTION-DATE: April 30, 1994, Saturday

 CORRECTION:
An article on Thursday about the mood in Belgrade misidentified Slobodan Milosevic. He is the President of the Serbian republic, not the Yugoslav federation, which also includes the republic of Montenegro.

HEADLINE: Currents;
Baltimore Plays Connect-the-Attractions

BYLINE: By MICHAEL McDONOUGH 

 BODY:
Maybe it's the recession and the lack of toothsome commissions. Or the absence of a compelling new theory since deconstruction. Whatever the case, architects and designers seem to be getting comfortable with history again. Examples of "new but old" style -- contemporary with a retro twist -- abound. What might be mistaken for a look backward could be the beginning of something new.
SITE PROJECTS INC., a New York-based arts and architecture firm, is working with Anshen & Allen Inc., architects and planners, to improve the connection between Baltimore's restored but struggling Market Place buildings and its thriving Inner Harbor core. "The Maryland Science Center, the National Aquarium, the shops near the water are very successful," said Ed Hord, the managing partner of Anshen & Allen's Baltimore office. "But people have not wandered up the three blocks of Market Place, and businesses there have failed." 
 "Market Place has beautiful buildings but no street life to connect them," said James Wines, the president of Site.
The company is taking its cues from Baltimore's mercantile history to make the area an attraction with many recreational uses.
An existing slip will be extended and filled with vintage barges and fishing vessels that will become shops, restaurants and cafes. "It's a playground at the scale of a neighborhood," Mr. Wines said. The project is scheduled to be completed by 1996.

HEADLINE: Currents;
An Urban Bicycle With Attitude

BYLINE: By MICHAEL McDONOUGH 

 BODY:
Maybe it's the recession and the lack of toothsome commissions. Or the absence of a compelling new theory since deconstruction. Whatever the case, architects and designers seem to be getting comfortable with history again. Examples of "new but old" style -- contemporary with a retro twist -- abound. What might be mistaken for a look backward could be the beginning of something new.
IF you're looking for an urban commuting bicycle that is tough but stylish, the Globe series by Specialized bicycles of Morgan Hill, Calif., is a good bet. "We call it retro-moderne," said Reed Pike, a marketing administrator for Specialized. "It's a fairly sophisticated package with a lot of functional details." 
 Look for high-practicality features like the internal gear hubs that shift at the handlebar grips; a built-in wheel lock; a chain guard that protects baggy pants or skirts; full-wrap fenders that keep mud from splashing; a cargo rack; a built-in light, and a kickstand (remember them?). Available in two-tone combinations (including green and blue, and lilac and yellow) by July, the three- and seven-speed models will cost $299 to $429. Information: (800) 245-3462.

HEADLINE: Haitian Impasse -- A special report.;
Failure on Haiti: How U.S. Hopes Faded

BYLINE: Special to The New York Times; This article is based on reporting by Elaine Sciolino, Howard W. French and Douglas Jehl and was written by Ms. Sciolino.

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, April 28

 BODY:
The cameras had been turned off and the formal interview at the White House was over last week when President Clinton was asked about Randall Robinson's hunger strike to protest the Administration's policy toward Haiti.
In an extraordinary statement for an American President, Mr. Clinton not only admitted that his policy had failed, but also left the impression that he was somehow powerless to change course. 
 "I understand and respect what he's doing," Mr. Clinton said of the fast by the leader of TransAfrica Forum, a lobbying group and research center. "And we ought to change our policy. It hasn't worked. For the last 10 days, except for my forays on health care, I've done almost nothing but work on Bosnia and Haiti and one or two other foreign policy issues. He ought to stay out there. We need to change our policy."
As a candidate, Mr. Clinton railed against the Bush Administration's Haiti policy and promised to restore democracy to Haiti.
But after 16 months of lofty oratory, half-measures and mixed signals from Washington, Haiti's ousted President, the Rev. Jean-Bertrand Aristide, is no closer to going home. Haiti's military leadership has consolidated its power through a brutal campaign of terror.
Mr. Clinton and Father Aristide, who have met three times, have never agreed on a common strategy. The Pentagon steadfastly opposed any involvement of the American military. Ambassador Lawrence A. Pezzullo, the career diplomat who was entrusted with Haiti policy until he was dismissed last weekend, often worked in a political vacuum and put too much faith in Haiti's military chiefs.
Most important, Mr. Clinton has focused only episodically on Haiti, as on other foreign policy issues.
And as the Clinton White House is preparing yet another incremental policy shift, interviews over the last week with senior Administration officials, diplomats, lawmakers and independent analysts show that even the Administration's plan to impose harsher sanctions may fail to convince Haiti's military to step aside.
There are no illusions in the Administration that these will guarantee the return of Father Aristide. If there is one overwhelming principle and national interest guiding American policy, it is to prevent a flood of refugees into southern Florida.
As the violence in Haiti has increased, many of the President's traditional allies -- Democratic lawmakers, the Congressional Black Caucus, American labor and leaders of Jewish groups and human rights organizations -- have castigated the policy. And last week, David R. Obey, the head of the House Appropriations Committee, stunned the White House when he called for an American-led invasion to oust Haiti's military rulers, to be followed by a new presidential election.
The criticism runs so deep that a 60-page study published last month by the United States Army War College criticizes the Administration for vacillation and confusing actions that have been interpreted in Haiti as indecisive. "Good intentions are not enough," the report says. "One must have clearly defined and realistic goals, the means of attaining them and the will to persist."
 
Clinton Deeply Troubled
 Mr. Clinton is said by aides to be deeply troubled by the violence, which has trained the spotlight on his policy of forcibly returning Haitians who are seeking political asylum. "They're chopping people's faces off," he has repeatedly said to his aides of the country's military.
The result has been a speedy policy review, and an announcement in recent days of elements of what the Administration is calling a major policy shift: an American-led campaign at the United Nations for tougher economic sanctions against Haiti's military Government, a substantial increase in relief aid and tentative proposals for an increase in United Nations and human rights monitors in Haiti.
But when Deputy Secretary of State Strobe Talbott and the deputy national security adviser, Samuel R. Berger, briefed a group of House members today, the new policy ran into a buzz saw of criticism. Some members said it was doomed, while others insisted that what was needed was a credible threat of force, or perhaps even an outright invasion to overthrow the military rulers, several people who attended said.
And to someone like Mr. Robinson, who has had no communication with Mr. Clinton since he began fasting 17 days ago, the President's admission of a policy failure and the decisions that have followed simply add to the weight of evidence of an unfocused policy gone wrong.
"To have the President suggest that the policy should change and I should stay out there on a hunger strike while he abdicates his responsibility is deeply disturbing," he said in an interview. "The President can make the policy effective and humane with the stroke of a pen, but he hasn't changed a thing. It's sad to say, but he appears to be without a moral compass."
 
Early Lurchings
U.S. Credibility Waned and Died
 When Mr. Clinton was elected President, he made the spread of democracy a linchpin of his foreign policy. Haiti was to be the test case. But even before he took office, he sent confusing signals that prevented the Haiti policy from ever getting on course.
Although Mr. Clinton had criticized President Bush's policy of forcibly returning Haitians who fled to this country by boat, he adopted the same policy himself. A State Department analysis prepared for him explained why: Hundreds of thousands, perhaps even millions, of Haiti's 6.4 million people would flee to the United States if the Coast Guard failed to turn them around.
In addition, the Administration's policy was based on the premise that democracy in Haiti meant the return of the democratically elected President. But it was only in the coming months that Mr. Clinton and his aides learned first hand just how difficult a partner Father Aristide could be. Opponents of the policy inside the Administration, particularly in the Pentagon and the Central Intelligence Agency, had only to point to a C.I.A. analysis -- since deemed inaccurate -- that the populist leader and former priest was mentally unstable and therefore unworthy of much United States attention.
The poor communication between Father Aristide and the Administration was aggravated when Ambassador Pezzullo, working with the United Nations, formulated an agreement last summer at Governors Island in New York that lacked any enforcement mechanisms for removing the military leaders who overthrew him, a crucial flaw that in the end doomed the accord.
But by all accounts, the event that buried the accord and signaled the collapse of United States credibility came in October when a ship carrying American military trainers to Haiti was scared off by armed thugs demonstrating ashore. The Pentagon felt vindicated. Still reeling from the death of 18 soldiers in Somalia the week before, it had opposed sending any American troops to Haiti from the start.
Walter B. Slocombe, Deputy Under Secretary of Defense for Policy, boasted at a cocktail party that by turning back the U.S.S. Harlan County, he had helped save the United States from a "small war," some of those present said. He vowed that the Pentagon would not risk American soldiers' lives to put "that psychopath" back in power.
Although Mr. Slocombe neither recalls nor denies making such a strong statement about Father Aristide, he said in an interview, "I do not have any reason to believe President Aristide is a psychopath."
In the months since, the cablegrams to the State Department from the American Embassy have sounded a recurring theme: The moment the Harlan County turned around, the United States lost all credibility. The status of the American Embassy has sunk so low that when Ambassador William L. Swing gave a reception marking the anniversary of Haiti's democratic constitution in March, most of Haiti's political leadership, including outgoing Prime Minsiter Robert Malval, declined the invitation.
 
Flip-Floppings
A Growing Need For Policy Change
 As the violence mounted in the wake of the Harlan County's retreat, there was an unwillingness both in the American Embassy in Port-au-Prince and in Washington to recognize that the American policy preventing Haitians from reaching the United States was becoming increasingly difficult to defend.
When the Assistant Secretary of State for Human Rights, John Shattuck, publicly suggested that the Administration needed to review its policy on the forcible return of boat people, he was given a stern dressing-down by the Under Secretary of State for Political Affairs, Peter Tarnoff.
On the diplomatic front, the Administration flip-flopped between pressing Father Aristide to make concessions and calling for tougher sanctions against the military rulers. In an increasingly desperate attempt to get a quick solution, Ambassador Pezzullo conceived a power-sharing plan with some elements of the military that Father Aristide rejected as immoral. Mr. Pezzullo was forced to acknowledge at a Congressional hearing that the United States had hand-picked the members of Parliament who were proposing the plan, helped them draft it and paid for their plane tickets so they could present it to Father Aristide in Washington.
After Father Aristide started a public relations offensive accusing the Administration of racism and won the support of lawmakers who demonstrated in front of the White House, the Administration announced a shift in policy.
Mr. Tarnoff called in Mr. Pezzullo on Thursday to tell him that the Administration was embarking on a new course. Mr. Pezzullo submitted his resignation to Secretary of State Warren Christopher two days later.
 
Latest Shift
Painful Choice Of More Sanctions
 In an interview with on NBC's "Meet the Press" in November, Mr. Clinton expressed reluctance to back a call by Father Aristide for a near-total embargo of Haiti, saying additional sanctions "will be more painful in the near term to average Haitians who are already suffering."
With the new sanctions proposal, however, Mr. Clinton has decided that the risk of further hurting the poor is worth the long-term goal of restoring democracy.
"It is a fact that the tougher the sanctions, the more you are going to hurt the average Haitian," Mr. Berger, the deputy security adviser, said in an interview. "But we're not going to get this done otherwise."
But the American-led sanctions resolution at the United Nations falls far short of an all-out effort to strangle Haiti's economy.
The American plan bans most trade except for food and medicine, bans non-commercial air traffic, prohibits all countries from admitting senior military and police officers and and urges other countries to follow the American lead and freeze their overseas assets. But it does not go as far as legislation proposed by six senators last week that would also ban all commercial flights, impose mandatory sanctions against third countries that do not abide by the embargo and overturn the policy of returning Haitians intercepted at sea.
"You shut down American Airlines flights and deny Mrs. Cedras the ability to pack up her Louis Vuitton luggage and visit Miami," said Senator Christopher J. Dodd, the Connecticut Democrat who is the driving force behind the legislation, referring to the wife of Lieut. Gen. Raoul Cedras, the Haitian military leader.
And the overall plan that is emerging -- at least for the moment -- offers no strategy for next steps if the sanctions do not work, no timetable for Father Aristide's return, no plan on how to carry out the Governor's Island accord of last summer and no changes in the policy to return forcibly the Haitian boat people seeking political asylum.
Despite the rising wave of terror in Haiti, the Administration has already ruled out the creation of refugee camps along the border with the Dominican Republic and the processing of political asylum claims either on American ships or in third countries, senior Administration officials said.
 
No Desire to Send Troops
 The Pentagon, meanwhile, has made clear in inter-agency meetings that it has no appetite for putting any American troops in Haiti, even as military trainers and civil engineers, until there is a change in the military leadership.
Even some senior officials who are committed to Father Aristide's return to Haiti as soon as possible remain unconvinced that he himself is committed to making the leap from celebrated exile to head of state. That view is shared by Mr. Malval, who said in an interview shortly after his resignation as Prime Minister, "If we were to find another way out of this crisis, I think Aristide would find a way to keep it from happening."
There is a growing sense in some quarters of the Administration that the new strategy is at best a holding mechanism and that Father Aristide may not get back to Haiti before his five-year term ends in 1995.
That, says Mr. Talbott, is simply not true. "The present goal and objective is to restore democracy working through the United Nations and the Organization of American States and return a man whom 70 percent of the people voted for as President," Mr. Talbott said in an interview.
But the United States may have a long way to go before it can convince the military leadership and the people in Haiti of the seriousness of its intentions.
After all, the American Embassy in Port-au-Prince still buys the gasoline to fuel its cars on the black market, in violation of existing economic sanctions. And a generator fueled with contraband gasoline lights the tennis courts at the residence of the American Ambassador at night, even when no one is playing.

HEADLINE: 6,100 TO GIVE UP MUNICIPAL WORK IN GIULIANI OFFER

BYLINE: By STEVEN LEE MYERS 

 BODY:
The administration of Mayor Rudolph W. Giuliani said yesterday that at least 6,100 municipal workers had accepted voluntary severance packages to give up their jobs, an important first step in the new Mayor's drive to cut the city work force and reduce the budget.
The number fell short of the Mayor's severance goal of 7,600, however, and Mr. Giuliani said he might still have to resort to layoffs to cut the payroll further. 
As the deadline for workers to take the severance passed last night, the Mayor said that despite what he characterized as overall success, there were some agencies, including the Department of Transportation, where so few workers had agreed to take the offer that he now had to consider "very targeted" layoffs in those agencies.
More broadly, Mr. Giuliani warned that even more layoffs could be necessary this year if he falls short in other parts of his plan to cut a total of 15,000 municipal jobs through voluntary severance and attrition and to close a $2.3 billion budget gap for the fiscal year that begins on July 1.
"There are other variables, including what happens with the other aspects of the budget in which we intended to get savings," the Mayor said at City Hall. "If we can get those, then we avoid the need for layoffs. If we can't, then the possibility of layoffs is there."
The administration had hoped that the severance program, offered after intense negotiations with the municipal unions, would cut a total of 7,600 of the 216,000 jobs paid for entirely by the city. By 4 P.M., only about 6,100 workers had accepted the severance packages, which ranged from $3,500 to $15,000 and other benefits, depending on years of service.
The city's Personnel Director, Lilliam Barrios-Paoli, said the final figure could change as late applications trickle in and as workers reconsider, which they are allowed to do until May 13. But the Mayor said he could begin deciding as soon as today whether layoffs would be necessary and where.
Throughout the sprawling municipal government, the severance deadline ended four weeks of opportunity, of sadness and of anxiety. For some, the decision came easily. For others, it proved wrenching. And until the end, many workers wavered, trying to decide whether to turn their backs on Civil Service careers or simply to walk away from the security of a job.
"I couldn't give you a black or white reason," David R. Zuckerman, 53, said of his decision to quit as a lawyer for the Probation Department after six years. "I have a chance to have $5,000, to look for something else and to take a break for a while."
But Billye Mayo, who works for the Probation Department, said, "Economically, it wasn't feasible" for her to take the offer.
"In the economy today, $15,000 doesn't last very long at all," she said.
Although the program ended up short of its target, Mr. Giuliani declared it a success that moved him toward his goal of cutting a total of 15,000 jobs by June 1995. The Mayor has committed himself to cut that many jobs to persuade the state's Municipal Assistance Corporation to free up a $200 million surplus to pay for the severance program.
"The program is more successful at this stage than we thought it would be," the Mayor said.
Eugene J. Keilin, the corporation's chairman, said the Mayor still had further to go. "It's a good start," he said, "but it's a long season."
 
'A Good Sign'
 Stanley Hill, executive director of the city's largest union, District Council 37 of the American Federation of State, County and Municipal Employees, agreed that the number of takers was "a good sign." But he disputed the need for any layoffs. "I think we should also recognize that we can find other ways to balance the budget than layoffs," he said.
The severance program marked the first significant, concrete step in Mr. Giuliani's pledges to wrestle down the size of the city's work force, which was slashed drastically to 160,000 after the fiscal crises of the 1970's only to climb steadily upward to 221,000 by 1990.
In addition to reductions through the severance program, the Mayor has proposed cutting an additional 7,400 jobs, including 2,500 at the Board of Education, to bring the total number of jobs to 201,000 over the next 14 months. The other cuts are planned to come through attrition and stricter adherence to a hiring freeze.
With the severance offer now closed, the administration will begin reassigning thousands of workers between agencies, filling what the Mayor's office determines as essential positions vacated through the severance plan. The Mayor said the process, agreed to by the unions for the first time, would prove as important as the severance payments since it allows his administration to redeploy workers from unneeded positions.
With more than 2,000 Civil Service titles and categories, the redeployment effort will be complicated and fraught with pitfalls. The administration, for example, expects to have to refill some positions vacated through severance because qualified replacements may not be found in the current work force. "Some people will have to be rehired," Ms. Barrios-Paoli said, "but we will explore every other possibility before that."
 
Below Severance Targets
 Most agencies reached or exceeded the severance targets, but others came in far below, including the Transportation Department, where preliminary figures showed only 250 of a targeted 1,000 workers took severance. The Department of Homeless Services had only 200 of a target of 680, while the Correction Department reached only 160 of 500.
It was those agencies that Mr. Giuliani identified as facing possible layoffs. Another agency that fell even farther short of its target was the Social Services Department, where 2,100 workers of an expected 3,600 accepted the severance. But one official said the agency would avert layoffs since the extra workers could be redeployed to other agencies.
The Mayor said the decision on layoffs would be made once his aides determine how successful they will be in reshuffling the remaining workers.
 
Impact on Services
 Another uncertainty that remains is the impact of the reductions on services. Mr. Giuliani said that would not be fully known until the redeployment was completed -- after he presents his executive budget to the City Council on May 10.
One worker who accepted severance said it was those impending cuts that prompted her to leave. Anita C. Vitale, a manager at the Division of AIDS Services, said she decided to leave the city payroll after 26 years after sitting through meetings at the Human Resources Administration to decide how to deal with the AIDS patients the division serves if in fact it is cut by the Mayor, as proposed.
"I couldn't participate in the destruction of this program," she said. She stands to receive $15,000 in cash as well as unused sick leave and vacation time, but she broke into tears as she pondered her decision. "I felt," she said, "like we were doing something really extraordinary."
John Valles, assistant commissioner in the Department of Transportation, said he was not ready to leave city government. "I love what I'm doing, and I don't feel I've completed my mission in Civil Service," he said.

HEADLINE: U.S. Expects U.N. to Toughen Haiti Embargo

BYLINE: By PAUL LEWIS, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: UNITED NATIONS, April 28

 BODY:
A senior Clinton Administration official predicted today that the United Nations Security Council would vote next week to impose tough new sanctions on Haiti in its latest effort to force the military dictatorship there to step down and allow the return of the country's exiled President, the Rev. Jean-Bertrand Aristide.
The American representative to the United Nations, Madeleine K. Albright, said that there was now "a major consensus" in favor of the Administration's latest plan for stepping up pressure on Haiti's military rulers, who overthrew President Aristide in 1991. Ms. Albright said she expects the Security Council to endorse the plan early next week. "Our goal, as always, is the restoration of democracy in Haiti," she said at a news conference here. 
 The American draft resolution, circulated to other Council members today, puts an immediate ban on all noncommercial flights to and from the impoverished country. This is intended to end Haiti's role as a staging post for international drug smugglers, one of its last remaining sources of foreign currency. The proposal also prohibits all countries from admitting some 600 of Haiti's senior military and police officers and urges them to freeze the overseas assets of these officers.
 
15 Days to Consider
 But if the top members of the military Government -- including Lieut. Gen. Raoul Cedras, its leader, Philippe Biamby, his chief of staff, and the police chief, Michel Francois -- have not stepped down within 15 days and started to carry out the Governors Island agreement providing for Father Aristide's return to power, then the new Security Council resolution imposes a total commercial embargo on all trade with Haiti.
Administration officals say the two-week delay gives Haiti's military rulers time to reflect on their fate if they do not hand over power. It also delays the closing down of some industries that might be hard to restart.
The only exceptions to the total trade embargo, which is similar to those the Council has already imposed on Iraq and the rump Yugoslavia, are relief items, basic foods and the exporting of mangoes so that Haitians will not be tempted to cut down their mango trees for firewood.
The speed with which the Administration now wants the Security Council to act against Haiti and the publicity the United States mission here is giving its new initiative all suggest Washington is anxious to be seen to mean business this time. This follows the failure of its efforts to restore democratic rule to Haiti and renewed criticism of the Administration from Father Aristide himself, which led to the dismissal this week of President Clinton's special envoy to Haiti, Lawrence A. Pezzulo.
The complete ban on trade with Haiti extends to the whole world and makes mandatory the present voluntary embargo the United States and other members of the Organization of American States have imposed on Haiti and which some of their companies circumvent.
 
Economy Near Collapse
 As a result of this trade ban and the existing Security Council oil embargo against Haiti, Administration officials say the Haitian economy is teetering on the brink of total collapse. Almost all foreign trade has stopped, inflation is running at 60 percent a year and Haiti lacks 70 percent of its normal fuel requirements.
About 70 percent of the working population is thought to be unemployed and an equal percentage of the country's industrial capacity is idle. Electricity is available for only about four hours a day in the capital, Port-au-Prince, but virtually nowhere else.
In the past the Administration has hesitated to call for a total trade embargo because Washington felt this would deal a devastating blow to what little economic life remains there and because it feared the added hardship would fall mainly on the poor rather than on their military rulers.
Also, a total embargo risked sending a wave of Haitian refugees across the Caribbean into Florida and other parts of the United States.
 
New Refugee Stations
 Administration officials said today that the United States plans to set up two new refugee processing stations in Haiti to interview Haitians seeking admission to the United States as refugees because they fear persecution from the Haitian Government. Currently some 15,000 Haitians have applied for refugee status. About 3,000 have been admitted while 55,000 more are thought to be interested in applying but have not yet done so.
The United States said today that it would press the Dominican Republic, which shares the island of Hispaniola with Haiti, to tighten sanctions along the border to prevent gasoline and other goods from being sent into Haiti.
The Administration also plans to ask the United Nations and the Organization of American States to increase the number of human rights monitors in Haiti. And officials say the United Nations is looking into the possibility of sending back the small peacekeeping force charged with retraining the armed forces and improving communications in Haiti.

HEADLINE: Economy Grows 2.6% In Quarter

BYLINE: By ROBERT D. HERSHEY Jr., Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, April 28

 BODY:
Throttling back from a breakneck autumn, the economy grew at a moderate 2.6 percent rate in the first three months of 1994 as bad weather, the California earthquake and weak exports restrained activity, the Commerce Department reported today.
At the same time, some gauges of inflation moved higher and some traders and investors concluded that the new data understated the pace of growth. 
 This spelled bad news for the bond market, where traders and investors are still concerned about a jump in inflation in the months ahead and are expecting the Federal Reserve, which has already raised short-term interest rates three times this year, to push them even higher soon. The price of the 30-year bond dropped sharply while the yield shot up to 7.26 percent from 7.10 percent on Tuesday. [Page D12.]
 
Different Conclusions Reached
 But some economists and Clinton Administration officials reached different conclusions from those of the bond market. They argued that the economy was growing solidly but with relatively low risk of a significant revival of inflation, although the rise in gross domestic product -- the total output of goods and services within the nation's boundaries -- was somewhat below expectations.
"Over all, today's report should calm fears that the economy is growing at an unsustainably rapid rate or that inflation is about to spike upwards," the President's chief economic adviser, Laura D'Andrea Tyson, said in a statement.
The economy's 2.6 percent January-March pace of expansion followed a fourth quarter in which growth had once been calculated as high as 7.5 percent before being revised to 7 percent, still the highest in almost 10 years.
While almost nobody expected that pace to be sustained, some analysts had worried that the economy was not cooling off enough to avoid reigniting inflation. In early February, for the first time since 1989, the Federal Reserve began raising short-term interest rates, lifting its foot from the monetary accelerator and moving toward a tap on the brakes.
Today's G.D.P. results, however, were said by economists to cast little light on whether the Fed acted too soon, although some Congressional Democrats stepped up complaints that the central bank was pushing rates higher more because of a hunch about future inflation than on evidence of current price pressures.
 
 
'Misguided' Actions
 Senator Jim Sasser, Democrat of Tennessee, the head of the Budget Committee, called the Fed's moves "completely misguided," finding in today's report "absolutely no sign of accelerating inflation."
The first quarter "was significantly distorted and constrained by Mother Nature," said James E. Annable, chief economist for the First National Bank of Chicago.
For example, growth of home building slowed, and other types of public and private construction declined. The Commerce Department also said that consumer spending later in the quarter might not have fully made up for weakness in January.
On the other hand, inventories rose sharply as severe weather prevented sales or even shipments of many goods, adding 1.7 points to first-quarter output but generating a stockpile to inhibit growth later.
"The seeds of a slowdown are planted in this report," Mr. Annable said, forecasting a modest second-quarter rebound to a pace of 3.5 percent or more, followed by a slackening to 2.5 percent or less in the second half of the year as consumers run out of cash and borrowing power.
Despite the bad weather, personal consumption remained brisk over the winter, climbing at a 3.8 percent rate after a 4.4 percent burst during the fourth quarter.
The inflation gauges related to G.D.P. also posed an interpretive challenge. The fixed-weight index for gross domestic purchases -- reflecting prices paid by residents of the United States -- showed a rate of 2.3 percent, reassuringly unchanged from the preceding quarter.
 
Deflator Jumps to 2.6%
 But the overall fixed-weight index climbed to 2.9 from 2.3 percent and the so-called deflator jumped to 2.6 percent from what Ms. Tyson and others regarded as an unsustainably low 1.3 percent in the fourth quarter.
A fixed-weight index measures an unchanging group of items while the deflator is adjusted for shifts in what is actually produced or bought during the quarter.
"I'd be more concerned if it was the fixed-weight" index that was rising, commented Kathryn L. Kobe, vice president of Joel Popkin & Company, a Washington consult specializing in wage and price analysis. "We don't see any signs of real acceleration in inflation."
A department economist dismissed the rise in the overall fixed-weight index by attributing it to export prices and to an annual pay increase for Government employees.
Expressed in current dollars, rather than in 1987 dollars, G.D.P. rose at a 5.2 percent pace in the first quarter, down from 8.4 percent in the final months of 1993, the report also showed.
 
 
Less Savings
 Reflecting the jump in inventories, real final sales of domestic product rose just nine-tenths of 1 percent in the latest period, down from 6.8 percent in the fourth quarter.
Personal savings fell to 3.7 percent of disposable income from 4 percent over the same periods.
The department also reported that its monthly tally of orders for durable goods, those intended to last at least three years, showed a rise of four-tenths of 1 percent in March. This was somewhat below expectations, but analysts were heartened to see that February orders were revised to show a smaller, 1.9 percent decline instead of the 2.6 percent drop that was initially posted.
Moreover, excluding transportation equipment, new orders gained 1 percent, the 10th straight increase, and nonmilitary capital goods other than aircraft rose another 2.8 percent.
In still other indicators today, the Labor Department reported that first-time claims for unemployment insurance tumbled 31,000 last week, reversing recent increases and bringing the level down to 333,000.
And the Conference Board said its index of help-wanted advertising rose two points in March, to 117, following a 10-point February jump.
Data included in the gross domestic product for the fourth quarter of 1993 follow, with all dollar amounts in billions at seasonally adjusted annual rates. Percentage changes are from the previous quarter at seasonally adjusted annual rates.

HEADLINE: Galbraith, a Former Envoy, Enters the Gubernatorial Race

BYLINE: By JAMES DAO, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: ALBANY, April 28

 BODY:
Evan G. Galbraith, a businessman who was Ambassador to France in the Reagan Administration, formally announced his candidacy today for the Republican nomination for governor, joining a field that now numbers five.
In brilliant sunshine on the steps of the State Capitol, Mr. Galbraith, 65, outlined a platform to reduce state personal and corporate taxes by about half, institute the death penalty for all homicide-related crimes, eliminate most forms of welfare and cut the state payroll by 25 percent. 
 Saying that only a conservative can win the Republican nomination, he dismissed two more liberal Republican candidates, Bill Green and Richard M. Rosenbaum, and said the real contest will involve himself, State Senator George E. Pataki and Herbert London.
Without offering a detailed economic blueprint, Mr. Galbraith said he would pay for tax cuts by reducing the $62 billion state budget by $10 billion through layoffs, privatization and reduced services. He said that tax cuts would also stimulate economic growth that would increase tax revenues.
He said that Gov. Mario M. Cuomo, a Democrat seeking his fourth term, "doesn't know much about economics," and blamed him for the loss of 600,000 jobs in the state during the deep recession of the last five years.
But he saved his toughest words for the chairman of the state Republican Party, William D. Powers, whom he accused of using party resources to assist Mr. Pataki, a violation of state laws. He called Mr. Powers "our No. 1 thug."
John E. Sweeney, the party's executive director, said that although Mr. Powers has publicly expressed support for Mr. Pataki, he has not used party money to help him.
"Everyone knows Evan Galbraith is a failed candidate except Evan Galbraith," Mr. Sweeney said. "He's acting out desperately, and that's sad."
 
'Modestly Rich'
 A graduate of Yale College and Harvard Law School, Mr. Galbraith has been a managing director of Dillon Read and Company and is chairman of LVMH Moet Hennessy-Louis Vuitton, which makes and imports cognac, perfume and other luxury products. He is also on the board of National Review magazine.
Calling himself "not rich rich, but modestly rich," Mr. Galbraith said he is paid about $900,000 a year. But he said he didn't expect to spend more than $100,000 of his own money on a campaign because "I have a lot of rich friends who have been as afflicted by Cuomo as I have."

GRAPHIC: Photo: Evan G. Galbraith speaking yesterday in Manhattan where he formally announced his candidacy for the Republican nomination for governor. (Jack Manning/The New York Times)

HEADLINE: Report Sees Rise in AIDS For Children

BYLINE: By STEVEN A. HOLMES, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, April 28

 BODY:
A rising number of infant and childhood deaths from AIDS is beginning to set back years of hard-won improvements in childhood survival rates in the third world, the Census Bureau said in a report today.
The report, which makes projections on AIDS in 13 sub-Saharan countries, Brazil, Thailand and Haiti, predicted that unless there was a sharp slowing in the rate of infection in these countries, childhood mortality rates would triple by the year 2010. 
 For example, bureau demographers estimated that 209 of every 1,000 children would die in Malawi in the year 2010 as a result of AIDS, compared with estimates of 136 without the disease. In Thailand, the child mortality rate would be 103 out of 1,000, as against an estimated 21 without the disease.
Until the onset of the epidemic in the mid-1980's, these countries, like the other developing nations analyzed in the report, had seen marked improvement in the survival rates of children from birth to 5 years of age.
 
AIDS to Double Death Rate
 The report, compiled by the bureau's Center for International Research, also predicted that AIDS would double the overall death rate expected to occur naturally from 1985 to 2010 in the African countries the center studied, and in Brazil and Haiti. In Thailand, the researchers said, the epidemic would triple the expected mortality rate.
As a result of the large number of adults in their 30's and 40's who will die from AIDS, and the large number of infants who are infected in their mothers' wombs, life expectancy rates that have been rising over the last 40 years have started to fall in a number of countries.
For example, the report predicted that by 2010, the average life expectancy in Uganda would drop to 32 years. Without AIDS it would be 59. In Haiti, bureau demographers estimated, life expectancy would drop to 44 years from 59 years.
It is the rate of childhood survival in the third world that will be most significantly affected by AIDS, researchers said. Because 94 percent of the AIDS cases in sub-Saharan Africa are the result of heterosexual intercourse, a higher percentage of women become infected during their childbearing years than is the case in the West. But because it may be years before a person infected with the AIDS virus develops symptoms, women may not realize they carry the virus until after they have passed it on to their children in utero.
 
AIDS Cases at 14 Million
 The predictions are included in "World Population Profile: 1994," a report prepared by the bureau every two years and tracks worldwide population growth, fertility, mortality and contraceptive use. This year's report includes a chapter on the effects of AIDS on population trends.
It notes that despite the 14 million people estimated to be infected with H.I.V., the virus that causes AIDS, the epidemic will not cause a decline in the population in most third world nations, including sub-Saharan Africa, where the World Health Organization has estimated 8 million people are infected.
But it will slow population growth. The demographers estimate that there will be 121 million fewer people in some of the most highly affected countries in Africa like Uganda, Zaire, Zambia and Zimbabwe -- as well as in Haiti, Brazil and Thailand -- by 2010 because of the epidemic than if there had been no exposure to AIDS.
One country whose population is expected decline as a result of AIDS is Thailand, where the report predicts a change from a growth rate of 0.9 percent in 1985 to a loss of 0.8 percent by 2010. Researchers say Thailand already has a low birth rate and its death rate is expected to triple by 2010 because of the disease.
 
Economies to Be Hard Hit
 Moreover, the Census Bureau and researchers from the World Bank say, AIDS will devastate the economies of many countries in sub-Saharan African, already some of the world's poorest nations.
"Economic growth will be encumbered by increased morbidity and decreased productivity of H.I.V.-infected workers, and many others will be unable to enter the work force because of care-giving responsibilities among families," the report said.
Not only will AIDS strike down many workers in their most productive years, but research has also found that the disease disproportionately strikes at these countries' more educated populations.
Studies have shown that in many developing nations, especially Africa, men with more education and higher status tend to engage in more encounters of casual sex, and were thus more likely to be exposed to the virus. As a result, the disease has infected a higher proportion of the class of men whose education tended to make them more productive workers.

HEADLINE: Dow Down 31.23 Points, Tracking a Bond Selloff

BYLINE: By Bloomberg Business News 

 BODY:
Stocks fell sharply yesterday, shaken by the bond market's selloff after a Government report raised investors' worries about inflation.
Surprisingly strong earnings from Johnson & Johnson and the General Motors Corporation cushioned the stock market's fall. The Dow Jones industrial average closed down 31.23 points, to 3,668.31. Weighing heavily on stocks was the bond market's rout. 
 "The reversal in bonds is killing the stock market," William Lord, vice president in equity trading at UBS Securities Inc., said. "All the good earnings in the world aren't going to stop this."
Bond prices plunged and yields surged after the Government's estimate of gross domestic product growth in the first quarter fanned expectations that the Federal Reserve would raise interest rates at its next policy meeting in May. The yield on the Treasury's 30-year bond jumped to 7.26 percent, from 7.10 percent on Tuesday.
Both the stock and bond markets were closed Wednesday in memory of former President Richard M. Nixon.
 
Nasdaq Index Falls 2.52 Points
 Most broader stock market indexes also retreated. The Standard & Poor's 500-stock index lost 2.77 points, to 449.10, and the Nasdaq composite index fell 2.52 points, to 731.69.
Bucking the trend, the American Stock Exchange's market value index rose 0.72 point, to 438.92.
The Commerce Department's estimate for first-quarter G.D.P. growth steered both stocks and bonds. The Government said G.D.P. growth slowed to a 2.6 percent annual rate in the first quarter from the fourth-quarter's 7 percent pace.
But one inflation measure in the G.D.P. report, the so-called price deflator, was higher than expected. It rose to a 2.6 percent annual rate in the first quarter from a 1.3 percent pace in the fourth quarter. Many economists had expected a 2.3 percent increase.
That rise fueled concern that the Fed may push up short-term rates for a fourth time this year to keep inflation under control. Since Feb. 4, the Fed has raised its target for its Federal funds rate, the interest rate on overnight bank loans, to 3.75 percent from 3 percent.
"Everybody fully expects the Fed to raise rates to 4 percent," Barry Berman, head trader at Robert W. Baird, said.
Declining stocks outpaced advancers, 1,275 to 936, on the New York Stock Exchange. Trading was active, with about 323 million shares changing hands on the Big Board.
 
Oil Stocks See Selloff
 Oil stocks were among yesterday's biggest decliners, reflecting a drop in petroleum prices. Chevron slid 2 1/4, to 88 5/8; Exxon dropped 1, to 61 7/8, and Mobil shed 2 1/4, to 77 3/8. Weak oil prices contributed to the decline, with crude oil for June delivery falling 34 cents, to $16.57 a barrel on the New York Mercantile Exchange.
Stocks briefly pared some losses around midday. The catalyst was a rebound in auto shares led by G.M.'s optimistic comments to shareholders in a conference call yesterday morning.
G.M. posted first-quarter earnings from operations of $1.86 a share, exceeding analysts' estimates of about $1.50 a share.
G.M., which had fallen as much as 2 1/8 early in the day, later rose as much as 1 1/8 following the conference call. In the end, though, G.M. closed unchanged at 56 3/4.
Ford Motor and Chrysler rebounded briefly along with G.M. before the broader market's selloff erased their gains.
Strong earnings reports helped a number of other stocks. Johnson & Johnson advanced 1 3/8, to 41 5/8, after it posted an increase of nearly 8.2 percent in first-quarter profits. Also, Xerox rallied 4 1/4, to 100 7/8; America Online vaulted 5 1/4, to 68 3/4; Cirrus Logic soared 3 3/8, to 36 3/8, and Premark International leaped 5 3/4, to 78 5/8.
Among biotechnology stocks, Sciclone Pharmaceuticals plunged 79/16 , to 53/16 , and Alpha 1 Biomedicals skidded 43/16 , to 2, after preliminary tests showed that an Alpha drug licensed to Sciclone might not be effective against chronic hepatitis B.
Meanwhile, Molecular Biosystems jumped 2, to 18, after the Food and Drug Administration granted it an "approvable letter" for one of its diagnostic imaging products.
Agco rallied 5 3/8, to 42, after it agreed on Tuesday to acquire the Massey Ferguson tractor-making division from Varity for $328 million.
In Tokyo, Japanese stocks ended lower yesterday. The Nikkei average of 225 stocks slipped 3.90 points, to 19,725.25. The Tokyo stock market is closed today for the Greenery Day national holiday.

HEADLINE: Meadowlands Sports Agency Proposing a Direct Rail Link

BYLINE: By CLIFFORD J. LEVY, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: EAST RUTHERFORD, N.J., April 28

 BODY:
Seeking to keep pace in the furiously evolving entertainment business, the New Jersey Sports and Exposition Authority today unveiled an $850 million plan for its Meadowlands complex that calls for a rail line to lure visitors to a grab bag of new attractions, from a conference center to an amphitheater to an ice rink.
At the core of the project is a proposal to ease the increasingly burdensome traffic congestion in the area by using about $133 million in Federal transportation funds to lay down the one-mile rail line. It would allow some of the millions of visitors who come for Giants football, Nets basketball or a host of other events every year to ride trains from Manhattan and New Jersey to the center of the complex. 
 The planned expansion in the Meadowlands, scheduled to be finished by the end of the decade, represents a risky venture for an agency that carved a lucrative enclave from the swamps of northern New Jersey in the 1970's, only to fall on such difficult times in recent years that it has required state aid.
To succeed, the authority must not only obtain financing and approval from many Federal and state officials and developers, but also correctly predict the future leisure tastes of an often fickle public. The authority's strategy is to pile choice upon choice, hoping, for example, that people might attend a football game, browse through retail stores offering sporting goods and clothing and then have a picnic in a new tailgating area or maybe a drink at a sports bar -- all without leaving the complex.
"We now have to look for other sources of revenues and be creative in the ability to generate those revenues," Michael D. Francis, the authority's chairman, said at a news conference this morning.
Perhaps the biggest challenge for the authority is to persuade the state to spend about $55 million to relocate Route 120, which now runs through the complex. That move would open up space for a private developer to build numerous projects: a 600-room hotel; a television studio; a 500-seat theater; restaurants and sports bars; retail stores, especially those selling sporting goods; health clubs; museums, picnic areas and office buildings.
 
New Train Station in Secaucus
 Gov. Christine Todd Whitman, who can veto the Meadowlands expansion, has not yet announced her position on it. Her past criticism of using state money for such projects might indicate that she would reject it. But Mr. Francis, whom she appointed and who was a political fund-raiser for her, said today that he was confident that both the Governor and State Legislature would support it. The Assembly has scheduled a May 19 hearing on the project.
The rail line would connect the Meadowlands to a station in nearby Secaucus that is to be built by the end of the decade with nearly $400 million already allocated by Congress. That station, known as the Secaucus transfer, is supposed to alleviate overcrowding by linking many of the state's rail lines and providing easy access to and from Manhattan.
Representative Robert G. Torricelli, the Democrat who represents the Meadowlands area, said that if New Jersey's elected officials united to lobby Congress, he and the rest of the state's Congressional delegation should be able to obtain approval for the money for the rail line to the Meadowlands. But he said that if that effort failed, he and many others would oppose the expansion because the rail line was so crucial.
"The proposal is a well-thought-out, balanced and reasoned approach to getting more out of the asset of the sports complex," Mr. Torricelli said. "But since my highest priorities are the local interests, my position can go from enthusiastically supporting to doggedly trying to defeat it depending on whether or not there is a mass-transportation component."
The authority estimates that the building of the new attractions would cost a private developer about $220 million. Mr. Francis said today that he and Robert E. Mulcahy 3d, the authority's president, had spoken with several interested developers, including Melvin Simon, who has built some of the largest malls in the country. The developers would pay the authority for the rights to build.
In today's dollars, the transit plan and private development would cost $400 million to $500 million, Mr. Francis said. When inflation and other costs are included over the length of the project, the price tag rises to about $850 million on completion.
 
No Baseball for Now
 With the expansion, the authority, which has more than 3,000 full- and part-time workers, is seeking to bolster revenues that have suffered in recent years as attendance has dropped sharply at its race track. Because its balance sheet has suffered, the authority has been forced to ask the state for assistance in servicing its debt: $17 million this year, which is expected to rise to $26 million next year.
The expansion at this point focuses on enlarging the 750-acre complex, which includes Giants Stadium, Byrne Arena and the Meadowlands race track, and does not include the construction of a baseball stadium in the area. That issue has loomed large because George M. Steinbrenner 3d, the owner of the Yankees, has threatened to move the team from the South Bronx, saying that he is unhappy with the neighborhood and the condition of Yankee Stadium.
If he abandoned New York for the Meadowlands, Mr. Steinbrenner would be following a path already trodden by the Giants and Jets of the National Football League. But a baseball stadium is apparently not feasible now because of its enormous cost and land constraints. And Governor Whitman has said she refuses to spend taxpayers' money to coax sports teams to New Jersey.
The expansion has generated intense interest in nearby towns, with some residents bemoaning the possibility of ever-greater swarms of people converging on the area, and others allowing that the project might be good for local business. Most everyone is concerned about traffic, mindful of the football Sundays when the roadways become insufferable.
"If their theory about the commuter situation is correct, then I don't think it will impact at all," said Mayor Dominick Presto of Carlstadt, referring to the authority's decision to rely on the rail line to unclog the highways. "But if they are wrong, it's going to be a bad, bad situation."

HEADLINE: Rifkin Shows Severe Psychosis, Expert Says

BYLINE: By JOHN T. McQUISTON, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: MINEOLA, L.I., April 28

 BODY:
Joel Rifkin tested "off the scale" as a paranoid schizophrenic, showing an extreme inability to think logically or experience time, a clinical psychologist testified today in the trial of Mr. Rifkin, who has confessed to strangling 17 women.
"His mind is like a videotape in that it fast-forwards, then suddenly rewinds," said the psychologist, Dr. Barbara R. Kirwin, who has worked with the criminally insane for 20 years. "There is no normal time sequence in his thinking." 
 Dr. Kirwin, the first and only witness for the defense, said she had spent more than 170 hours studying and testing Mr. Rifkin. She said the results showed that "at the time of the offenses, Mr. Rifkin suffered from a mental disease of such severe pervasiveness as to impair his ability to know the nature of his actions."
 
'Extremely Volatile'
 Since the defense has acknowledged that Mr. Rifkin killed 17 women, the jury is being asked to determine only whether he will spend the rest of his life in prison or in a state hospital for the criminally insane.
Dr. Kirwin said Mr. Rifkin's mental illnesses were made worse because he was "an extremely volatile, hypersensitive person, one who is likely to react to the slightest emotional nuance in a psychotic way."
Under questioning by the defense attorney, John Lawrence, Dr. Kirwin told the jury that Mr. Rifkin had difficulty "making sense out of the world," that he could "not always trust what he heard or saw one day to the next," which made him "unsure of what was fantasy and what was reality."
But when asked by Mr. Lawrence if she had found any evidence of sexual perversion in Mr. Rifkin, Dr. Kirwin said, "No, I did not."
This appeared to contradict testimony during a pretrial hearing by another psychiatrist who said that Mr. Rifkin had taken mementos from his victims so he could later achieve sexual satisfaction by reliving the encounters.
Dr. Kirwin, before beginning a private practice in Huntington, L.I., in 1986, was director of administration for the treatment of violent inmates at the Creedmoor Psychiatric Center, a state hospital for the criminally insane. She has appeared numerous times as an expert witness for the prosecution in Nassau County Court, but this was her first appearance in Nassau for the defense. She said she was being paid $165 an hour.
 
An Attentive Defendant
 Mr. Rifkin appeared attentive as she testified. He sat quietly at the defense table, dressed in a conservative business suit and tie.
He is on trial for the murder of his 17th victim, 22-year-old Tiffany Bresciani, whom he picked up in lower Manhattan on June 24, paid $20 for sex, then strangled. He was arrested in Nassau County four days later while trying to dispose of her body, which was found in the back of his pickup truck by state troopers who stopped him for a traffic violation.
Mr. Rifkin, a 34-year-old unemployed landscaper from East Meadow, L.I., subsequently confessed to killing Ms. Bresciani and 16 other women over a four-year period and of dumping their bodies in Suffolk County, Brooklyn, Queens and the Bronx, where he also faces murder charges.
The Nassau prosecutor, Fred Klein, is expected to cross-examine Dr. Kirwin when she returns to the witness stand on Friday. He was limited today to asking procedural questions about evidence.
In an apparent effort to unnerve the witness and discredit her testimony, Mr. Klein fired a series of questions at her about some handwritten comments on Mr. Rifkin's test results, which the defense had submitted as evidence.
"I'm sorry, I'm confused by your questions," Dr. Kirwin said as the heads of the jurors moved back and forth as if at a tennis match.
"I'm sorry you're confused, Dr. Kirwin," said Mr. Klein.
 
'Notes? What Notes?'
 The brief exchange was a hint of things to come. Shortly before 3 P.M., as Dr. Kirwin referred to some notes she had taken on earlier tests of Mr. Rifkin by another psychologist, Mr. Klein startled the jury by suddenly jumping to his feet and shouting: "Notes? What notes? I have never seen those notes."
The prosecution is entitled to all notes related to the testing of Mr. Rifkin.
Judge Ira H. Wexner asked the jurors to leave the courtroom so that Mr. Lawrence could supply a set of the notes to the prosecution. Mr. Klein has repeatedly asserted that the defense has been withholding evidence. Not until this afternoon's incident, however, had the jury been aware of the charge, which Mr. Lawrence has repeatedly denied.
The prosecution is to begin calling its own experts on Monday, starting with Dr. Elliot Park Dietz, a forensic psychiatrist who has testified at the trials of John Hinckley Jr. and the serial killers Jeffrey Dahmer and Arthur Shawcross.
Mr. Lawrence said during a break in the trial that it did not matter how many rebuttal witnesses the prosecution put on the stand. "This is not a game of quantity," he said. "It's a game of quality, and I have to rely on the strength of my expert witness."
