HEADLINE: CURRENCY MARKETS;
Dollar Falls to 8-Month Low Vs. Yen on Trade Concerns

BYLINE: By Bloomberg Business News 

 BODY:
The dollar tumbled to an eight-month low against the Japanese yen yesterday amid increasing concern that political jousting in Tokyo would aggravate Japan's long-running trade dispute with the United States.
Heavy selling in the American bond and stock markets also weighed on the dollar. Bonds dropped after a Government economic report showed some signs of inflation. 
 In trading yesterday, the dollar fell as low as 101.30 yen, its lowest since Aug. 19. In New York, it was last quoted at 101.36 yen, down from 102.35 yen on Tuesday. The dollar also dropped to 1.6622 German marks, from 1.6710 marks.
"Everyone is selling dollars for yen because they don't see any progress on the trade front," Dennis Pettit, foreign-exchange manager at Long-Term Credit Bank of Japan, said. "There's no one to buy dollars except the Bank of Japan."
The dollar started falling on Monday after Japan's Socialist Party bolted the coalition Government. That left Prime Minister Tsutomo Hata with a minority in Parliament, raising concern about whether he can restart trade talks.
If the United States does not gain trade concessions from Japan, many traders expect the White House to resume calls for a strong yen, which makes Japanese exports more expensive. The Bank of Japan bought billions of dollars in recent weeks to try to slow the yen's rise, traders said.
Traders were more willing to sell dollars for yen yesterday because Japanese markets will be closed for most of next week for the Golden Week holidays, which start today. The Bank of Japan will probably have to rely more on foreign central banks to slow the yen's rise during the holidays, traders said.
A selloff in the Treasury bond market also prompted dollar sales yesterday, traders said.
The British pound rose to $1.5136 in New York yesterday, from $1.5060 on Tuesday. The dollar fell to 5.6991 French francs from 5.7395 francs and slipped to 1.4126 Swiss francs from 1.4257 francs. The dollar fell to 1,591 Italian lire from 1,608 lire. The Canadian dollar slid to 72.36 United States cents from 72.67 cents.

HEADLINE: FUTURES MARKETS;
Coffee Prices Rise to a High As Fears of Oversupply Ease

BYLINE: By Bloomberg Business News 

 BODY:
Coffee prices climbed to a two-and-a-half-year high yesterday after exporters said they would reduce their inventories gradually rather than flood the market.
Coffee exporters have been holding coffee off the world market since October in an effort to bolster prices. Traders had been concerned that exporters would rush to sell coffee stockpiles once prices rose high enough. 
 But officials from the Association of Coffee Producing Countries said on Tuesday that coffee would not be dumped on the market.
When coffee supplies are sold, said Eduardo Libreros, an official with the exporters group in New York, "we will do that in a very, very careful way."
Coffee for July delivery, the most active contract, rose 3.95 cents yesterday, to 88.55 cents a pound, its highest price since October 1991 on the New York Coffee, Sugar and Cocoa Exchange.
 
 
'Less Helter-Skelter'

"That's a plus, because it allows for a less helter-skelter distribution of stocks," said Bill O'Neill, senior futures strategist with Merrill Lynch & Company.
Still, some traders said yesterday's gains would increase the temptation to sell off coffee stockpiles.
"In three or four days, it could be right back down," said John Connolly, managing director with the Alpine Capital Management Group in Englewood Cliffs, N.J. "They've still got a ton of coffee" that will be sold eventually, he said.
On other commodity markets, copper prices gained amid expectations that European economies would soon emerge from recession. Copper for July delivery gained 2.15 cents, to 91.40 cents a pound, its highest price in more than a month on the Commodity Exchange of New York.
 
Gold Prices Gain
 Gold prices also rose on the Commodity Exchange, with the June futures contract, the most active, gaining $1.50, to $376.80 an ounce.
On the Chicago Mercantile Exchange, lumber prices surged their daily trading limit as the lowest lumber prices since October spurred renewed demand. Lumber for May delivery rose $10, to $317.50 for every 1,000 board feet, after rising its limit on Tuesday. Commodity markets were closed on Wednesday in memory of former President Richard M. Nixon.
Warmer weather is letting builders resume construction that was delayed by the winter's severe cold, pushing up lumber demand.
And crude oil prices slumped following a report late Tuesday showing stockpiles of crude and unleaded gasoline rose last week. On the New York Mercantile Exchange, June crude fell 34 cents, to $16.57 a barrel. Unleaded gasoline fell 2.12 cents, to $48.59 a barrel, and heating oil fell 0.85 cent, to $46.52 a barrel.

HEADLINE: Air Quality Inside Jetliners Is Not Harmful, Study Says

BYLINE: By MARTIN TOLCHIN, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, April 28

 BODY:
The reduction of fresh air in airline cabins since the mid-1980's poses no harm to passengers and crew members, according to a study commissioned by the airline industry that was made public today.
"Both older aircraft, with an all-fresh air system, and newer aircraft that have a combination of fresh and recirculated air, meet or exceed standards that are designed to insure a healthy environment," said Jim Landry, president of the Air Transport Association, which financed the study and represents major airlines. 
 Consolidated Safety Services, a Virginia consulting firm that conducted the study for the industry, tested the air in jetliner cabins during 35 flights without notifying the airlines or the crews. It found that the air quality met Federal health and safety standards for workers in offices and factories.
But a spokesman for flight attendants criticized the study on the ground that air quality standards for offices and factories are not appropriate for judging the crowded confines of a loaded airplane.
 
Workplace Standards Used
 Aaron Sussell, an industrial hygienist with the National Institute of Occupational Safety, said the study had improperly used his agency's standards and those of the Occupational Safety and Health Administration. Those standards were meant to protect people in the workplace, he noted, but airplanes serve the general public, which requires more stringent rules because passengers include infants, the elderly and disabled people.
A case in point were carbon dioxide levels as high as 1,395 parts per million. These were well below OSHA's maximum of 5,000 but above the limit of 1,000 parts per million set for the general public by the American Society of Heating, Refrigerating and Air-Conditional Engineers.
In the 1980's, airlines in the United States reduced the amount of fresh air circulated in the cabins, from 100 percent fresh air pumped in every three minutes, to half fresh air and half recirculated air every six or seven minutes. The airline industry says the reduction of fresh air saves an average of $60,000 per jetliner each year because less fuel is needed to cool the air that comes in through the plane's engines.
Since the change was introduced, some flight attendants and passengers have complained of headaches, nausea and other health problems, especially after long flights. Many attribute the problems to the reduced ventilation.
 
House Hearings Planned
 The Air Transport Association's study has disputed this claim. "Results of the study did not reveal a potential for human health hazards," said Dr. Jolanda Janczewski, president of Consolidated Safety Services Inc. of Okaton, Va., which has conducted studies for Federal agencies.
The study was made public in anticipation of Congressional hearings on the issue that are scheduled for next month by the Aviation Subcommittee of the House Public Works and Transportation Committee. "We thought it was important to get this study out in advance of the hearing," Mr. Landry said.
The study measured contaminants aboard 35 flights involving two older planes that provided 100 percent fresh air and two newer aircraft that provide 50 percent fresh air and 50 percent recirculated air. The older aircraft were a Boeing 727 and McDonnell Douglas DC-9.; the newer aircraft were a Boeing 757 and a McDonnell Douglas MD-80.
The newer aircraft generally had a higher level of contaminants than the older aircraft, but these were still within the range allowed by the Labor Department's Occupational Safety and Health Administration and the Public Health Service's National Institute of Occupational Safety and Health, the study said.
"Total and average levels for volatile organic compounds, respirable particulates and bacterial and fungal contaminants were relatively low for all types of aircraft, seating configuration, flight duration and airlines," the study said.
A 1986 study recommended that fully loaded airline cabins have "maximal airflow" of 100 percent fresh air. The study was conducted for Congress by the National Research Council, which includes the National Academy of Sciences, the National Academy of Engineering and the Institute of Medicine.
The study noted that the fresh air varied from less than 7 cubic feet per minute for economy-class passengers to 50 cubic feet per minute for first-class passengers to up to 150 cubic feet per minute for the cockpit, where the air is needed to cool electronic equipment.
Representative Jerrold Nadler, Democrat of Manhattan and Brooklyn, has introduced a bill that would provide a minimum of 20 cubic feet of fresh air per minute for all passengers.

HEADLINE: COMPANY REPORTS;
G.M.'s Profit Jumps 66.5% in Quarter

BYLINE: By JAMES BENNET, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: DETROIT, April 28

 BODY:
With auto sales booming in North and South America and strengthening in Europe, the General Motors Corporation reported today that its first-quarter profits jumped 66.5 percent from a year earlier, to $854 million.
The robust earnings, which at 81 cents a share exceeded the expectations of automotive securities analysts, came despite a previously announced $758 million charge G.M. took to account for benefits for some former employees. Without the charge, G.M., the world's largest auto maker, earned $1.6 billion. 
 In the first three months of the year, G.M.'s revenues climbed 8.5 percent, to $37.5 billion.
 
 
Stock Drops, Then Rebounds
 Immediately after G.M. announced its results, its stock slid as much as $2.125 a share on the New York Stock Exchange in a frenzy of selling that may have been prompted by confusion over whether the recovering North American operations had performed as well as expected. G.M. shares recovered by midday and closed at $56.75, unchanged.
The report showed that the North American operations lost $197 million, compared with a loss of $170 million a year earlier. But excluding the one-time accounting charge, the operations earned $511 million on rising sales.
"The North American turnaround is genuine, and it's very, very good," said Wendy Beale Needham, the auto industry analyst for Donaldson, Lufkin & Jenrette in New York.
Reflecting growing confidence at the company, journalists attending the earnings announcement at G.M. headquarters here were pointedly issued green badges, replacing the black badges issued with fourth-quarter earnings and the red ones of previous quarters.
The profit margin for North American operations improved from eight-tenths of a percent a year earlier to 2.1 percent, before the accounting change. The margin, a measure of G.M.'s success in cutting costs, is still short of the 5 percent goal Mr. Smith has set, said G. Richard Wagoner, the executive vice president who is G.M.'s finance and purchasing chief. "That's in the right direction, but on a long-term basis nowhere near where we want to go," he said. "We are not at all satisfied with the profitability on our car lines."
A shift by buyers during the quarter away from luxury vehicles and toward smaller, less profitable cars like the Chevrolet Cavalier in the United States and the Opel Corsa in Europe also ate into profits, he said.
But Ms. Needham said that growing margins were already generating a handsome return per vehicle. According to her calculations, the net profit per vehicle in North America, before interest or taxes, jumped from $149 in the fourth quarter of 1993 to $650 in the first quarter. A year ago, G.M. was losing $51 a vehicle, on average, in North America. Ms. Needham said the improvement stemmed from "cost reduction in terms of material and head count."
 
 
 
Lower Rebates
 G.M. also reduced rebates to its customers and other financial incentives. Incentive costs per vehicle fell 16 percent from a year earlier, to $800. During the fourth quarter of 1993, incentive costs averaged $880.
G.M.'s international operations contributed $425 million to the bottom line, a threefold increase from a year earlier. Mr. Wagoner attributed the jump in part to surging sales in South America. In Brazil, where auto sales are soaring as customers rush to showrooms to beat inflation, G.M. sales rose 25 percent for the quarter.
"What continues to surprise me is just how strong the overseas business is," said Maryann Keller, an analyst for Furman Selz in New York.
In the United States. G.M.'s car sales outperformed the industry in the first quarter. Its retail car sales increased nearly 19 percent, to 759,407, according to Ward's Automotive Reports. Its sales of light trucks -- including pickups, mini-vans and sport utility vehicles -- rose 21 percent, to 463,805. During the quarter, the car market as a whole was up 15.4 percent, and the truck market rose 22.8 percent.

HEADLINE: Are Happy Days Here Again? Art World Prepares for a Test

BYLINE: By CAROL VOGEL 

 BODY:
ARTISTS may moan that theirs is a lonely profession. But the people who make the international art world move -- the dealers, collectors and auction-house experts -- tend to travel in packs. And their destination, for the next two weeks anyway, is New York City.
Art shows are happening all over Manhattan during this period, at conventional spots like galleries and the Seventh Regiment Armory and at more offbeat sites like a ship terminal and a hotel off Gramercy Park. Each show has been timed to coincide with the spring sales of contemporary, Impressionist and modern art that start at Christie's and Sotheby's on Tuesday evening. (The public viewings of the art that will be auctioned begin this weekend at the two houses.) 
 These semiannual auctions are the only public barometer of the art market, but this season they are also an excuse to capitalize on an optimistic mood that has been fluttering through the art world for the last six months. Some dealers say they are participating in an art show this spring because business is better, and so they have the capital to invest in publicity; others say they believe the exposure can give their business the boost it still needs. Whatever the reasons, everyone is getting into the act: from blue-chip galleries that are mounting special exhibitions to fledgling dealers who will literally be selling art out of their suitcases.
This weekend's biggest show of all, Art New York International, involves 65 dealers from more than 15 countries at Pier 92. It features well-known Manhattan galleries like Andre Emmerich, Robert Miller and Holly Solomon, as well as Waddington Galleries of London, Galerie du Centre of Paris and Busche Galerie of Berlin. The art ranges from a $1 million Dubuffet to the work of emerging artists like Fabian Marcaccio for under $1,000. The show also has a 10,000-square-foot indoor sculpture space, with works by Leger, de Chirico, Robert Indiana and Fernando Botero.
Another serious first-time show, one scheduled around the Impressionist and modern sales, is the International Fine Art Fair, opening at the Seventh Regiment Armory on May 13. Sixty-five international dealers will show paintings, drawings and sculpture from the Renaissance through Impressionism.
When word began circulating about these shows, other galleries became competitive. "We figured there would be a lot of out-of-towners coming to New York," said Ronald Feldman, a SoHo dealer who helped organize a multi-gallery open house, sponsored by the Art Dealers Association of America.
They named it "the best free show in town," Mr. Feldman said, observing that he frequently gets calls asking how much his gallery charges for admission. "These calls are telling," he said. "This isn't just about selling art. We want to broaden our audience." About 40 uptown galleries held an open house last night. A group in SoHo will do the same thing tomorrow morning. Although Saturdays are always heavy-traffic days in SoHo, many galleries will have their artists present to discuss the works.
A more ad hoc event is taking place this weekend at the Gramercy Park Hotel, where 30 conventional hotel rooms are being transformed into live-in art-fair booths with the installation of artworks. Aptly named the Gramercy International, this alternative art fair will concentrate on emerging artists and, consequently, less expensive works. The dealers include well-regarded galleries often on the cutting edge of trends, from David Zwirner, American Fine Arts, Fawbush and Jay Gorney in SoHo to Jay Jopling of London, Ma Galerie of Paris and Walcheturm of Zurich.
"I thought it would be fun," said Paula Cooper, the SoHo gallery owner, who is one of the few long-established dealers in the group. Ms. Cooper said she chose the Gramercy International over Art New York because of her younger artists, including Robert Gober, Rudy Stingel and Tony Feher.
 
Into the Auction Rooms
 The laid-back setting of the Gramercy Hotel couldn't offer a greater contrast to the pristine carpeted walls of Sotheby's and Christie's. But never mind. During the next two weeks, the auction rooms will be filled with more blockbusters than either house has had a chance to sell since the boom years of the 1980's.
For art lovers, the viewings at Sotheby's and Christie's provide a chance to see some historically important works before they disappear into private collections. For art professionals, this spring provides yet another test of the art market. For the last two years, auction house experts have been crying poor. As confidence in the market waned, so did the amount of art for sale. "This spring our volume has increased significantly, both in terms of quality and price," said Christopher Burge, chairman of Christie's in New York. "I guess the time has come to see if we're as good as our word."
Dreamy scenes of Venice by Monet, Cubist works by Picasso as well as vintage Jasper Johnses are all going up for sale, at estimates that are 15 to 20 percent higher than they would have been six months ago. The price levels, Mr. Burge said, correspond to where the market was during the first half of 1988. At the high end, the spring sales offer more paintings with estimated prices of $4 million or more than have been up for sale in three years. "A year or two ago, if you talked about $5 million to $10 million paintings, people winced," said Diana D. Brooks, who heads Sotheby's auctions worldwide. "There's no wincing now."
Most of these big-ticket works come from estates, traditionally the anchor of important auctions. "We have more estate property than we've seen for a while," said Mr. Burge. "You might assume people don't die during a recession, but actually, estates were holding on and not selling unless they absolutely had to. This spring a number of collectors have died and their property has come willingly on the market."
 
Some Loans for the Sellers
 But the process of getting that estate business was so competitive this year that the auction houses reportedly gave sellers substantial loans or advances on the property to be auctioned. This practice challenges the conventional role of the auction house as disinterested agent. Neither Christie's nor Sotheby's would discuss its financing terms.
The splashy advertising and marketing of the spring sales does raise the question of just how large a financial stake the auction houses have in the property they are selling. "Less than people think," said Mrs. Brooks of Sotheby's. At Christie's, Mr. Burge said, "The company does not discuss the arrangements it makes with its sellers."
Christie's is advertising works from two major estates. One, called simply "a European estate," is showcased in its own hard-back catalogue. The property is from the estate of Jacques Koerfer, a German businessman who lived in Switzerland. Among the highlights is Picasso's Cubist work "Violin, Bottle and Glass," from 1913, estimated to sell for $4 million to $6 million. A true connoisseur's painting, it exhibits all the strength and deftness of the finest Cubist compositions. It has been in the same private collection for nearly 30 years and the painting is immaculate, neither lined nor varnished. The same is true for Gauguin's rich 1888 landscape "River L'Aven Through Pont-Aven," from the same collection, which is estimated to bring $5 million to $7 million.
The second estate on view at Christie's is a small, but impressive collection of four Impressionist works that belonged to Neil A. McConnell, a New York-based investment banker who died in May. Mr. McConnell bought primarily from William Acquavella, one of New York's most prominent dealers. Cezanne's "Still Life With Open Drawer" (1877-1879), which Christie's estimates will sell for $4 million to $6 million, "is an amazing painting, all about gravity and the device of the open door," said Michael Findlay, director of the Impressionist and modern art department at Christie's. "It's quite unique because everything is just a bit off center." The McConnell estate also includes a large Monet, "The Mula Palace," a sparkling image from 1908 painted as though the artist was looking at the Gothic facade of the palace in Venice through a zoom lens. Christie's has estimated it will sell for $4 million to $6 million.
 
Another Monet of Venice
 The Monet is on the cover of Christie's catalogue for the May 10 Impressionist and modern art sale. By coincidence, Sotheby's catalogue for its May 11 Impressionist and modern sale also features a Monet scene from Venice, "The Grand Canal and the Santa Maria Della Salute" (1908), one of six variations of the church the artist painted during his first visit to Venice. The painting is being sold by the Onichi Museum in Japan, and Sotheby's estimates it will bring $7 million to $8 million.
Most of the other top works in Sotheby's Impressionist and modern art sale come from the estate of H. Gates Lloyd, a former Deputy Director of Central Intelligence, and his wife, Eleanor, who founded the Washington Gallery of Modern Art. They amassed a collection of great breadth, including a Mondrian, "Composition" (1939-42), that is in pristine condition and is estimated to bring more than $3 million, and Henry Moore's "Festival Reclining Figure," a 1951 bronze created for the 1951 Festival of Britain, a centenary exhibition organized by the Arts Council of Great Britain. The Moore is expected to fetch $1.5 million to $2 million.
Another rare picture from an estate at Sotheby's is Klimt's "Lady With a Fan" (1917-18), a portrait of a beautiful woman against a colorful background of flowers and exotic birds. "It's rare because most of the great Klimts are in Austrian museums," said David Nash, director of the department of Impressionist and modern art at Sotheby's. Mr. Nash estimates the painting will bring $7 million to $9 million. "I'm still looking for solid ground with certain estimates," Mr. Nash said. "It's still difficult to predict what the market will bear."
 
Fresh Signs of Recovery
 It is even more difficult in the field of contemporary art, which has taken longer to crawl out of its slump than the Impressionist and modern market. Six months ago, contemporary art showed the first signs of a recovery since 1990, and the offerings this spring confirm that the worst is over.
"It's the best sale we've had since the market's fall," said Diane Upright, Christie's head of contemporary art, speaking about both quality and prices. "But great things are still difficult to get." Among the few highlights of the Christie's sale, on May 3, are a classic Jackson Pollock drip painting, "No. 22" from 1949, which is expected to bring $2 million to $3 million; Mark Rothko's "Four Reds" (1957), estimated at $1.2 million to $1.8 million, and the Pop icon "Great American Nude No. 2," by Tom Wesselmann, estimated to fetch $150,000 to $200,000. Sellers include Meshulam Riklis, the financier who owns the financially troubled McCrory Corporation, and Michael D. Abrams, of the publishing family. But the Christie's sale pales next to Sotheby's contemporary sale on May 4.
Sotheby's has put together an outstanding group of works, especially strong in 1950's and 60's blue-chip artists from celebrated collections. The painting on the cover of the sale catalogue, for example, is Jasper Johns's "Highway," a 1959 painting that is being sold by Peter Brant, the newsprint manufacturer and owner of Interview Magazine, and his wife, Sandy. The colorful abstract painting depicts the feeling of being caught in traffic with the red and yellow traffic lights on the bottom. It comes from one of the artist's most desirable periods and is estimated to sell for $8 million.
A later, untitled Johns, from 1983, is being sold by the publisher S. I. Newhouse Jr. and his wife, Victoria. It is estimated at $1.5 million to $2 million. Mr. Newhouse has been quietly selling some of his collection for several years now. In this sale, he is also offering Roy Lichtenstein's "Black Flowers," from 1961, which is estimated to bring $1.8 million to $2.5 million, as well as Claes Oldenburg's "7-Up," a wall relief that Sotheby's expects will bring $250,000 to $350,000.
Another rare work for sale that night is David Smith's "Cubi V," 1 of the 28 polished stainless steel sculptures the artist began creating in 1961, which is estimated to fetch $2.5 million. "This sale has all the right ingredients," said Lucy Mitchell-Innes, director of Sotheby's department of contemporary art worldwide. "There's a real hunger out there for 1950's and early 1960's work, which has been rare to the market."
While both auction houses are gearing up for bigger sales than ever, a question mark still hangs over them. "There are two important issues," Mrs. Brooks said. "The first is, how fast is the market coming back? And the second, how many buyers are willing to actually step up to the plate?"

HEADLINE: Abroad at Home;
Miracle With Reasons

BYLINE: By ANTHONY LEWIS 

DATELINE: BOSTON

 BODY:
By the standards of today's world the election in South Africa is a political miracle. Many would have predicted that white domination would end in a cataclysm of violence, not a one-person-one-vote election supported by all major parties and population groups.
But it is a miracle created by human beings. A series of calculated political judgments made possible the relatively peaceful transition to a new South Africa. 
 President F. W. de Klerk made the judgment, long overdue, that white rule could be maintained only at devastating costs, economic and social. He understood that the present leaders of the African National Congress were the most moderate interlocutors the whites could hope to find. He took the dramatic step in February 1990 of releasing Nelson Mandela and his colleagues from prison.
Mr. Mandela took the path of reconciliation from the day of his release. When I interviewed him two months later, I asked whether he favored the prosecution of men who had carried out murders on behalf of the Government. He answered:
"No, no, no. The whole spirit of negotiations would be against taking revenge on any particular individual. You think of a settlement as involving the entire community in support of the settlement. Otherwise it will be an intolerable situation."
That spirit of inclusiveness and reconciliation was carried on through the long negotiations and then the election campaign. Mr. Mandela urged campaign crowds to support the police, who had been the hated enforcers of apartheid. He went the last mile to bring the prickly Mangosuthu Buthelezi into the election.
The new Constitution, drafted mainly by lawyers of the A.N.C. and the governing National Party, contains assurances to whites and other minorities. It has a detailed bill of rights and a Constitutional Court to enforce it against the future government.
Perhaps most important, the negotiators agreed to conduct the first election on the basis of proportional representation. In a system like Britain's or ours, with legislators elected by districts, few whites would have won -- because only a few districts in South Africa have white majorities. P.R. will produce many more white members, because each party will have the same share of seats as it has of votes in the whole country.
The A.N.C. agreed to P.R. even though it will give the party fewer winners. As a study by the Center for Voting and Democracy in Washington put it, the leaders realized that the distortions caused by a district system "would be fundamentally destabilizing in the long run for both minority and majority interests."
The Constitution also provides that any party with 5 percent of the vote will have a seat in the cabinet. There will be a government of national unity for five years, with Mr. de Klerk as a likely vice president.
Those concrete measures made it easier for the National Party to give up its absolute hold on power. And on top of them, Mr. Mandela and his colleagues have given assurances to the bureaucracy and the army that their interests will be protected. Their pensions, too.
A.N.C. leaders have taken all these steps on the premise that a new government can succeed only if most South Africans accept it as legitimate. Mr. Mandela told a television interviewer: "To be dismissive of opposition -- that is what was done in Angola and Mozambique. We must not make that mistake."
Americans might look at South Africa and think about our politics. South Africans lined up for hours, determined to vote; half of us do not bother. There, victims of oppression built bridges to those who held power; here we increasingly have the politics of division and hate. They used proportional representation to mitigate conflict; we mocked a Lani Guinier who thought we should consider a form of P.R. for the same reason.
Of course the commitment to reconciliation and national unity will be tested by the realities of governing. South Africans who live in shacks hope to achieve at least their modest expectations of a water tap and a job, and those will not be easy for the Government to provide. But many people meant it when they said, as they waited to vote, that this was giving them what they wanted most of all: human dignity.

HEADLINE: As Competition Expands, Nursing Homes Diversify

BYLINE: By ESTHER B. FEIN 

 BODY:
From the corner outside his apartment on 51st Street in Borough Park, Brooklyn, Anthony Nardi can see patients from the Metropolitan Jewish Geriatric Center's nursing home one block away, sitting outside in their wheelchairs, their creased faces turned toward the warm noon sun.
That single-block distance pleases him immensely: he, too, is a patient of Metropolitan, having suffered two heart attacks and a stroke that left him weak beyond his 62 years -- but he is served by the staff in his home. 
 As a way of staying competitive with home care and the widening array of alternative services for the elderly, nursing homes in the New York area and around the country have begun to reinvent themselves, offering programs and services that reach beyond the traditional, residential institutions that so many people now say they want to avoid in favor of aging at home.
Some in the industry have compared the new nursing homes with shopping malls offering "boutique services" under one roof. Metropolitan, for example, offers adult day care and visiting nurses to people still living at home. It also has a respite program that allows people who need to go away on business or a vacation to leave an elderly relative temporarily in the nursing home's care.
The Glenn Hill Convalescent Center in Danbury, Conn., opened a housing complex for older people on the same grounds as the nursing home, offering amenities like housekeeping and meal service, while allowing them to live as independently as they like in spacious apartments designed to accommodate the needs of the elderly.
Pacific Homes in Woodland, Calif., has gone into what it calls the personal security business, in which older people in the community can pay to have the nursing home check up on them daily.
The Eddy, in Troy, N.Y., has evolved over the last decade from a 19-bed nursing home to a diversified organization providing a variety of services to some 14,000 mostly elderly clients.
For the frailest and the sickest among the elderly, and for younger people with debilitating injuries and illnesses, many nursing homes now also run subacute care units, admitting for long-term and temporary rehabilitation people who might otherwise have to remain in a hospital at greater expense.
"Our whole industry is on the verge of changing dramatically," said Craig Duncan, executive director of the Eddy. "We are moving away from an institutional base except for the frailest population, and that's because we have better-educated older consumers telling us what they want, and that is to stay out of a nursing home. If we want to maintain and gain a share of that market -- and let's face it, all of us are revenue-driven -- we had better respond."
Since these changes are fairly new, nursing home operators, both nonprofit and for-profit organizations, are still weighing which new services will prove most lucrative, said Donna L. Wagner, vice president for programs at the National Council on the Aging.
As with nursing home stays, reimbursement for these different services by Medicare, Medicaid and private insurers varies widely by state and by case. Much of the diversification by nursing homes is in areas that are not medical -- like adult day care, apartment complexes, transportation -- and are paid for out of pocket.
For someone requiring extensive care, health care experts say, putting together a home health care program either through a nursing home or other organization roughly equals, on the average, the cost of keeping someone in a traditional nursing institution, about $36,000 a year.
But the experts point out that people who can maintain their independence with support services usually heal faster and remain healthier than those forced unnecessarily into institutions, saving future health care dollars.
"Your home instead of a nursing home" is how Metropolitan's long-term home health care program was pitched to Mr. Nardi. But that also reflects the way Metropolitan and other nursing homes are responding to changing philosophies about aging that now promote independent living for people who in the past had been institutionalized.
Through Metropolitan, for example, Mr. Nardi has a home health aide who comes five days a week for seven hours each day, helping him to dress, bathe, prepare meals and go to doctors. Every two weeks he is visited by a nurse he calls a "master sergeant" who takes his blood pressure and other vital statistics.
He occasionally consults with a social worker about filing insurance forms and has blood tests done monthly in his apartment by a laboratory technician. And every four months a senior nurse comes by to re-evaluate his plan of care. Because of his age, disability and need, Mr. Nardi's costs are covered entirely by Medicare and Medicaid.
 
'I'm a Free Man Here'
 "There are days I can't go out of my house and days I can't get out of my bed," said Mr. Nardi, a chatty man who loves to talk about his days as a chef in Italian restaurants and to reel off sports trivia. "But I'm a free man here. I got my freedom all around. The home is still down the block."
There are about 1.7 million beds in more than 16,500 nursing homes nationwide: about 75 percent of them are for-profit concerns, 20 percent of them are nonprofit and 5 percent are operated by Federal, state and local governments.
Changes within the $70 billion nursing home industry will not lead to the extinction of those residential institutions, people in the industry and experts on aging say. With the United States Census Bureau predicting that the over-65 population will grow two and a half times over the next 40 years to more than 70 million people, and that the population of people over 85 will nearly triple to about 9 million, there will still be a demand for nursing homes.
 
A Range of Services
 But increasingly, people in the field say, for-profit and nonprofit nursing home operators have begun to compete not with one another but with organizations and establishments that have developed programs aimed at replacing the traditional homes. But unlike companies that provide specific services, like home care, the nursing homes hope to attract customers by providing a continuum of services that treat people, from the time they are most healthy to when they are most sick.
"They read the tea leaves correctly," said Daniel Thursz, president and chief executive officer of the National Council on the Aging. "There is still a place for nursing homes at the end of the continuum and for certain kinds of dementia. But nursing homes are facing the fact that they are only one of many alternatives and they will not be able to survive if they provide only that one service. What's going on is an important change that recognizes the desire of almost all people not to be institutionalized."
Glenn Crest, an apartment complex for older people in Danbury, Conn., was opened in December 1987 by the owners of the Glenn Hill Convalescent Center on the same grounds as the nursing home. Its owners recognized earlier than most in the industry that society was moving toward more community-based options for its increasingly aging population.
In a three-story building with 24 one-bedroom and 24 two-bedroom units -- each with a bay window, fully-equipped kitchen and bathroom, washer-dryer, living room and terrace -- Glenn Crest offers, among others services, a home-delivered meal daily, housekeeping, transportation to shopping and doctors' appointments and an emergency call system.
These services are included in the monthly rental fee, which is $1,860 for the one-bedroom apartment and $2,340 for the two-bedroom, and, like rent, is not reimbursed by insurance. Residents who get medical care in the nursing home pay extra for their treatment, which is reimbursed by either Medicare or private insurance.
Some residents have ailing spouses who live in the nursing home, and all it takes is a walk across the manicured lawns to visit. Inside the nursing home itself, Glenn Hill has a subacute care unit, respite care and a hospice.
"It made economic sense for us to expand this way," said James K. Malloy, Glenn Hill's administrator. "We've been in the business of providing services to the elderly since 1963. Reaching out to other segments of that population, meaning well people and very sick people, was logical."
 
Subacute Care
 One of the nontraditional services being most vigorously pursued by nursing homes is subacute care, which is comprehensive inpatient treatment for people recovering from illnesses like pneumonia, injuries like a broken hip or chronic diseases like arthritis that do not require the intensive level of diagnosis or surgery available at hospitals.
Usually, when nursing homes enter the subacute market, they broaden beyond geriatric clientele to include younger patients. Since they operate without the high overhead of hospitals, nursing home subacute care units typically charge 20 to 60 percent less than hospitals, according to studies by industry analysts and health maintenance organizations, which are increasingly sending patients from hospitals to such institutions.
A study last year by Dean Witter found that for appropriate patients, average daily charges in such units ranged from $300 to $550, while charges for the same treatment in acute care hospitals ran between $700 to $1,000. Health care analysts estimate that about 10 to 20 percent of general acute care hospital patients could be cared for in subacute care units at nursing homes.
Given the growth of managed medical care in the country and the increasing population of elderly people, industry analysts estimate that the subacute care units for nursing homes have the potential to grow into a $5 billion to $20 billion market in the next decade.
 
'Everyone Is Specializing'
 The field is so ripe that the American Health Care Association, which represents about 11,000 for-profit and nonprofit nursing homes, published a business guide this month to aid its members in setting up subacute care units.
"I don't know anybody in this business who just wants to keep being or buying up traditional nursing homes," said Richard F. Grosso Jr., administrator of the Lakeview Subacute Care Center in Wayne, N.J. "Everyone is specializing." His institution has changed over the last 14 years from a 120-bed traditional nursing home to a center with a variety of specialized subacute care wards, including ones for oncology and ventilators, and only 30 traditional beds.
"We shouldn't be linked to bricks and mortar," said Eli S. Feldman, chief executive officer of Metropolitan in Brooklyn. "We're here to provide the best services for the individual the way they want it, which is why we took an organization that was 100 percent institutional to where we are delivering services to 20,000 people in their own homes. We are changing as an industry. We have to evolve or we will die."

Martha and Harold Hansen have an apartment at the Glenn Hill Convalescent Center in Danbury, Conn. The center allows residents to live as independently as they like. HEADLINE: CURRENCY MARKETS;
U.S. Enters Currency Market To Prop Up Tumbling Dollar

BYLINE: By THOMAS L. FRIEDMAN, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, April 29

 BODY:
The Clinton Administration intervened in foreign exchange markets today to prop up a sagging dollar and faltering investor confidence that was already pushing interest rates higher at home.
The Federal Reserve, acting for the Administration, bought dollars and sold yen as the dollar tumbled to near its lowest point against the Japanese currency since World War II. It also sold marks for dollars as the American currency seemed to accelerate its slide against the German currency. The dollar stabilized quickly against the yen and the mark after the intervention. 
 Since Tuesday, the weakening dollar has helped send long-term interest rates soaring. The yield on the 30-year bond ended at 7.30 percent today, up from 7.10 percent on Tuesday. For the Administration, which is depending on low interest rates to fuel the economy, a sharp rise in long-term rates would undermine its goals.
Investor confidence is a fragile commodity, and if it is lost, financial markets can turn sour quickly. Some currency analysts said that the Administration's apparent willingness before today to let the dollar continue to drop against the yen had been fostering concern.
In addition, they said that the 4 percent slide of the dollar against the German mark in the last three weeks was a signal that some investors were losing confidence because they were selling dollars despite the attraction of better economic growth here than in Germany.
"The dollar had fallen into the crisis zone," said John P. Lipsky, chief economist at Salomon Brothers. "There was evidence of deteriorating investor confidence in the dollar, because some investors had concluded that the Administration was content to see the dollar weaken in order to pressure Japan in the trade negotiations."
In a highly unusual move that emphasized the importance of the effort to support the dollar, Treasury Secretary Lloyd Bentsen publicly announced the intervention by the Federal Reserve. "U.S. monetary authorities intervened today in foreign exchange markets to counter disorderly conditions," Mr. Bentsen said. "This is in line with our previously articulated policy that recognizes that excessive volatility is counterproductive to growth."
While it is not unprecedented for the Treasury to acknowledge interventions in foreign currency markets, its normal policy is not to discuss such moves.
Several economic analysts have argued for some time that the Administration's Japan trade policy, which was tacitly fostering a weaker dollar and a stronger yen, was working against its domestic fiscal and monetary policy, which aimed to hold down long-term interest rates to stimulate steady growth. The fact that the Administration had to step in today to bolster the dollar against the yen seemed to be an acknowledgment that it could no long pursue this contradictory approach.
The Federal Reserve started buying dollars this morning as the currency suddenly plunged against both the mark and yen. The dollar was on the verge of collapsing through the 100.35-yen level, the post-World War II low that was set last August. But the dollar also fell against the German mark to 1.64 marks from 1.66, a big move for one day -- especially when the fundamentals of the American economy appear to be sound.
By the end of trading in New York, the dollar was at 101.70 yen, up from 101.36 on Thursday. Against the mark the dollar was at 1.6540, up from its morning level but down from 1.6622 the day before.
 
Viewed as Dangerous
 American officials have always viewed such currency volatility as dangerous. Not only does it hamper trade -- since corporations, exporters and importers cannot know what value a currency will have from week to week -- but it plays havoc with interest rates. When the dollar weakens, it fuels inflation by making imports more expensive. More inflation, in turn, can force the Federal Reserve to raise short-term interest rates and traders to sell, pushing long-term interest rates up.
A falling dollar can also force the Treasury Department to raise interest rates on its notes and bonds to keep attracting foreign buyers. Such upward pressure on interest rates is exactly the opposite of what the White House wants right now, as rising rates would crimp economic growth.
While the intervention stabilized the dollar today, the Administration's resolve was likely to be tested in the weeks ahead as currency traders try to find out how far the Treasury and the Fed will go to defend the dollar.
Market analysts and traders cited a combination of factors that prompted the dollar's decline. One was the widespread expectation among speculators that the new Japanese Government would be too weak to resolve its trade dispute with Washington. That might mean that the Clinton team would rely on a strong yen for the time being to force Tokyo to open its markets. When the yen is strong against the dollar everything Japan sells is more expensive and Japanese exports are hurt.
As for the German mark, analysts pointed to the fact that recent strong growth data from Germany suggested that the Bundesbank, Germany's central bank, might not lower interest rates as quickly as expected. If German rates remain relatively high, investors will be tempted to keep more money in marks than in American dollars.
Many analysts believed that as long as Japan refused to negotiate on American terms an agreement to open some of its closed markets, the Clinton Administration would rely on a strengthening yen to squeeze Tokyo. The Administration's policy was viewed as "benign neglect" of the dollar at best and "malign neglect" at worst, Mr. Lipsky of Salomon Brothers said.
But what may have spurred today's further drop in the dollar against the yen was the formation of the new, weak, minority Cabinet in Tokyo, led by Tsutomu Hata. Such a fractured Japanese Cabinet seems unlikely to be able to adopt the tough, market-opening reforms sought by Washington. This seems to have convinced some currency speculators that Washington would rely on a strong yen.

HEADLINE: A Brown Seeks Her Own Political Identity

BYLINE: By RICHARD L. BERKE, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: LOS ANGELES

 BODY:
Kathleen Brown was in tears. In a few hours she would announce her candidacy for governor of California, and her brother, Jerry, a former governor himself, had told her that he would not join dozens of other family members at her speech in San Francisco.
Mr. Brown resented being trotted before the cameras when his father was Governor and was not about to play a prop again for his younger sister. "I've got my own ideas on how you do these things," he said. "Maybe a little of the schmaltz is all right. But that's not the way I would have done it." 
 But Kathleen Brown would not hear of it and prevailed on her often-aloof brother to show up for the February announcement. "I wanted him there and it was important to me," Ms. Brown recalled. "He's my brother. I said, 'Hey, this is family.' "
Family, in fact, is what Kathleen Brown's race for the Democratic nomination for governor is all about. Ms. Brown, who for the last three years has been the state treasurer, readily admits that she would not be running, let alone leading in the polls, if her name were not Brown, if her father, Edmund G. Brown, had not had the job, and if her brother, Edmund G. Brown Jr., had not followed in his footsteps.
While her last name has catapulted Ms. Brown this far, it is also her biggest liability. The Browns are perhaps the most absorbing -- some say peculiar -- political dynasty short of the Kennedys in the country. As she struggles to define herself and refute critics who say she has been a disappointing candidate, she finds herself caught between the competing impulses of family loyalty and political preservation.
These inherent conflicts were on display at the announcement in San Francisco. Her 89-year-old father, who is still beloved by Californians and adores Kathleen, his youngest daughter, is in poor health and was confined to his home. While Ms. Brown wanted her brother there with 45 other relatives to present a picture of family unity, her advisers were not nearly so eager. And when a reporter asked Ms. Brown whether she was keeping her distance from her brother, he bounded to the stage and, uncharacteristically, kissed his sister.
Yet, the embrace was misleading. Mr. Brown has refused to endorse his sister and has spoken highly of a dark-horse candidate, Tom Hayden, the anti-war leader who is now in the State Senate. (Kathleen Brown endorsed her brother when he ran for President in 1992.)
For those who missed the picture of the bad-boy iconoclast kissing his good-girl traditionalist sister, the seasoned publicity machine of Gov. Pete Wilson, a Republican, faxed copies to newspapers up and down the state. "Jerry Brown is the ghost in the attic, the crazy aunt locked in the basement," said Dan Schnur, the Wilson campaign's acid-tongued spokesman. "And he is going to be a constant reminder to the people of California of all the things they don't like about Kathleen Brown."
He may be regarded by many as a political oddball, but how could he not endorse his own sister?
Ms. Brown was asked the question over a decaffeinated cappuccino and a toasted bagel at a favorite breakfast spot of hers on Sunset Boulevard the other day. She stared down her interviewer in mock disbelief and then broke out laughing: "This is Jerry Brown we're talking about."
John Garamendi, the state's insurance commissioner and Ms. Brown's leading rival in the Democratic primary on June 7, is also making an issue of the Brown clan. He hardly needed to be more explicit when he declared at the recent state party convention that he was the candidate who "does not believe he is entitled to this job." His campaign manager, Darry Sragow, says Ms. Brown has a "coronation complex."
 
Help, Then Hindrance?
 The conventional wisdom is that the Brown legacy may help Ms. Brown draw votes in the primary but would be a liability in the general election when she needs to broaden her appeal. Saying he was simply staying out of politics, Jerry Brown denied in a telephone interview from Oakland, Calif., that he was trying to help his sister by not endorsing her. And he put her lead in the polls in stark, if not self-serving terms: "You know that her connection to me is the key reason that Garamendi can't move."
Since Ms. Brown's public life consists of only one term as treasurer and service on the Los Angeles school board more than a decade ago, it is no surprise that she is overshadowed by memories of her father and brother. But their vastly different approaches to government are obsolete in an era of fiscal turmoil, when California has lost its allure.
Pat Brown's legacy as Governor from 1959 to 1967 was a free-spending and fast-building California. He beat Richard M. Nixon in 1962 but lost to Ronald Reagan in 1966. Even those who do not remember the elder Mr. Brown recall Jerry Brown's campaigns for President in 1976, 1980 and 1992 and his stewardship of California from 1975 to 1983, with his futuristic notions about the environment and technology.
 
Combining 2 Styles
 In the interview, Ms. Brown, who got her law degree from Fordham University in 1984, gave a nod to the legacies of her better-known family members while trying to establish her own identity. After protesting the inevitable comparisons ("I am my own person," she said), Ms. Brown suggested that she was striving for a hybrid between the back-slapping appeal of her father and the more remote and more intellectually curious Jerry Brown.
"I am well aware of the great strengths my dad had and of his limitations," she said. "And I think in some ways my brother reacted to what he perceived as the shortcomings of my father's administration. In my dad's era, there was so much of 'all politics is local.' And when my brother came to the governorship he brought his extraordinary global vision and he ignored the local. He didn't want to build new highways.
"I feel that you can't do it one way or another."
Unlike her brother, who had a competitive relationship with his father, Ms. Brown is known as her father's daughter. She shares his easy way with people and seeks to benefit from the nostalgia for him, peppering her speeches with tales of growing up with Pat Brown. "Kathleen is Pat's favorite," said Bob Mulholland, a Democratic consultant in Sacramento who is close to the family. "This is a father who really loves his kids but really shines when Kathleen comes into the room."
In another contrast with her brother, who is single, Ms. Brown emphasizes that as a woman and a 48-year-old grandmother, she would bring a new perspective to the governorship.
For months, however, Ms. Brown, whose slogan when she ran for treasurer was "a different shade of Brown" has been widely perceived as beige -- too bland when contrasted with the passion of her father and brother. She was also attacked for being unable to advance a clear vision.
Even worse, she was labeled wishy-washy in shifting her positions, like her 11th-hour decision to oppose the North American Free Trade Agreement on the ground that it would cost the state too many jobs.
Yet, Ms. Brown has maintained a comfortable lead over the cash-strapped Mr. Garamendi and Mr. Hayden, and is viewed as the easy favorite for the nomination in part because her family name has allowed her to dwarf her opponents in contributions. She also benefits by drawing on the female majority that votes in the Democratic primary, and polls show her ahead of Mr. Wilson.
Still, Ms. Brown was worried enough to reshuffle her senior staff in March. She hired Clint Reilly, a bright consultant with a reputation as a hired gun, to run her campaign, and at a recent state party convention, Mr. Reilly unveiled what he said was a new Kathleen Brown.
In a well-delivered speech, Ms. Brown said her priority would be the economy, and she hammered away at a new anthem: "Jobs for a million people." But many political analysts said her new vision was hardly ambitious given economic trends showing that a million new jobs would be created in that period anyway.
 
'A Person on My Own'
 Ms. Brown was sure-footed at a news conference afterward, until the questioning went beyond the subjects at hand. She stumbled when asked about a controversy over whether to divert water to Los Angeles from Mono Lake in Northern California.
"She was hitting buttons on jobs," said Shirley Blumberg, a delegate from Mammoth. "But I didn't think it came from her gut. She hasn't managed yet to say, 'I am a person on my own. I am not under the direction of my father and mother.' "
Ms. Brown defends her early performance, saying she is confident that she is far more capable than Mr. Wilson but acknowledging that she has not always succeeded in articulating a clear message. "The problem has been distilling it into its essence," she said.
She allowed how it is not always easy being a Brown. "How many kids would hear their mother on '60 Minutes' asked, 'Who's smarter?' " she said with a laugh. For the record, Bernice Brown replied to Morley Safer on the program last year: "I think Jer---- Well, I---- Kathy is smart. Don't misunderstand. But Jerry's brilliant." Softening the blow, she called her daughter "a better politician."
Maybe that is why Ms. Brown does not turn to her brother for political advice. (Still, Ms. Brown insists that they are close enough that they spent Easter together.)
In keeping with smart politics, the Brown campaign has already has made several decisions to differentiate Ms. Brown from her 56-year-old brother. For example, advisers said Ms. Brown steered clear of supporting an insect spraying program for fear that it would remind voters of her brother's much-attacked sluggishness on the medfly controversy when he was Governor.
Jerry Brown does not seem worried about how his own legacy might affect his sister's aspirations. He is host of a radio program out of Oakland, Calif., and wants nothing to do with the brand of politics that propelled him to the governor's office and that Kathleen Brown is now practicing. "I did the hack politics," he said.
But John Buchanan of Los Angeles, a delegate at the state party convention, sympathizes with Kathleen Brown's familial tribulations. "What's she supposed to do, disown her brother?" he asked. "Of course not."

Brown Jr. kissed his sister Kathleen when she announced her candidacy for governor. (Associated Press)(pg. 1)

HEADLINE: On Return, Gay Native Son Is Killed, And a Hometown Is Looking Inward

BYLINE: By TAMAR LEWIN, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: SMITHTON, Pa.

 BODY:
Two years ago, Paul Edward Steckman gave up his Manhattan apartment and moved back to this tiny town on the Youghiogheny River to care for his ailing mother, leaving behind the career he had nurtured over two decades in New York playing the piano at restaurants, hotels and piano bars.
Mr. Steckman, then 44, moved into his mother's home, one block from the church where he used to play the organ -- and from the spot where he was beaten to death last month at 1:30 A.M. by a 17-year-old man who said that Mr. Steckman had made sexual advances. 
 The killing of a native son, a gay native son, has shaken Smithton, where everybody knows everybody, and most everybody has had something to do with coal mining, steel mills or the Jones Brewery, which sits on the edge of town, next to the railroad tracks and the river.
For some, Mr. Steckman's death was a chastening lesson in small-town intolerance. But it may also have been a tragic mismatch between a young man determined to prove his toughness and an older man who drank too much and, for Smithton, was shockingly open about his sexual interests.
 
Chilling Effect
 "It's just unheard of, a crime like this in a close-knit little town like Smithton, where people will do anything for each other," said Nancy Ullom, a longtime friend of Mr. Steckman's. "Paul was always a little different, even in high school, but everybody loved him for his sense of humor. I can't say I'm a gay-rights activist, but it never mattered to me what Paul was. He was just a wonderful friend. If there is anybody else in the area who's gay, they'd probably think twice now about expressing it."
When it comes to attitudes toward homosexuality, there is a vast gulf between Smithton and New York. About a third of Smithton's 300 or so citizens are over 65, many of them old friends of Mr. Steckman's mother, Opal, and they refuse to believe that Mr. Steckman was homosexual.
Harold Axel, a retired steelworker whose green-awninged house overlooks the spot on Second Street where Mr. Steckman was killed, said homosexuality simply did not exist here.
"Paul was just in the wrong place at the wrong time," he said. "Nobody can convince me that Paul made advances. If he was a homosexual, that would have been something that occurred in New York, not here. We ain't got none of them around here."
 
Trouble Was Coming
 But the younger men in Smithton, the ones who hang out at the Zoo, the rowdiest of the town's bars and the one Mr. Steckman went to on the night of his death, had a different view. "Everybody knew he was a homosexual," said Ron Johnston. "He would come into the Zoo and flirt with people. It was real obvious."
Down the street, at the Wood Shed, where fishermen on the Youghiogheny (pronounced yahk-uh-GAY-nee) buy maggots, red worms, wax worms, meal worms and night crawlers, or hang out around the pool table, some said it was inevitable that Mr. Steckman would find trouble.
"He would proposition people," said Eric Celapino. "Maybe in New York you could get away with that, but not here."
Mr. Steckman's New York friends, who knew him by his professional name, Paul Edwards, are still trying to understand his death in a place so near, yet so far, from New York. They are planning a memorial service for him on May 1 at the Bitter End, a Greenwich Village music club.
"Paul was killed so senselessly," said Pam Wilson, who knew him for 10 years and whose husband owns the Bitter End. "I feel like I've lost part of my soul. Paul was incredibly talented. But he was also a very vulnerable, nonviolent person."
 
Deadly Encounter
 The person charged with beating Mr. Steckman to death is Samuel Louis Sethman, a 6-foot-2-inch, 250-pound 11th grader.
According to testimony at his preliminary hearing on April 7, Mr. Sethman told state troopers that he had been drinking from a case of stolen beer when Mr. Steckman, "a known queer," had approached him on the street and asked him to go to a bar. Mr. Sethman said he had told Mr. Steckman, who was 5 feet 8 inches and 170 pounds, to leave him alone but that Mr. Steckman had followed him and pinched his buttocks. Mr. Sethman said he became enraged and began to strike Mr. Steckman.
Mr. Sethman, who is free on $100,000 bond, will be tried as an adult because of the seriousness of the charge. He will be arraigned in the Westmoreland County Court of Common Pleas in May. His lawyer, John DeBarardinis, has advised him not to speak to reporters.
Smithton people try to rationalize Mr. Sethman's actions. "I think he wanted to beat him up to be cool, to say, 'I beat up a faggot,' but then it went too far," said Michael Gluz. "I've heard people say Paul Steckman deserved to be beat up, but nobody deserves dying like that."
People in Smithton also talk a lot about Paul Steckman these days, trying to understand his killing, and his life. They remember that in high school Mr. Steckman played for the glee club and dated and became engaged to a local girl, who called off the wedding at the last moment.
They remember that Mr. Steckman was one class ahead of the father of Mr. Sethman, and they remember the string of misfortunes that trailed the young Sam Sethman: his abandonment by his mother, then his father's death and the death of the grandmother who was raising him, leaving him to live with an aunt.
People in Smithton also remember that a few weeks before Mr. Steckman's killing Mr. Sethman was walking around town with 50 stitches in his head, a bloody memento of a fight in the next town.
Behind the bar at the Longhorn Saloon, where Mr. Steckman used to buy fish sandwiches for his mother, Betty Nagy talked about Sam Sethman's reputation as the rough kid in town. "Whenever there would be new graffiti or something, everybody would say, 'Oh, that's Sammy again,' " said Mrs. Nagy who, like many Smithton residents, is intensely loyal to the Steckman family.
Mr. Steckman's New York friends said they had warned him that a return to small-town life might be too big a change from life on the Upper West Side. But Mr. Steckman was concerned about his mother's failing health, discouraged about his career, which had flagged during the recession, and he had no personal commitment to keep him in the city.
"He had had long-term relationships, but not since the mid-80's," said Brian Hamilton, a Los Angeles actor. "He'd lost a lot, a lot of friends to AIDS, and I think he sometimes wondered why they all died and he was left."
Liz Temkin, who said she would talk to Mr. Steckman four or five times a day, said he had seemed lonely. "He was very involved with his friends, and he had some relationships with men, but nothing that lasted long," she said. "That was a void in his life. He went to gay bars sometimes. But with AIDS and everything, I think he was very careful."
While Mr. Steckman's New York friends described him as comfortable with his sexual orientation, Mr. Furno thought otherwise.
"Coming back to Smithton was a regrouping time for him," said Mr. Furno. "He was struggling with being gay, whether it was acceptable to God, or acceptable to his mother, or even acceptable to himself."
 
Ill-Starred Return Home
 Looking back, he and others say, it was clear that Mr. Steckman did not take easily to life in Smithton. Twice he was picked up for driving while intoxicated. His habit of staying up late at night bothered his mother. He developed a relationship with a man in a nearby town, but it did not last. And when the other man suddenly broke it off, Mr. Steckman was so distraught that he made harassing phone calls to the man, who complained to law enforcement officials.
In Smithton, Mr. Steckman renewed friendships with three women he had known as a child, calling every day when one was treated for cancer and letting another stay with him for weeks while she planned her future. And he had pleasant relationships with his neighbors, many of whom admired his devotion to his mother.
"He was a very sociable person," said Nancy Bures, Mr. Steckman's sister, who lives in nearby Greensburg. "But of course I worried when he came home to live. I really didn't think it was a good idea."
Much of Mr. Steckman's life in Smithton was devoted to caring for his mother, who was in her 80's.
"He was still, in his 40's, working out his feelings for his parents," said Mr. Furno. "Caring for his mother, who was going blind and had emphysema, was not easy for him. But it gave him a purpose, and a free place to live. When Opal finally went into the nursing home, Paul visited every day and fed her."
 
Lofty Ambitions
 Mr. Steckman's friends say he was devastated when his mother died exactly four months before he was killed. But in the months between his mother's death and his own, he began to make a name for himself in Western Pennsylvania music circles, performing at private parties, at the Vista Hotel in Pittsburgh and at the Latrobe Country Club, where he played the night before he was killed.
"He really wanted to be a star, not someone who played in a restaurant, where most people don't stop talking enough to listen," said Ms. Temkin.
Mr. Steckman never intended to stay in Smithton: Soon after his mother's death, he began planning to move to Pittsburgh or New York, where there would be both a gay community and music jobs.
On the Saturday night he was killed, Ms. Bures said, her brother was to have drinks with a friend. But at 9:30 p.m. on March 27, the friend called to say she was too tired to go out. Mr. Steckman went out by himself. It was, apparently, on his way home that he ran into Mr. Sethman.
"He was not a person who would pick a fight," Mr. Bures said. "No one deserves to be beaten like that. I still can't believe it. Not in Smithton."

GRAPHIC: Photo: Paul Edward Steckman, a pianist in New York, was killed last month in his hometown of Smithton, Pa.

HEADLINE: BUSINESS DIGEST

 BODY:

Markets
 The Clinton Administration intervened in foreign exchange markets to prop up a sagging dollar and faltering investor confidence that was already pushing interest rates higher in the U.S. The Federal Reserve, acting for the Administration, bought dollars and sold yen as the dollar tumbled close to its lowest level against the Japanese currency since World War II. [Page 1.] 
 Stocks rose slightly in active trading after the Fed sought to support the dollar. The Dow rose 13.38 points, to 3,681.69. [40.]
Platinum prices soared amid signs that demand for the metal by the Big Three auto makers was rising. [49.]
Treasury prices fell, but ended off the day's lows after the Fed's intervention on the dollar. The 30-year bond fell 14/32 , to 8712/32 , to yield 7.30 percent. [50.]

The I.R.S. will stop reserving parking spaces in its executives' names in an effort to sidestep a new law that requires that taxes be paid on a part of the market value of workers' free parking. [1.]

HEADLINE: Many Doctors Don't Offer Their Workers Insurance

BYLINE: By ROBERT PEAR, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, April 29

 BODY:
One in three doctors' offices does not provide health insurance for employees, the American Medical Association reported today.
In this respect, it said, doctors are like many other small businesses: they would incur additional costs if they were required to provide coverage for employees, as President Clinton and many other Democrats have proposed. 
 Doctors tend to support the expansion of health insurance coverage, but "many medical practices are small businesses and are likely to be adversely affected by legislation that raises payroll expenses," the report said.
The A.M.A. focused on offices with 25 or fewer doctors, which accounted for 98 percent of all practices in the survey. It found that one-third of these offices did not provide health benefits to employees. But the researchers said that doctors' offices were more likely than other small businesses of similar size to offer health benefits.
 
Few Subsidies for Doctors
 Under Mr. Clinton's proposal, small businesses with low average wages would be eligible for Federal subsidies to help defray the costs of health insurance. But doctors have relatively high incomes, and their earnings raise the average for a doctor's office, so that few offices would qualify for subsidies, the association said.
The proposed employer mandate is at the heart of President Clinton's health plan. Mr. Clinton would require employers to buy health insurance for their workers. Many small businesses oppose this requirement, saying they cannot afford the extra expense.
But some big businesses that already provide health insurance coverage contend that all employers should be required to do so.
The Safeway grocery chain said today that it would put a message endorsing universal coverage on paper bags used at one of its stores in Washington. The message says, "Safeway supports affordable health care for all Americans."
Hillary Rodham Clinton plans to visit the store next week to show appreciation for the company's position. Brian G. Dowling, a Safeway spokesman, said the company might put a similar message on paper bags at other stores after testing reaction at the supermarket in Northwest Washington.
Safeway has 1,078 stores and 105,000 employees. Mr. Dowling said that at least 90 percent of the workers received health insurance through the company.
Mr. Dowling said Safeway was not specifically supporting Mr. Clinton's health plan. "We support health care reform," Mr. Dowling said. "We have not endorsed any particular plan. However, we believe in universal coverage. An employment-based system is the most realistic and practical approach to achieve universal coverage."
The announcement by Safeway came as Clinton Administration officials tried to generate fresh support for the idea of an employer mandate. Labor Secretary Robert B. Reich and Robert E. Rubin, assistant to the President for economic policy, said today that they felt sure employers would eventually support the proposal.
"After the posturing and negotiation, businesses will support our position," Mr. Rubin said at a briefing for journalists. "As we get closer to the end of the legislative process, we will get substantial support from businesses."
He and Mr. Reich refused to comment on Congressional proposals that would exempt companies with fewer than 10 employees from any requirement to buy or provide health insurance.
The American Medical Association found that the likelihood of insurance coverage tended to increase with the size of doctors' offices. Fifty-eight percent of solo practitioners offer health insurance to their employees, the survey found. Seventy-four percent of offices with two to four doctors offer coverage, and 85 percent of offices with five to nine doctors offer such benefits, it said. Slightly more than half the doctors surveyed were solo practitioners.
 
A Broader Recommendation
 For several years the medical association asserted that all employers should be required to provide health benefits to employees. But at a meeting in New Orleans in December, the policy-making arm of the A.M.A., the house of delegates, voted to recommend other approaches as well.
The alternatives include tax-free medical savings accounts and an "individual mandate," requiring people to obtain insurance with or without help from employers.
Researchers at the American Medical Association said that some doctors provided free health care to their nurses, receptionists, laboratory technicians and other office employees, regardless of whether the employees had insurance.
The association found that specialists with the highest earnings, including surgeons, anesthesiologists and radiologists, were more likely to offer health benefits to their employees. These specialists must offer such benefits to attract highly trained personnel, the report said.

HEADLINE: Warning To China On Trade

BYLINE: By DOUGLAS JEHL 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, April 29

 BODY:
President Clinton has decided to designate China as one of the world's biggest pirates of American products, a step that could lead to new trade sanctions against Beijing by the end of the year, Administration officials said today.
The officials said Mr. Clinton reached that decision after his top trade and economic advisers recommended unanimously this afternoon that he take steps to punish China for its pirating of American compact discs and computer software. 
 The decision is scheduled to be announced on Saturday by the office of the United States trade representative, the officials said. Under law, Mr. Clinton must submit to Congress each year a list of the worst violators of American copyrights and other intellectual property rights. This year, the deadline falls on Saturday night.
 
Deadline on Trade Status
 With a separate June 3 deadline looming for his decision on whether to withdraw China's preferential trade status, the President's decision is certain to send to Beijing a strong message of American unwillingness to compromise its trade standards. But by not making the announcement himself, Mr. Clinton will likely avoid casting himself as an ogre in the sensitive negotiations that American officials still hope will produce a breakthrough allowing the Administration to extend China's most-favored-nation status.
In deciding to designate Beijing as a "Priority Foreign Country" under the 1988 trade measure known as Super 301, Mr. Clinton is singling out perhaps the most notorious offender of laws intended to stop other countries from making illegal copies of American recordings, software and books and profiting from their sales without paying royalties to the artists, authors and designers who created them.
A recent estimate by the trade representative's office concluded that China's failure to crack down on pirate companies cost American entertainment countries some $800 million in lost exports last year.
 
Officials Express Concer
 Some Administration officials had expressed concern that adding China to the list might merely reinforce Chinese intransigence, causing Beijing to refuse to take the steps on human rights that the United States has said it must take to maintain its preferential trade status. Because China has emerged as a big trading partner, American officials are deeply wary of having that relationship disrupted.
But Mr. Clinton's advisers told him during a telephone conference this afternoon that China's violations of American intellectual property rights were so egregious that to overlook them, even this year, would undermine the credibility of American trade law.
At the same time, recent progress by China toward meeting the human rights standards imposed by the United States has led some of Mr. Clinton's advisers to argue that tough action by the Administration now would not necessarily lead to a fatal rupture.
The designation of China as a priority offender under the Super 301 law brings no immediate penalties against Beijing, meaning that any showdown over pirating will be delayed until after the June 3 deadline on China's most-favored-nation status. But if China does not agree to a solution after negotiations lasting seven to nine months, the United States would be required under law to impose restrictions on selected Chinese exports.
The United States last added China to the pirating list in 1991, and the threat of retaliation then forced the Chinese to adopt modern copyright, patent and trademark laws. But Congressional critics led by Senator Max Baucus, the Montana Democrat, have contended that China has failed to enforce its new restrictions.
According to Senator Baucus, who is chairman of the Senate subcommittee on international trade, at least 26 pirate compact disk factories are operating in China with a production capacity of 50 million CD's a year. In addition to causing American recording artists to lose more than $820 million last year in revenue, the Chinese factories are also beginning to compete with American subidiaries in other Asian markets.
"The situation is intolerable," Senator Baucus said recently. "If a country as big as China can go into full-scale piracy, our efforts in all other countries, no matter how successful, will become meaningless."
