HEADLINE: Sales of New Homes Jumped by 11.1% in March

BYLINE: By ROBERT D. HERSHEY Jr., Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, April 29

 BODY:
Encouraged by a buoyant economy and a snapback from poor weather, sales of new homes climbed 11.1 percent in March, to an annual pace of 739,000, the Government reported today.
The unexpectedly brisk sales, the highest since September, pushed inventories of unsold homes back under five months' worth, and suggested that the industry's activity would continue at a high level for some time, despite higher mortgage rates. 
 "Job prospects, income prospects have historically been more important determinants than interest rates" for housing, said David W. Berson, chief economist for the Federal National Mortgage Association.
The Commerce Department also reported today that once again, growth in personal incomes somewhat exceeded growth in personal spending during March. Incomes rose $33.1 billion, or six-tenths of 1 percent, while spending rose $18.9 billion, or four-tenths of 1 percent.
But while housing analysts welcomed the sales results, which nearly equaled the surge of 12.1 percent in new dwellings on which construction started in March, they cautioned that the current pace would probably ease a bit as the year unfolded.
"Can you justify continued sales and production at the very elevated levels" that prevailed in late 1993, Edward F. McKelvey, a senior economist at Goldman, Sachs, asked rhetorically. "My answer is no. Housing has probably seen the peak of its activity for a while."
The market has already benefited, he and other specialists said, from the previously indecisive buyers who were driven to action when mortgage rates reversed course and began to rise last autumn.
Sales rose last month in all regions but the Midwest, though the gains were uneven. In the Northeast, sales climbed 38 percent, to a pace of 69,000 houses a year on a seasonally adjusted basis. The South reported an advance of 19.6 percent, to 329,000, while sales in the West edged up 1.4 percent, to 215,000.
In the Midwest, sales edged down by eight-tenths of 1 percent, to 126,000, according to the report, published jointly by the Departments of Commerce and of Housing and Urban Development.
The stock of unsold houses would last 4.9 months at the current sales pace, down from 5.4 months in February, and matching the level for October and November, when sales were comparably strong.
The actual number of new houses sold last month -- that is, without annualization or adjustment for recurring seasonal variation -- was 75,000, or 25 percent more than the total in March 1993.
The average price of a new home sold last month rose by $1,000, to $154,500, while the median price -- half the houses cost more, half less -- edged up $100, to $130,000.
Sales of new homes generally run only at about half the pace of housing starts, which were 1,473,000 last month, because the sales figures do not include dwellings built on buyers' own lots or units in apartment houses.
Sales of existing homes were reported earlier this week to have rebounded 5.7 percent in March, to a pace of 4.06 million.
The income and spending report was generally as expected, analysts said, and included upward revisions in both elements for February, and downward revisions for January.
Income from all sources ran at an annual rate of $5.63 trillion, up six-tenths of 1 percent, with February's gain now put at 1.8 percent, the biggest since December 1992, instead of the 1.3 percent that had been initially reported.
The exceptionally big rise in February mainly reflected a rebound from uninsured losses to homes and business property from the California earthquake, subsidy payments to farmers, and bonuses to workers in the automobile industry. Without these special factors, the Government said, the February increase would have been only four-tenths of 1 percent.
The effects of the earthquake showed up mainly in losses of rental income to property owners.
The higher spending last month was concentrated in cars and other big-ticket durable goods, which rose at a pace of $10 billion, in the face of higher interest rates. Spending in the far larger services category rose $8.6 billion, while spending on nondurable goods was mostly unchanged.

GRAPHIC: Graphs: "Personal Income" shows total personal income from Oct. 1992 to March 1994. (Source: Commerce Department); "New-Home Sales" shows new private homes sold during month from Oct. 1992 to March 1994. (Source: Commerce Department); "Prices of New Homes" shows median prices for new homes from Oct. 1992 to March 1994. (Source: Commerce Department)

HEADLINE: Newspaper Guild Is Last of 12 Unions at The Times to Approve a Contract

BYLINE: By WILLIAM GLABERSON 

 BODY:
Members of the New York Times unit of the Newspaper Guild of New York decisively approved a new contract with the newspaper yesterday that is to run through the year 2000, guild officials said last night.
With the ratification, The Times has secured contracts with all of its 12 unions through the end of the century. All of the newspaper's union contracts now contain no-strike clauses.
The guild represents about 1,600 news, advertising, clerical, cafeteria and other employees. When voting was completed last night, officials said 577 members had voted yes and 86 had voted no, with 3 abstentions. 
 The union said the wage increases it won were comparable to those won by The Times's other unions and amounted to 3.5 percent, 3.2 percent and 3.1 percent over three years, beginning retroactively in 1993. Wages for the period after 1996 are subject to renegotiation.
In a joint statement after the union vote, Barry F. Lipton, the guild president, and John M. O'Brien, The Times's executive vice president and deputy general manager, expressed pleasure at the agreement.
"Our members clearly saw this as a very good settlement," Mr. Lipton said, "particularly given the tough economic conditions that prevail in our industry."
Mr. O'Brien said: "By working together, we will have the best opportunity to build a strong and secure future for our newspaper."
The Times won the right to reclassify some categories of work other than journalists, resulting in lower pay levels for some new employees. A spokesman for the newspaper, William K. Adler, said the pay of current employees will not be affected by the reclassifications. Mr. Adler also said the newspaper could not estimate how many new employees would be hired at the lower wages.
Union leaders told their members at meetings yesterday that they had defeated broad management efforts to weaken existing job-security protections. But in one major area, the union made significant concessions. Under the prior contract, employees with 10 years of seniority at the newspaper were given "enhanced job security" protections and could be laid off only for cause in most cases.
That protection will apparently now be given only to current employees when they reach 16 years of service. It appears that new employees will qualify for that protection when they have worked at The Times for 20 years.
A second major job-security issue involved the extent to which seniority, a traditional union protection, would continue to protect union employees in the event that The Times lays off employees. New York newspaper executives have complained for years that seniority rules have hampered their ability to manage their newsrooms.
The issue is particularly sensitive because The Times has said it is beginning an effort to reduce its white-collar workforce and has not ruled out layoffs. Yesterday The Times spokesman, Mr. Adler, said the new contract reflected management's "desire" to achieve staff reductions through buyouts and other voluntary means. But Mr. Adler said, "it is not possible to rule out layoffs under any circumstances."
The prior contract assured employees that any layoffs would begin on a last-hired, first-fired basis. But it provided an exception that said employees of "outstanding ability" or those involved in special functions could have their jobs preserved.
In a concession by the union, The Times has now broadened that exception and the newspaper's management can dismiss more senior employees "if The Times determines" that more junior employees have superior qualifications. The Times's determination can be overturned in arbitration only if "clearly erroneous."

HEADLINE: Consumers' Optimism Up

BYLINE: By Bloomberg Business News 

 BODY:
The University of Michigan's consumer sentiment index for April rose to 92.6 from 91.5 in March, according to people with access to the survey.
The preliminary reading for April issued two weeks ago was 94.2. In February, the Michigan index reached 93.2. 
 The index is based on 100, set in 1966. A monthly reading rising closer to 100 indicates that consumers are becoming more optimistic about the economy. Since 1966, the peak for the sentiment index has been 101, set in March 1984. The low was 51.7, set in April 1980.
The university also reported that its April index of consumer expectations had fallen to 82.6 from 85.1 in March. The early reading was 84.8.
The April index of current economic conditions rose to 108.1 from 101.4 in March. The early reading was 108.9.

HEADLINE: U.S. Changes Aim of S.& L. Enforcement

BYLINE: By KEITH BRADSHER, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, April 29

 BODY:
Federal savings and loan regulators announced today a new enforcement policy that focuses on active institutions, rather than failed ones, ending another chapter in the effort to go after wrongdoers from the nation's costliest financial crisis.
The Office of Thrift Supervision, which is part of the Treasury Department, said the change in enforcement priorities stemmed from the improved financial condition of the industry. 
 The pace of institutional closings by the office has dropped sharply. Last year there were only 299.
Some of the biggest and most publicized civil actions were brought by the office after the collapse of hundreds of savings and loans in the 1980's. Those cases involved seeking hundreds of millions of dollars in restitution from some of the nation's leading law and accounting firms.
 
Discussed for More Than a Year
 Carolyn Lieberman, the acting chief counsel of the thrift office, said the shift in enforcement priorities had been discussed within the agency for more than a year and was a result of several factors: the closing of fewer institutions, fewer requests from officials in the field for enforcement actions, and the ability of other agencies, like the Resolution Trust Corporation, to get monetary relief in cases that the Office of Thrift Supervision chose not to pursue.
The office regulates the industry while the Resolution Trust Corporation disposes of the portfolios of failed institutions.
Savings and loan industry officials said they were delighted by the change in enforcement policy. They said they were particularly happy with the Office of Thrift Supervision's decision to give priority to forcing dishonest executives to pay restitution to existing institutions, rather than pursuing dishonest executives of failed institutions and forcing them to repay the Federal Government for the losses borne by taxpayers.
"We really think it's a logical focus of their limited resources in preserving the health of the industry," said Robert P. Schmermund, a spokesman for the Savings and Community Bankers of America, an industry group based here.

HEADLINE: FUTURES MARKETS;
Platinum Soars Amid Signs Of Demand by Auto Makers

BYLINE: By The Associated Press 

 BODY:
Platinum prices soared yesterday amid signs that demand for the metal by the Big Three auto makers is rising during a period of instability in South Africa, the world's top platinum producer.
The active July platinum futures contract jumped $7.70, to $402.30 an ounce, on the New York Mercantile Exchange. 
 Other precious metals also rose. On the Commodity Exchange in New York, June gold gained $1, to $377.80 an ounce, while May silver rose 9.8 cents, to $5.338 an ounce.
Platinum prices have been seesawing in recent days over worries that the first all-race elections in South Africa would be accompanied by widespread violence. But even after no large outbreaks materialized, investors were worried yesterday about the policies of a new government, which is expected to have Nelson Mandela, leader of the African National Congress, at its head.
"Everybody's beginning to wonder what is going to happen," said Nina Lipton, research manager with the Platinum Guild International in New York. "The cost of production is likely to go up because the Government will have to create more taxation for the social policy programs they've outlined."
 
Used in Catalytic Converters
 Most of the world's supply of platinum comes from South Africa and Russia, both politically and socially unstable.
Prices also climbed amid signs of rising sales by the Big Three auto makers, which use platinum in making catalytic converters.
The Ford Motor Company reported a 58 percent gain in first-quarter earnings yesterday, following strong reports from Ford, Chrysler and General Motors indicating that despite rising interest rates the auto makers may have their best year since 1989.
"It appears the auto sector is booming, and the trend is for better growth overseas, with a booming business returning to Europe" after years of recession, Ms. Lipton said.
On other commodity markets, lumber prices rose their daily trading limit in nearby contracts in a continued technical correction after weeks of losses. Lumber prices have plunged amid slack demand and oversupply. May lumber rose $15, to $332.50 for 1,000 board feet, on the Chicago Mercantile Exchange.
Coffee prices continued to move higher on the Coffee, Sugar and Cocoa Exchange in New York after news that Brazil did not plan to release millions of pounds of coffee for sale at one time. Investors had feared such a development would flood the market and send cash prices plunging. May coffee rose 1.10 cents, to 89.15 cents a pound.
Energy prices advanced on the New York Mercantile Exchange on word of gasoline refinery shutdowns. June crude oil rose 33 cents, to $16.90 a barrel; May unleaded gasoline rose 0.61 cent, to 49.20 cents a gallon, and May heating oil rose 0.53 cent, to 47.05 cents a gallon.

HEADLINE: COMPANY NEWS;
Antar Gets 12 1/2 Years For Fraud

BYLINE: AP 

DATELINE: NEWARK, April 29

 BODY:
Eddie Antar, the founder of the Crazy Eddie electronics chain, was sentenced today to 12 1/2 years in prison for racketeering and stock fraud, more than four years after his company collapsed and he fled the country.
Mr. Antar, who was convicted in July, was also ordered to repay $121 million to stockholders who were victimized in what authorities described as one of the country's largest stock frauds. 
 Mr. Antar, legs shackled and wearing blue prison garb, apologized for the "pain and agony I caused my family."
"I don't ask for mercy," he said, "I ask for balance." Mr. Antar, 46, has been in jail since he was arrested in Israel in June 1992. He was extradited to the United States in January 1993.
 
Prosecutor's Comparison
 United States Attorney Michael Chertoff, who prosecuted the case, called Mr. Antar the "Darth Vader of capitalism."
Mr. Antar's brother, Mitchell, 38, was sentenced to 4 1/4 years in prison and ordered to pay $3 million in restitution, the amount the Government contended he made through sales of falsely inflated stock. Neither brother was fined.
A third brother, Allen, 44, was acquitted. Three former executives of Crazy Eddie who pleaded guilty are to be sentenced next month.
Eddie Antar said he was confident that he and Mitchell would be exonerated on appeal.
The brothers were accused of receiving more than $80 million by selling stock that they artificially increased in value through inventory inflation and other means.
In February 1990, the Securities and Exchange Commission won a $73 million judgment against them, which with interest is now $90 million. The agency has since identified more than $72 million that Eddie Antar accumulated, and it has collected more than $62 million. The money collected is to be disbursed to the victims in the case.
At its peak, Crazy Eddie had 43 stores and reported annual sales of $350 million, although tens of millions of dollars of that figure were in false sales.

HEADLINE: Prosecution Claims Records Prove Rifkin Is Not Insane

BYLINE: By JOHN T. McQUISTON, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: MINEOLA, L.I., April 29

 BODY:
Joel Rifkin, who has confessed to killing 17 women, was an honor student in high school, made the dean's list in college and scored in the superior category on intelligence tests, according to evidence presented today by the prosecution in his murder trial.
Fred Klein, the prosecutor, also produced reports by a Nassau County jail psychiatrist indicating that since Mr. Rifkin's arrest on June 28 in the slaying of Tiffany Bresciani, 22, of Manhattan, he has not been psychotic, appeared able to discern his situation without emotion and has occupied himself with his legal defense. 
 Mr. Klein was trying to bolster his argument that Mr. Rifkin is not insane, as his lawyer claims, but is "an intelligent, cunning man who is very capable of telling right from wrong."
The only witness for the defense, Dr. Barbara R. Kirwin, a clinical psychologist and expert on the criminally insane, was called back to the witness stand today by Mr. Klein to defend her testimony on Thursday that Mr. Rifkin was so mentally ill that he could not separate fantasy from reality when he killed Ms. Bresciani and his 16 other victims.
 
Straight 'A' Student
 Mr. Klein produced Mr. Rifkin's academic records from East Meadow High School and the State University of New York at Farmingdale, which Mr. Rifkin attended for two semesters before dropping out. He said Mr. Rifkin got straight A's in 1987, despite his father's death on Feb. 28, 1987. Dr. Kirwin had testified on Thursday that Mr. Rifkin had gone insane after his father's death.
Mr. Rifkin, a 34-year-old unemployed landscaper who says he killed his first victim in March 1989, dismembered her body and scattered its parts in New Jersey, sat slumped in his chair at the defense table, his chin resting in his hands.
The jury must decide whether he is guilty of murdering Ms. Bresciani, whose decomposing body was found in the back of his pickup truck by state troopers who stopped him for a traffic violation, or not guilty by reason of insanity.
If found guilty, he faces 25 years to life in prison. If found legally insane, he will be remanded to a state hospital where he would probably remain for the rest of his life.
Mr. Rifkin also faces murder charges in Suffolk County, Brooklyn, Queens and the Bronx, where he dumped the bodies of many of his victims along rivers, canals, dirt roads and empty fields.
The trial is to resume on Monday when Mr. Klein calls Dr. Elliot Park Dietz, an expert on serial killers who appeared for the prosecution at the trials of Jeffrey Dahmer, who drugged and killed 15 young men in Milwaukee, and Arthur Shawcross, who murdered 11 women in Rochester.
Dr. Dietz, like Dr. Kirwin, rarely testifies for the defense in criminal cases. In fact, Dr. Kirwin has appeared four times in Nassau County Court as a prosecution witness in murder trials.
Mr. Klein mentioned all four cases to the jury today, implying that Dr. Kirwin, as a clinical psychologist, was not fully qualified to be an expert witness for the defense in a criminal insanity case because she was not a forensic psychologist.
 
Credentials Questioned
 "Didn't you once testify that clinical psychology was primarily for the treatment of the mentally ill, and that it has to do with treatment in a clinical setting, not a courtroom," he said.
"I don't recall," said Dr. Kirwin. "I would have to see a transcript of my testimony."
After producing the transcript and reading it to the jury, Mr. Klein continued to question Dr. Kirwin's credentials and methods of testing and analyzing Mr. Rifkin.
Mr. Klein asked her if she had studied the reports on Mr. Rifkin prepared by Dr. Allen Reichmann of the Nassau County Department of Mental Health, who interviewed Mr. Rifkin two days after his arrest last summer and on several occasions since.

HEADLINE: Mr. Clinton's Risk in Macedonia

 BODY:
Greece is slowly strangling independent Macedonia's economy with a dangerously misguided blockade. The collapsing economy, in turn, is generating explosive friction among Macedonia's diverse peoples, which could endanger the lives of 500 American peacekeepers dispatched to Macedonia to contain ethnic strife.
The risk should have moved President Clinton to press Greece to lift the blockade when he met with Prime Minister Andreas Papandreou last week. Instead he reportedly bowed to the Greek-American lobby, well placed in Congress and the White House, and soft-pedaled his opposition to Athens's perverse policy. 
 Macedonia is where Belgrade's ambitions to incorporate the republic into Greater Serbia run smack into competing territorial claims. Albania and Bulgaria historically have sought to absorb much of the same territory, and Greek nationalists also have their eyes on that prize. The Greek Government fears that the intense rivalries could endanger Greece's hold on its own part of Macedonia. But Athens is letting its hysteria over history rob it of sound judgment.
During much of this century, Macedonia has been divided between northern Greece and Yugoslavia. In the late 1940's, Yugoslavia, allied with Greece's Communists, tried and failed to grab all of Greek Macedonia. In 1991, when Yugoslavia fell apart, the portion of Macedonia in Yugoslavia declared its independence.
Greeks fear that the new republic may soon break up into conflicting ethnic groups, with links to Albania, Bulgaria and Serbia, and that the trouble could spill over into Greek Macedonia. Although Prime Minister Papandreou says he favors the survival of an independent Macedonia, Greece's stubborn stance on recognition and its economic embargo are fast making that impossible.
Playing to impassioned nationalists at home, the Greek Government objected to the new republic's use of the name Macedonia, as well as its flag and Constitution. Greece first tried to block recognition of the republic. When Greece's European Union partners granted recognition, Athens imposed a blockade. That not only makes landlocked Macedonia more dependent on trade with Serbia; it is also destabilizing the country as economic privation intensifies ethnic friction.
Mr. Clinton sent American troops as monitors to Macedonia in hopes of heading off ethnic conflict. They could get caught in the middle if the blockade pushes Macedonia into civil war. Mr. Clinton is pandering to the Greek lobby at his own peril.

HEADLINE: COMPANY REPORTS;
Ford Posts $904 Million Profit, Highest Since 1989

BYLINE: By DORON P. LEVIN, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: DEARBORN, Mich., April 29

 BODY:
Rounding out a spate of positive financial news from the nation's auto industry, the Ford Motor Company today reported its best quarterly results since 1989, posting a first-quarter profit of $904 million, or $1.66 a share. That sum, up 58 percent from a year earlier, reflected an extraordinary charge against earnings of $440 million from the sale of First Nationwide Bank, a savings and loan.
A year ago, Ford reported a first-quarter profit of $572 million, or $1.02 a share. On Thursday, the General Motors Corporation posted a first-quarter profit of $853.7 million, or 81 cents a share, a 66 percent gain from a year earlier. Last week, the Chrysler Corporation reported a record quarterly profit of $938 million, up 77 percent from a year earlier.
 
Big Gains in U.S.
 Like the gains at Chrysler and G.M., Ford's improved results stemmed largely from higher United States automotive sales. Ford also gained from a better performance overseas as well as from its financial services group, absent the First Nationwide write-off. On April 14, Ford agreed to sell First Nationwide to First Madison Bank for $1.1 billion. 
 Net income from worldwide automotive operations was $955 million, compared with $176 million a year earlier. Automotive operations in the United States accounted for $835 million of the profit, compared with $113 million a year earlier. Excluding the write-off, financial services earned $389 million, down from $396 million. Ford's total revenue for the quarter was $30.4 billion, up 13.6 percent from $26.8 billion a year ago.
 Ford said the latest good financial news should be just the beginning, since automotive sales are expected to grow even stronger. "If you think of this in baseball terminology, the recovery is in the first inning in Europe and the third or fourth inning in the United States," said David N. McCammon, vice president-finance and treasurer.
 Mr. McCammon said Ford expected sales of cars and light trucks in the United States to surpass the previous record of 16.3 million vehicles, putting Ford's forecast in line with those of many economists who believe sales will not peak until late 1995 or 1996. Despite positive earning news, investor concerns about the duration and robustness of the upswing in automotive sales have knocked Big Three stocks off their recent highs. Ford shares closed at $58.375 a share yesterday, up 87.5 cents, in active trading on the New York Stock Exchange.
 Harvey Heinbach, automotive stock analyst for Merrill Lynch & Company, said his firm expected sales of 16.8 million vehicles in 1996. "If we're right, these stocks will bounce back," he said. One risk, however, is nervous institutional investors who do not want to be caught giving back gains won over the last two years and are, therefore, inclined to sell the stocks. "The last thing they want," Mr. Heinbach said, "is a round trip."
 David Healy, auto analyst for S. G. Warburg & Company, called Ford's report "a clean, good quarter." But, he said, shares of Ford and other auto companies "will have trouble as long as interest rates are going up."

 
8% Rise in Output
 Mr. McCammon said supplies of Ford's most popular models -- the Explorer sport utility vehicle, Mustang sport coupe and Windstar mini-van -- were tight, but "the rest of the stocks are in good shape." The auto maker is scheduling an 8 percent increase in North American production in the second quarter, which means more overtime, faster assembly line speeds and full production at Ford's Oakville, Ontario, assembly plant, where production of the Windstar van is replacing compact-car output.
 In the first quarter, Ford factories in North America ran at 110 percent of straight-time capacity; absolute maximum capacity is about 125 percent of straight time, or two eight-hour shifts a day.
 Prices of cars and trucks have firmed, as evidenced by the smaller rebates that Ford paid to stimulate retail sales. The average rebate was $565 in the quarter, down from $720 a year earlier.
 Ford estimated that its pretax profit on vehicles sold in the United States was $1,400 a unit, compared with about $2,000 for Chrysler and $800 for G.M. Mr. McCammon said the main reason for Chrysler's superior profitability was its model mix, which is skewed toward light trucks: mini-vans, sport utilities and pickups. Accordingly, Ford is raising the percentage of its production that consists of light trucks to 52 percent, from less than half.
